UNDER THE GUMTREE
A Literary Mixture.
PE-jlHE Diet of Australian
| School Children / Geo.
...M . Robertson ; Sydney) is Dr.
Philip MuskettV sixth
book in less than six years; and
.it is time the well-intentioned
doctor rested. A book-child every
year will break down the
strongest literary constitution;
and Dr. Muskett’s was moder-
ately weak to commence with.
Most .of the message he. has to
deliver was given in “ The Art of
Living in Australia ” —the rest
has been chiefly recipes, reiter-
ation, and padding. Generally
speaking, it may be held that
Dr. Muskett has done a great
deal of good. He has denounced
undue meat-eating; praised light
wines as compared with heavy
beers; and compiled in readable form, with
especial reference to Australian conditions, a mul-
titude of useful health hints. What he should do
now is to condense his books for adults into a
single volume, instead of annually repeating him-
self. The preface to his “Book of Diet” closes
with an apology for haste and errors; and “The
Diet of School Children,” which has no apology,
needs one badly. Apparently, when the author
reached the end of his story at p. 140 he found his
book too short; for he has added twenty pages
which sayjmrocisely what he has said before, even
twice before —often in the same language. For
example—
Page xix. Page 138. Page 153.
Opr Australian Our present sj’s- .. . Our Aus-
system of educa- tem of education tralian system of
tion is a form of is a form of spe- education fails in
specialism of the cialism of the that it does not
worst kind. We Worst kind. We equally develop
are severely edu- are severely edu- the brain . . . in
eating one small eating one small order to ensure
locality in the' locality in the the equality of all
| brain—at the ex- brain—at the ex- the various parts

I rest of the organ, rest of the organ, hands must be
The aim of tech- The aim of teehni- employed arid
nical education is cal education is to trained as well as
to train the hand train the hand as the mind. But
as well as the well as the head, hand training is

brain skill. quality.
This is the worst example of Dr. Muskett’s gri • ous
lack of method, but it is typical of his ackl
On p. 136 he repeats in identical language a >as-
sage on p. xviii. All through the book he iv eats
himself in different language. His 1.40 pp. book
and 23 prefatory pages could easily be boiled down
into 50 much more forcible pages, without omit-
ting one vital phrase or idea. It is the most slip-
shod piece of bookmaking seen locally since—
since Dr. Muskett’s previous book.
Nor, though the form be bad, is the matter
always good. Dr. Muskett has the same nostrum
for every kind of -constitution, performing every
variety of labour. He persists in reintroducing
MulhaU’s fallacy that Australians consume 2761b5.
of meat per head per annum—a figure which is cer-
tainly excessive, and for which there was never
any evidence. He abounds in careless statements
and unguarded quotations such as that “coffee
tends to strengthen the heart ” (p. 49), and
“Vogel . . . recommends coffee with cream* as a
tonic and valuable food to weakly children.”
If he had said that coffee tends to stimulate the
heart, and the moderate use of coffee, in some cases,
tends to strengthen the heart, the statement
would be. accurate. And though the moderate use
of coffee is beneficial to some weakly children—
sluggish, strumous children for example; yet for
many nervous,.febrile Australian children coffee is
rank poison if taken often or taken strong ;
and Dr. Muskett rightly says coffee should be
made strong—for adults who can stand it.
Dr. Muskett means so well, and says so many
useful things for people who can” make the
necessary qualifications and reservations, that
criticism has been twice postponed. But he
should really compile fewer books, and compile
them better.
Melb. University classics Prof. Tucker’s “ Criti-
cism of Australian Poetry” in July Review of
Reviews is constructed on the non-lucendo prin-
ciple—it criticises by,not criticising. Or, rather,
it does not cri ticise at all. The article lays down
various principles of criticism, some of which
seem sound and some debatable; but the only ap-
plication of his principles is to Mr. J. B. O’Hara’s
poetry, which cfoesn’t of itself justify the
caption. Prof. Tucker, in fact, seems to have
been so concerned to make his no - criticism
absolutely impartial that he has made it
almost absolutely bloodless and colourless. He
gets to close quarters with Mr. O’Hara, but he
beats the air in regard to Australian poetry. His
generalities have little force without an applica-
tion ; and Prof. Tucker cautiously—and wisely—
refrains from making applications. There is one
exception: he. shivers the timbers of Sladen and
his “ Century of Wooden Australian Song ” —ho !
he does smite Sladen ! But Sladen is slain : we
buried him a dozen years ago t : Prof. Tucker can’t
take his scalp, for fragments of it are mouldering in
a hundred wigwams already. When Mark Twain
was a mining reporter in California, he was
always very careful to say nothing against the
live shows which advertised in the paper: he
hit nothing .which coaid Jut back ; but now and
then, to show his perfect”'independence, he would
pick upon some old abandoned claim and give it a
blast of withering invective which made its'dry
hones rattle. That is precisely Prof. Tucker’s
method in his Review of Reviews article.
jdie Bookfellow's quatrain competition stimu-
lated Hobart Clipper to offer a.prize for the best
quatrain on Tasmania. This was judged prize-
worthy—
One is tempted to spell “ Floss ” with a capital.
This was neat —
Say, little toddler, who are you,
So sunny, smart, and showy ?
Australia is the Kangaroo
Are you Australia’s “joey”?
“ A Canvasser ” sent this—
And this played on Tasmania’s heart-shape—a
fertile source of fancy—
The “New English Dictionary” isn’t yet sure
of its name. Many scholars call it the “ Histori-
cal English Dictionary,” while it is a point of
honour with Oxford men to call it the “Oxford
English Dictionary.” It is now re-issued in
monthly parts at 3s. 6d. ; and anyone who pays
£l7 down (or £9 10s. if already subscribing) can
secure the whole, gaining a discount of about £9.
Completion is expected in 1909.
Literature’s “ relentless longueurs ” is the right
word for Henry .James’s “The Awkward Age.”
James spins an interminable cobweb of words—
and not even a dewy cobweb glistening in sun-
light, but that tangling cobweb of darkness which
wins the spider curses.
Apropos the Mendes-Vanor duel as to whether
Hamlet was literally “fat and scant of breath,”
Lond. Spectator recalled that, some seventy years
ago, a British naval officer challenged a foreign
critic who averred that Ophelia had been seduced
by Hamlet. More likely it was the other way
about.
Literature nutshells Nietzsche as moralist :
“ Nietzsche’s standpoint is simply this : that the
Christian morality of pity and brotherly kindness
is a mistake ; that what is excellent is individual
perfection of strength arid intensity of life, and
that each man should pursue this ideal -to the ut-,
most of his power regardless of social checks and
claims. ”

The Book fellow.
Where fern-enfolded runnels steal through moss
And silence till they catch the torrent’s song,
Australia’s lovely sister binds with floss
Sweet flowers for Progress, who has tarried long.
Rain-drenched rocks on thy outer rim,
Mist-wrapt forests and creek-washed ferns ;
Every traveller has to swim,
Else that traveller never returns.
The Canvasser.
O heart-shaped isle, where treasures sleep,
Why wait the word unspoken ?
The rain-drenched pines and fern fronds weep
As if that heart were broken !
Bookfellow's Competitions.
VII.—WANTED—A TRIOLET.
The Bookfellow wants a triolet, which must have
a distinct Australasian reference. 10s. 6d. will be
paid for the best received by September 9. (The
required reference is a hamper, but need not be
much hamper if you consider that a triolet may
deal with love, war, politics, scenery, books, art,
music—anything.) The points considered in judg-
ing will be (1) force and interest generally ; (2)
technical quality as a triolet.
For information : The triolet comes from
France, source of so many joys. It has eight
lines and two rhymes ; in any measure. The
order of the rhymes is a, b, a, a, a, b, a, b, like
this —
Lines 1, 2, 7, 8 must be the same, and line 1 is re-
peated as line J (A punning change is often, in-
troduced, as in line 4 above, but it is a technical
blot on the triolet, though sometimes almost
justified by the result.)
The art of the triolet is, while using repeat-
lines, to do away with the sense of repetition. In
many so-called triolets lines 7 and 8 are surplus-
age : they add nothing to the thought or interest.
So the thing to do is to take base-lines which are
capable of construction in different ways of sense,
or sound, or grammar—or all three; and subtly
interweave the variations. The adverbial or
prepositional clause, which may be tacked on at
beginning or end of a main statement, is a
favourite opening—as in this by Robert Bridges—
When first we met, we did nob yuess
That Love would prove so hard a master;
Of more than common friendliness
When first we met we did not guess.
Wiio could foretell the sore distress.
This irretrievable disaster,
When first we met?—we did not guess
That Love would prove so hard a master.
The clause “When first we met” is the key
which opens these variations. But similar keys
are as numerous as writers’ ingenuities. Some use
exclamation to disguise the repeat—as F. G.
Bowles —
Suoh a lot to do !
Such a little time !
Hardly know who’s who—
Such a lob to do !
Simply hurry through;
Breathing is a crime—
Such a lot to do !
Such a little time 1
Italics help ; so does a subject which repeats natu-
rally, like street and theatre cries ; and punctua-
tion can be varied. A short line,'a light subject,
and a dainty touch seem to suit the triolet best;
but it can grapple seriously with passionate moods
and long metres -it is not solely “ a tiny trill of
epigrammatic melody turning so simply upon its
own innocent axis. ” And more information will
be found in Gleeson White’s collection of “Ballades
and Rondeaus ” (Canterbury Poets series; Is.) —a
little jewel of a book.
Maud is mistress of hearts
Iu the township of Caledon.
Swains are sad in those parts
(Maud is mistress of arts !)
Such a penance of smarts
Hath no slave iu a galley done :
Maud is mistress of hearts
In the township of Caledon.
Hint s to Young Suicides.
IT is a cold-blooded Australian doctor
who sends these suggestions to The
Bulletin :
On the whole, the yearly number of suicides, in
proportion to population, hardly fluctuates—so far
as statistics go. In so many thousand people, so
many will die their own hands, just as among
all letters posGn during the year such-and-such a
number will be unaddressed or unstamped; so
many arrests in every hundred will be made ; and
so on. If an epidemic of suicide breaks out during
any period, and the average is over-topped, the
succeeding period will have a return lower than
the average. People will die by their own hand ;
but they usually die clumsily and painfully, when
a little knowledge would enable them to make
their quietus with grace and artistic refinement.
The advanced State of the future will provide a
means of exit from life for its disillusioned
children, and the Free Temple of Death will be as
much a Government institution as the Free Public
Library. The following article is to be regarded
as a professional summary of the objections which
a doctor feels upon viewing at a post-mortem ex-
amination the mangled and unpleasant corpse.
There may, after the recent examples, be a small
boom in potassium cyanide as an agent for self-
destruction ; but directly the public realises the
unsesthetic and indelicate results of that chemical,
there will be a slump. KCn. is rapid and cer-
tain ; but the swift heart-stoppage by which it acts
is produced by a gigantic spasm of acute agony,
and the staring eyes, foam-spattered lips, and
wrenched body, knock all the romance out of the
exit. No self-respecting modern suicide can afford
to overlook these drawbacks.
Deplorable lack of taste is evinced by the
majority of suicides. Popular physiology, and
the elementary anatomy taught in State-schools,
may do something to remedy the fault ; but until
the great public reaches a working knowledge of
the first principles of anatomy and toxicology,
it will go on as of yore cutting holes into the floor
of its mouth while searching for its carotid artery,
and absorbing boxes of rat-poison in tlie hope of
becoming a romantic and graceful corpse for its
family to weep over. Lovely woman contem-
plates a mind-picture of a‘ peaceful white face,
snowy hands, and clean underclothing, joined in
the calm sleep of death; takes an ounce of
strychnine (an objectionable drug if only from a
gustatory point of view); and in a few hours the
undertaker is swearing over a curiously-distorted
corpse which cannot be hammered into any coffin
in stock. Man, with fierce determination, lodges
a 22-calibre bullet from a cheap revolver in the.
back of his pharynx, blowing his mouth full of
powder-grains and bursting open his cheeks. After
a period in hospital he is fined 20s. for “attempted
suicide ” — i.e. , contributory negligence.
The decadent fin-de-sieale male suicide is
generally neat and certain. He approaches the
problem from the experimentor’s point of view,
and has usually tried it on the dog. Medical
suicides are naturally especially sure, although
absurd mistakes have been recorded. The quali-
fying examinations at all the great teaching centres
produce a regular crop of suicides—the medical
candidates, by virtue of profession, setting the
fashion of style and environment. Some years
ago, for instance, it beoame the rage among
Parisian students to shoot themselves while lying
beside a perfect lady. Until the fashion changed
such ladies were very shy of students about exam.-
time.
Now for methods: If poison be selected, the
irritants, corrosives and convulsants should be
eliminated at once. The cyanide series leaves
unpleasant signs. Narcotics form a favored class,
but not all narcotic agents are suitable. Lauda-
num and chloral often produce vomiting in the
tyro, and the preliminary delirium of cannabis
indica leads to discovery—and the stomach-pump.
Less-known poisons are hard to obtain, and may
cause error in tlie hands of laymen. Morphia is
distinctly the best; and tlie tiny tabloids in which
it is put up for hypodermic use form a very con-
venient medium either for oral or subcutaneous
exhibition. Twelve half-grain tabloids make an
effective but not extravagant dose by the mouth,
and possess a certain advantage in that an emetic,
followed by sgrs. potassium permanganate, will
prove an efficient antidote in case of second
thoughts. Morphia needs time for its action, but
a night is amply sufficient, and the bed-clothes are
not disturbed or stained.
Some prefer the knife. Let them observe the
golden rule and cut low. At the base of the neck
the carotids are easily reached (only one will pro-
bably be severed, but it is amply sufficient); and
by using a lieavy-bladed, razor-edged hunting-
knife the process is rapid and simple. A bath is
the best place in which to perform the operation,
as the resulting mess is considerable, and the
larger arteries throw a powerful jet. On no
account let the cut be higher than an inch below
the Adam’s apple; otherwise it means slow suffo-
cation by a flapping tongue or damaged larynx,
or . else aii ineffectual wound and an attack of
pneumonia.
Pistol ? A 42-cal. single-action long-barrelled
weapon has great advantages, though some prefer
a rifle or shot-gun. The pistol is easier to manage,
and quite as effectual The right auricle of the
heart is probably the best. situation, but diffi-
cult for the anatomical tyro to locate. The con-
cussion alone of the heavy cartridge from such a
pistol will produce the desired effect anywhere
about the heart region; but this is inartistic and
unscientific. If the head be favored—it is, per-
haps, safer—select the mouth an 1 fire well upwards
at a point about one inch in front of the uvula.
Some may prefer the outer surface of the skull,
in which case the bullet should be planted
about an inch-and-a-half above the orifice of
the ear, in a horizontal direction. A mirror
will be of service, arid the bathroom again
forms a fitting environment. The forehead
shot is unsuitable, and disfigures the face.
Accidents have frequently resulted from a temple
shot, the ball passing forward and blinding the
operator, in addition to smashing up his nasal
r^n^ y L7K C i' ee V 1) ,' mes ' V, y deflection. The mastoid
many s drawbacks. 18 1S ° ften salected ' but has
ei ni hanging is cruel and bar-
J}?£HT* llout a well-adjusted drop, which is
dilhcult to construct and test. A stab is very tin-
et; se lected, it should be made into the
i interspace between the ribs on the left side,
as close as possible, to the breast-bone, and directed
soin.w\ lat under it. This is more effective than
an attempt to reach tlie. apex, but error is easy ;
ana the stab is not to be recommended. A similar
situation on the right side is effective, but also un-
certain. Opening veins or arteries in a warm bath
lias its advantages, but the water is apt to get
cold, and the spectacle is unpleasant. Jumping
troin a height is exhilarating, but messy. Drown-
ing is not advised ; for, in addition to being a
disagreeable death, it necessitates a period of
fish-bait and watery decomposition, which is
decidedly detrimental to post-mortem appearance.
Charcoal fumes have great advantages, but. possess
a tell-tale odor, and may harm other people, a
fault which will put this method out of the ques-
tion with all right-thinking suicides.
To sum up : Use morphia or a well-selected
weapon (taking care, in the latter case, to go into
the bathroom), make sure of your anatomy, and
leave a letter in case of unfounded suspicion falling
on an innocent person. Beware of popular poisons
and, cheap revolvers ; don’t make a mess ; and
don’t alarm the neighbors more than can be
helped. And unless you are a single man or
woman with nobody dependent on you for
daily bread, or the victim of an incurable disease,
don’t do it at all ; but put up your hands for
another round and take your gruel as it comes.

WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
“VVoolloston House, Castlemaine, Nov. 30, 1897. My
dear Mr. Malyon,—l have used your ointment Ligmolliene
with distinct success in my own case, but with greater as
an emollient unguent for the face of a young lady suffer-
ing from ‘ Acne,’ very common during puberty, and you
should push it in that direction. Mr. Best, solicitor,

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “Eczema Capitis,’’ from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M'Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please acoept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely r THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOC I ETY is prepared to LEND in any amounts,
I at lowest current rates, upon FREEHOLD and LEASEHOLD CITY and SUBURBAN PROPERTIES ;on
FREEHOLD and CONDITIONALLY-PURCHASED PASTORAL and FARMING LANDS.
LOANS can be arranged either for a fixed term, or repayable by instalments of principal and interest
extending over long periods.
Arrangements have been made, whereby valuations for country properties can be made for a very mode-
rate fee, to be paid as evidence of bona fides on the part of the applicant.
The legal expenses in connection with mortgages are also on a specially low scale.
The Society is also prepared to entertain proposals for purchasing Reversionary Interests in Estates, whether
in this colony or elsewhere, or to lend upon the security ef the same on liberal terms.
Forms of proposal may be obtained on application at the Head Office, or any District Office of the Society.
RICHARD TEECE, General Manager.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After Johnson’s Dance.
After Johnson’s dance—
Don’t you recollect?
I says, “ Goin’ ’ome ? ”
You says, “ I expect 1 0
I says, “So am I! ”
You says, “ Not with me ! "
I says, “ An’ for w’y ? ”
Blowed if I could see !
You says, “Go to France! *
After Johnson’s dance.
After Johnson’s dance—
I says “Em, you might t'
“ Might I, tho’ ! ” says you.
“ G’arn, you silly fright! ”
Then I kissed you, fair !
(How you did object 1)
Towsled all your hair!
Don’t you recollect?
Took my bloomin’ chance —
After Johnson’s dance!
After Johnson’s dance —
Smacked my face, you did 1
Then I caught you —so !
Like you was a kid.
“Just do that again—
Just you do,’' you says.
You says, “do it! ” plain :
An’, of course, I does !
Who made that advance —
After Johnson’s dance ?
NIL.
The Trick that Failed.
I leoked at Bill,
Bill looked at me,
And then I looked at Bill;
Starvation had us in his claws—
Both me and Bill and Bill and me;
No profit had come to our cause,
And we were stony-broke.
I looked at Bill,
Bill looked at me,
And neither of us spoke.
We walked in silence round the block,
We were a handsome pair !
And then our nerves received a shock
As though our very fears to mock ;
Our man comes walking round the block
With hayseed in his hair.
I winked at Bill,
Bill winked at me,
And then Bill took his leave.
I sauntered on, Bill sauntered off,
Bill brushed his hair just like a toff,
I had no need to dress.
I tapped the “ country ” on his arm,
I spoke with my accustomed charm,
“ You’re Sammy Brown, I guess.”
I looked at him,
He looked at me,
He said, “ My name is Smith.”
I begged his pardon, he begged mine,
We wandered then apart.
I saw him further round the block;
Bill spoke to him, gave him a knock ;
I saw the stranger start.
Bill looked at him,.
He looked at Bill;
I cannot say Bill’s smart!
Bill begged his pardon, he begged Bill’s,
Bill came to me, alone.
He said he knew a man named Smith,
But his own name was “ Stone.”
I looked at Bill,
Bill looked at me,
And —we exchanged a groan !
H.M.
The Oxford Professor Visits Maoriland.
A ZOOLOGICAL STUDY.
Fain would I be with thee, oh Ox-
Ford, with thy woodland charms.
Instead, I view the Maori rocks
And earthquake-shock alarms.
Instead of oak I see the kau*
Ri gums and tree-ferns tall;
Instead of Saxon, hear the Mao*
Ri’s noble accents fall.
Now must I leave thee, oh, thou Horse-
Tralasian waters wide;
But soon on narrow Thames’s course
Our narrower boats will glide.
Still do I love thee, oh, my As-
Tronomic lore and science;
Yet here strange facts I can amass,
Which bid to truth defiance.
Effects I find of former cafc-
Aclysmal changes vast,
Which books first taught me. Now I rat-
Tle off the names full-blast.
But now I go back to my Liz-
Ards, toads, and such small deer;
Oh, how I have enjoyed my Phys-
lology out here !

A SEA SERPENT IN THE HUON RIVER. /
“Mr. Robert states that about 4 p./m. on
Tuesday, July 25, he was returning to Garden
Island Creek from Huon Island in a small
punt, and when off the north point of
Charlotte Cove, about a mile below the
Garden Island Cove Creek jetty, he
saw some creature in the water
which was quite unknown to him.
It had, when first seen, elevated
a long neck about 20ft. out of
the water, with a triangular-
shaped flat head bent at
right angles to the
neck.”
No, there is not much gate-money in
the honest admiration of a mere com-
patriot.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 19, 1899.
To Make Great Wealth, out of Nothing.
The mass of the people are not deeply in-
terested in banking problems, and when
The Bulletin desires to draw their atten-
tion to one by which Australia loses some
£500,000 a year, it only expects that a small
portion of an indifferent community will lend
part of its ear to the subject. Yet this paper
offers no apology for asking the loan of all
the ear which may be available. There are
mauy dark financial problems with a large
hole at the bottom of them through which
the public cash goes down into oblivion, and
in very many of them the hole might be
stopped up if the public would only under-
stand where the leakage is, and demand its
stoppage loudly enough. So long as the
public doesn’t study and doesn’t understand,
it can only relieve its feelings by general
incoherent execration. An attitude of
general curse towards “the banks” does
nothing and leads nowhere, and a feeble
desire to break into the banks and dance
with the muddy boots of revolution on the
big ledger doqs nothing-either.
* m * *
The five Australian provinces and Tas-
mania owe, among them, about £185,000,000
in public debts alone, and the average rate
of interest is about 3f per cent. There are
constantly lying idle in the banks about
£18,000,000 in gold, and if, say, £13,000,000
of this could be used to help in paying off
this debt, without dislocating business, or
destroying confidence, or breaking any-
thing, it would obviously save something
very close on £500,000 a year in interest.
It might save even more than that if
it were judiciously applied to pay off
the older and higher-prioed loans. The
money at present earns nothing. It
»es in the bank strong-rooms, and is
either periodically looked at by the auditor,
or else taken on trust by the auditor. It is
there as a precaution against a possible run
on the bank by depositors ; so long as it is
there the depositors probably won’t run ; if
they knew that it wasn’t there, and that
thei’e was nothing there instead of it which
would answer the purpose, they would pro-
bably run with such fury that there would
be murder and sudden death in the doorway.
This great mass of idle cash is there bo
“ inspire confidence,” and in view of the fact
that the money represents a dead loss of
£675,000 a year, it is a very expensive way
of inspiring the substance in question. It
is worth considering whether confidence
couldn’t be inspired at a much cheaper rate.
* * * *
The experience of Canada certainly appears
to show that it could be done. The Austra-
lian banks have, in Australia and Tasmania,
£83,000,000 of deposits, and as security
for any sudden run on these they hold
£18,000,000 in gold. The Canadian banks
have about £52,000,000 of deposits, and
against them they hold £1,830,000 in gold.
But they also hold £3,488,000 in State
notes, which answer equally well, and it is
the want of this cheap, State-guaranteed
currency which makes Australian banking
the ruinous thing it is. Canada settled the
currency question in a courageous, off-hand
fashion, by enacting that every bank must
keep at least 40 per cent, of its cash reserves
in the notes of the Federal Government.
As a matter of fact it could have made it
compulsory to hold 65 per cent, of the re-
serves in State paper, for the banks do hold
that proportion with no trouble or damage.
And—what seems not a little remarkable--
this has been done without destroying the pri-
vate note issues of the banks, which are still
large. If the cheap and useful Canadian
system, as it is actually worked in Canada, was
introduced in Australia, a Federal Govern-
ment could obtain from the banks £13,000,000
in gold, and replace it by Federal notes, and
still leave them with a stronger reserve of
coin, in proportion to their liabilities, than
the Canadian banks find necessary. And if
that £13,000,000 was used to reduce the
country’s foreign debt as the loans fall due
it would make an annual saving of £500,000
a year in interest.
♦ * * *
Only a Federal Government, of course,
could do such a transaction. At present no
Government can issue paper which is good
outside its own narrow limits, therefore gold
is wanted to carry on the vast exchange
operations among the separate provinces.
When there is a Federal Government whose
paper will be good all over Australia, then
gold will only be wanted to pay
debts outside Australia. For internal
operations, State-guaranteed paper will do
just as well, and there will be so much less
coin to serve as a bait in some possible time
of war to the invader, who will want an
indemnity. A badly - defended country,
remote from assistance, with £18,000,000
of gold lying conveniently in its banks to pay
ransom with, is a huge bait for an invader.
The banks would lose nothing by the deal ;
so long as the law made £5,000,000 in gold
and £13,000,000 in State-paper as good
security to pay their depositors with as
£18,000,000 in gold, they could sit behind
the big polished counter and await the pos-
sible run with as much equanimity as here-
tofore. And if the money obtained was
used to reduce the public debt, there
would be no possibility of the supply
of gold for foreign exchange purposes
running short. Interest has to be paid in
coin, therefore this transaction would reduce
the outflow of gold by £500,000 a year, and
with £500,000 a year less gold going out,
and with the Australian mines turning out
the yellow metal at their usual rate, the
reserves of coin would accumulate again
rapidly. A Federal Government, by this one
deal, could reduce the public expenditure by
£500,000 a year. If it converted the re-
mainder of the public debts into a uniform
3 per cent. Federal stock, it could save
nearly £1,500,000 a year more in interest,
which would mean, in all, £2,000,000 a year
less expenditure and £2,000,000 a year less
gold leaving this country annually. And if
it had courage enough then to swear off all
borrowing, and either spend the £2,000,000
on public works itself, or allot it among the
States as a public works fund, Australia might
be surprised to find itself a solvent country
again, and able to live entirely on its own
resources.
* * * *
It is only by Federation that these two
great financial operations can be carried out.
No single State could work such a scheme.
It is a process whereby, practically, a vast
sum of money will be made out of abso-
lutely nothing. The only difference to the
community would be that it would, to a
great extent, draw its salary on Saturday in
the shape of one note and 16s. in silver,
instead of one sovereign and 16s. in silver.
In a vague sort of way it might learn at
times that there were £5,000,000 of gold
and £13,000,000 of State paper in the vaults
of the banks, and remark to itself, as it occa-
sionally does now, that banks are bloated
institutions- which live by grinding the
faces of the poor ; but the change would
not be noticeable. A great part of it
wouldn’t observe for years that it always got
a note on Saturday in place of getting a
sovereign sometimes and a bit of paper at
others ; and wouldn’t notice for some more
years that the bit of paper had the super-
scription of the Federal Government on its
face. Almost certainly in half-a-dozen
years the huge gold output of Australia,
when so much less of it was drained off to
England annually in payment of interest on
the public debt, would make the yellow coin
as plentiful again to all appearance as it is
now. The banks would probably protest at
first, for banks are conservative institutions,
but the experience of Canada shows that the
State paper experiment is absolutely feasible,
and that the big institutions which recon-
struct now and then can live under that
system as well as any other. So The Bul-
letin returns the public its ear uninjured
with many thanks for the loan, and with
this moral. There is an immense amount of
money to be made out of Federation-
money which can’t be made in any other
way —provided the people work the Federal
machine to the best advantage. And if the
people will, for once, take an intelligent
interest in a dry problem of finance they will
find their attention well repaid in cash.
More Snake.
The Bulletin receives a letter :
Dear Bulletin.— Re “the Snake of Religious Dissen-
sion ” and State-schools. You make some remarks
which—to quote the aentle Belle of New York —“ almost
tempt me to say ‘ Rubbish ’! ” The population of Vic-
toria is classified by the statist as 23 per cent. Catholic
and 77 per cent. Protestant and sundries. The sundries
include some Chows, Indian hawkers, and Jos. Symes. It
is true that “the Victorian religious community is
divided into two great classes —Catholics and Protestant,”
as you say—but why dodge the point that 75 per cent, of
the whole community is non-Catholic ? The whole of the
Catholic minority is religious, fearfully and wonderfully
so, from the average “Protestant’s” point of view,
whereas the bulk of the un-Catholic majority is not
religious. Nevertheless there is everywhere among the
adults of that majority a feeling in favor of a little formal
religious instruction for their children at school. They
believe that “ the singing of a hymn, the saying of a
prayer, the reading of the Bible”' would have a good—
certainly not a bad —influence on the youngsters’ moral
characters, and they wonder why the matter can’t be
arranged with the approval of the Catholics. You say—
“ But. the Catholics have their own hymns, say their own
prayers, read their own Bible. Anything else is
anathema ; and their conscientious objection will have
force.” I say give the children the hymn “Lead,
Kindly Light,” composed by R.C. Cardinal Newman, who
’verted from the Protestant church. As for the differ-
ence between the R.C. Bible and prayers and the Protes-
tant ditto, surely sectarian differences can be avoided
in the selection of passages. What’s the Catholic objec-
tion to the Cardinal's hymn aforesaid, the psalm begin-
ning “ The Lord is my shepherd,” which one gets from
the gramophone funnel at a public entertainment, and
the parables of the Talents, the Good Samaritan, and the
Prodigal Son ? Again, I ask, what is the Catholic objec-
tion to the reading in State schools of passages from
Scripture that are common to both sects, always provided
that they be read from a paper as quotations from com-
mon Scripture, and not from a Bible ? Can you show a
reasonable objection, and if unreasonable, is there any
reason why the majorit3 r should be “ bluffed ” by the
Catholic minority? You say “HDbakin and his fol-
lowers want religion taught, let them teach it in their
homes and in their churches,” but they don’t want
religion taught with the same furious persistency as is
shown by the Catholics, who are “always refusing to
send their children to‘godless’ schools, and always,
where possible, establishing their own schools” ( vide
Bulletin) for the maintenance of Catholic dogmas. The
great unbigoted majority ask only for a scholastic recog-
nition of an Immortal Power, and a few admirable exam-
ples of ancient Christian literature, and when you say
“directly so-called ‘unsectarian’ instruction is intro-
duced, the Catholic complaint is justified,” you talkwhat
the majority regard as “ rubbish”—and sectarian rubbish
at that.— Simple Poser.
* * * *
The Bulletin presumes this letter may be
taken to exemplify the sentiments and arguments
of the average religious-heathen to whom religion
is not a vital matter like dinner; but who vaguely
believes in religious education because it is En-
glish, you know, and Respectable, and the thing
he has been accustomed to in the old home. And
the writer of the letter is known to this paper as a
man of brains and education, so he may be pre-
sumed to have put the religious-heathen’s best
foot forward. Yet his letter is five parts ground-
less assumption, and three parts characteristic
non-comprehension. Two parts show glimmer-
ings of reason.
* * * *
“ Simple Poser ” assumes that “there is every-
where among the adults of the Vic. Protestant
majority a feeling in favor of a little formal reli-
gious instruction for their children at school.”
The Bulletin assumes that there is not. One
assumption is as good as the other. But in favor
of The Bulletin’s assumption are the facts that
the Vie. majority have established and upheld
secular education; and that in the neighboring
province of S.A. —where the religious proportions
are much the same as in Victoria—the agitation
for religious teaching recently had its neck wrung
at a referendum.
* * * *
“Simple Poser ” says this paper talks what the
majority regards as “Rubbish.” Well, this paper
says “Simple Poser” talks what the majority re-
gards as “ Rubbish.” One “Rubbish ”is as good
as another. Till the majority vote, nobody knows
how they will vote. Guesses waste time. Mud
wastes space.
* * * *
“ Simple Poser ” gives the hymn he likes, and
the passage of Scripture he likes. He has omitted
to forward the prayer he habitually uses when he
comes home broke from the races or warm from
the leg-show; and he doesn’t guarantee that his
“ like ” will be the majority’s “ like.” He cannot.
He does not even guarantee that his “ like ” will
be adopted. He cannot. It is easy to erect a
harmless Aunt Sally, and say : “ What a beauti-
ful old lady ! How moral! How improving !
Let the youngsters have a shy at her. If it does
them no good, it will do them no harm. Shame
on the people who would abolish my innocent
Aunt Sally ! Down with the non-religious
heathen and his fetish ! ”
* * * *
The trouble is that Aunt Sally has less to do
with the case than the wattle that blooms in the
Spring. Whatever the hymn selected, it is not at
all likely to be “Lead, kindly light”—for the
reason that “Lead, kindly light,” though an
excellent mystical poem for adults of a certain
temperament, is quite unintelligible to most
children without explanation, or even with ex-
planation. Anyway, explanation is barred by the
hypothesis. “The Lord is my shepherd,” is
similarly unintelligible unless taken as the imagin-
ative outpouring of an ancient Israelite, and con-
strued imaginatively. “Simple Snoozer’s ” ex-
treme simplicity is shown by the calm way in
which he provides for the tender shoot religious
nourishment which many grown trees cannot
assimilate. If he were a school teacher he would
know better. The Bulletin would be quite
satisfied to let the question of Vic. religious
education be decided by a vote of school teachers.
They know what a farce it is to parrot sacred
poetry in the intervals of geography and arith-
metic to an audience of children—the most un-
mitigated heathens extant.
The trouble is also that Aunt Sally would not
come under the guise of a meaningless hymn or a
mysterious parable. The people who want religious
education would—very properly—not be satisfied
with the colorless unintelligibilities which “ Same
old Snoozer ” proffers. They would want vital
truths —that is to say, dogmas and creeds; and
they would not be happy till they got them. Vic.
experience alone has shown that.
* * * *
Further, unless an exact statement of the hymn
and the prayer are given on the referendum-paper,
there is no knowing that a majority approves of
them. Certainly a minority will disapprove. It
is not the State’s business to offend the religious
convictions or no-convictions even of a minority.
There is good ground for State education
•experience has shown that, though education may
not make good citizens, ignorance makes bad
citizens ; and bad citizens hurt the State. State
education is State self-protection. As long as the
State confines itself to matters of known fact it is
safe. There can be no dispute about the teaching
that two and two make four, or that the Wradvt
weather nuisance will spread if Wradvt-junior is
added to Wradvt-senior at Mt. Whiskyusco. But
it is not the State’s business to affirm that there
exists “an Immortal Power.” The State is con-
structed to look after society in this world, and
in is as ignorant as anybody else about what is
beyond this world. Experience shows that the
State has always got along much better when it
laid to its book.
* * * *
“Simple Poser” triumphantly points out that
his hymn and his Scripture are so colorless as to
mean nothing in particular. That is to say, they
are husks. The reason is eminently creditable to
the Catholics. They believe that religion is a
vital matter, and they are not satisfied to have it
put before their children as if it was an illegiti-
mate baby on a doorstep. Consequently they
have their own schools, where they can teach
adequately the religion in which they believe. If
Protestants like “Simple Poser” did their duty
by their religion, they would have their own
schools too. But that logical person wants to
shirk teaching a red-hot religion calculated to do
some good, and he wants to make Catholics pay
for teaching even the cold-potato religion which is
all he cares about. Suum cuique —which means,
let every sect teach its own religion, and let the
State schools teach grammar and spelling.
This article is another chunk sawn off a large
subject; but the long and the short of the subject
is this. It is expedient for the State to see that
all its children are taught the common subjects of
education, upon which there is no disagreement.
While it teaches those only, no sect has an excuse
for complaining. As soon as it starts teaching
somebody’s pet hymn, or pet prayer—even if it
goes no farther —it gives just ground for complaint
by all who dislike that hymn or that prayer, or
who dislike to hear it mumbled on a wet morning
by a cold teacher with all his work cut out to
keep the class up to the inspector’s standard in
sums, and realising that hymn and prayer do no
good, and take so much time out of the working-
day. And, even if there did not exist churches
and parents to teach hymns and prayers, the State
has no business, by trenching one inch on the de-
batable ground of religion, to exclude from its
schools the children of people who object to go
that one inch. The State schools are for the whole
community; and no religious conscience - test
should be permitted to shut out one single indi-
vidual. We can all agree about sums.
The N.S.W. Labor Party.
The N.S. W. Parliamentary Labor-party is in
a pretty bad way. From about 30 members
in ’9O it has got to 19 in ’99 —and it is
more or less doubtful whether it has all
of them. Of these 19, the great majority
were lately not able to persuade their
constituents to take the same view that they
took of the biggest question that has ever
arisen in Australian politics. Fifteen Labor
constituencies voted on the question in pre-
cisely the way that 17 of the Labor members
told them not to, on any account. The two
members who did go as 15 out of 19 Labor
constituencies in N.S.W. went, and as every
other Labor constituency so far appealed to
elsewhere has gone, have before and since
been branded as “traitors.” Meantime,
the other 17 are as happy as sick black-
fellows ; mostly one section of them is
engaged calling other sections pet names.
Holman is bludgeoning the “official Labor
organ the official Labor organ is bludgeoning
Holman. Sleath, reckoned one of the most
extreme of the members, declares in a burst of
frankness that the men who are running the
movement make him tired of it. Nobody
knows precisely what the “Labor” policy is;
and there is altogether as much confusion
as one would naturally expect in a party whose
“leader” was chosen because other brighter
men were too jealous of one another to clionse
anybody else. Most leaders make themselves ;
but, under caucus rule, if one man, feeling
himself, begins to move lie is laid on the broad
of his back and sat upon. Sometimes he sub-
mits ; sometimes he doesn’t. The only ones
who don’t get sat on are good-natured, com-
plaisant fellows like Cann, who, when they
can’t agree with the caucus, go home and say
nothing about it; and the harmless nonentities
like and and But the list
is too long for publication;
* * * *
The party was born of the big strike of ’9O.
It swept one or two of the old four-seat Sydney
electorates and several in the country ; and, as
it looked as if it were in for a good time, it
soon had a following. But the heat of ’9O did
not last, and the following soon began to
slacken its energy. The party had, for one
thing, vowed to sink the fiscal issue; but it
presently dawned on some people that the party
wasn’t doing anything of the sort —that all the
time it was keeping up either one end of the
log or the other. The people who wanted the
other end kept up kicked; and every time the
party changed ends (in conformity with the
squeeze-and-ehuck policy) some supporters and
seats were lost—which probably has something
to do with the steady thick-and-thin support
given for years to Wriggler Reid. Besides,
there is hardly a member of the present party
who is a confessed Protectionist, while about a
dozen frankly avow themselves Freetraders.
But they still go on sinking the fiscal
issue ; and they still go on pretending to ex-
pect working-men Protectionists to vote for
them.
* 4 * *
Fifteen out of 19 Labor constituencies would


not take the almost unanimous advice of the
party on Federation. Whom, then, do the
Labor 19 represent at all ? Whose policy is the
Labor policy—who makes it ? The Political
Labor League is boss; and it represents a
working man here and there. Labor unions,
which are the best agencies to secure working
men’s salvation yet contrived, comprise only a
small percentage of workmen—not a seventh of
the total in N.S.W.— and of this less than
seventh about one in a dozen takes any interest
in the P.L.L. It gives no dividend, no bonus,
no sick-pay ; it doesn’t even offer a chance
in an “art” union ; and so it is passed.
Of the few outside the bush-unions who do
interest themselves in the P.L.L., about
one - third are talkative gentlemen with
ambitions (and there were only four M.L.C.
appointments among so many !), one-
third are mostly bitter men with grievances,
and one-third are fine, whole-hearted reformers,
with whom this paper hereby shakes hands,
wishing them well. In the bush unions, the
reformer section may be extended to, say, a
half of the total. Skite, bitterness, and
earnestness are thus associated ; and earnest-
ness doesn’t get more than half a chance.
Still, whatever results is put down to the
Labor-party. After all, then, it isn’t wonder-
ful that when it comes to a popular appeal to
the ballot, that policy and that party are no-
where. See what it did about Federation. First,
it adopted a platform which a few extremists
here and there conscientiously approved. Then
it nominated a full ticket of ten men to
fight for that platform. In every other pro-
vince it was proposed to run Labor candidates ;
but, instead of agreeing upon an Australian
Labor Federal policy, the N.S. W. party fixed
up one for itself away ahead of all the rest.
Running ten candidates, it also rendered
alliances impossible; and so it got no repre-
sentation at all. These members, or a majority
of them, still declare that the only acceptable
brand of Federation is their brand, and they
try to stop others getting any other brand.
Being few in numbers, they fail, of course, and
so they waste their whole force. They do not
realise that compromise spells political pro-
gress ; yet until they do, or until they
have a majority of their own, they must
go down every time that they don’t fluke
a win. They are careful, however, never
to get a majority, because they regard it
as incumbent on them to always keep ahead
of the crowd. So the Labor-party, if it keeps
on these lines, will never do'anything itself—
it will always be away ahead. When a man
among them, seeing a chance to gain something
that has been fought for, stops to do it, he will
be fired out as “ bogus.” And as most men of
much account do want to do something off their
own bat, most men of much account will be
fired out. Also, though administration is
often quite as important as legislation, it will
never get a chance to administer. That is, if
the party goes on these present lines. But in
that case it will not be a Labor-party at all;
it will continue to represent, as it does now,
mostly a few voluble extremists, many of whom
are not working-men at all. And because The
Bulletin wants to see a party really repre-
senting the grafters of the community, and
because at one time the Labor-party promised
to be that party, it writes these things in sor-
row, yet in hope.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Resources of this Great Country.
The anti-Bill meeting held last week in Bris-
bane naturally brought up old Hugh Nelson
with the weary invariable tale about the
Resources of this Great Country. No anti-
Federal meeting up North, or anywhere else,
is complete without some old inhabitant
babbling about the Resources of this Great
Country. Nelson’s theory was the one which
is always babbled about ou such occasions—that
owing to the extraordinary and unprecedented
resources of this great country Queensland can
get on perfectly well by itself, and that for a
place of such unparalleled resources to federate
with any other place would be practically an
insult to its Creator. Therefore, Hugh Nelson
urged Queensland, as a place of stupendous
resources, to go on its separate way as here-
tofore. Poor old Nelson is one of the
very large crowd which thinks that debt is the
same thing as wealth. Queensland has never
yet attempted to live on its own resources, so
how it would pull through on them is an abso-
lutely unknown quantity. It has borrowed,
publicly and privately, about .£60,000,000, and
had a giddy revel on the proceeds. Its exist-
ence has been a giddy revel from the very
beginning. It has now to send away annually
about £2,649,000 in interest to foreign money-
lenders and absentees, and it is borrowing more
per head than any other province, bar Wes-
tralia, to help meet this bill. Coghlan, the one
Australian statistical authority who deals sys-
tematically with the figures of all Australia,
publishes some harrowing tables on pages 430
and 433 of his “Seven Colonies” for 1898,
showing that Queensland is the most deeply-
indebted place, in proportion to population, in
Australasia—and also in the world. Even the
most profligate of the other provinces doesn’t get
within gunshot of the boss profligate up North.
It is mortgaged from one end to the other to
the financiers of Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney,
and London. It has made a spell of artificial
prosperity by building railways with borrowed
money till it is the most-railw'ayed country, in
proportion to population, in the world, Wes-
tralia only excepted, but its railway system is
lamentably unprofitable as compared with
those of Westralia, South Australia, N.S.W.,
or Maoriland. It is a great gold country, yet
it has only in one year got ahead of Victoria as
a gold producer, and Victoria is just about one-
eighth its size. It is about as fertile as any
other part of Australia on an average, and as
dry as any other place, and as tired. But there
is nothing extraordinary about its resources.
The country has never managed to live
on them yet, and what will happen when
it has to make the attempt—it can’t do
another 40 years of borrowing like the
last 40 or it will burst with a loud report —
there is no precedent to show. Queensland is
accustomed to brag a good deal about its gold
production, but it has borrowed—in public and
private borrowings—more than the value of all
the gold it has turned out since it first began !
It has borrowed as much as the value of all the
gold, silver, copper, tin and coal it lias pro-
duced up to date !! It has borrowed, publicly
and privately, as much as the value of all the
wool it has produced up to date !! ! It has
borrowed more than the value of all its agricul-
tural and manufactured products up to date.
Its great industries have been : (1) Borrowing;
(2) wool; (3) gold-mining. So when Hugh
Nelson assumes that Queensland’s progress in
the past is proof that it can safely rely on its
own great resources and doesn’t require any
Federation, he is a foolish old man talking
through a very demented hat. Queensland
has never even attempted to rely on its own re-
sources so far.
Victorian Prison Horrors.
Hume Cook, M.L.A., on Victorian prisons :
It is within the power of the governor of the gaol to
give a man two days’ solitary confinement for any offence.
... It is within the power of the visiting justices to give
a man two years in irons, 30 days’ solitary treatment, or
30 days’separate treatment. . . . The prisoner is brought
before the visiting justices in the office, or some place of
the kind, at the prison, and the only persons present are
the warder who accuses him, the governor perhaps, and
the visiting justice. What sort of fair play can a prisoner
get? . . . Case A was sentenced in 1890 to eight years’
penal servitude for robbery. For two years after that
man was sent to prison he committed no offence what*
ever; he was not guilty of the slightest breach
of the prison regulations or the prison disci-
pline. At the end of that time, one Sunday
morning, he refused to go to church. I would draw’
particular attention to the fact that this occurred
on a Sunday morning, because the prisoners are locked
up on Saturday at 12 o’clock, and remain in close con-
finement for 20 hours, whereas 13 hours is the longest
time for which they are confined on other days. The
man refused to go to church because, as he said, he was
not well. On the following Monday morning he admitted
his error and promised that if he was not punished he
would commit no other breach of the regulations. The
fact that for two years that man committed no offence
may be accepted as proof of his desire to get out of gaol
as soon as possible. He received for this offence two
days’ solitary confinement, and for disobedience
of orders two days’ separate treatment. After that,
his various sentences amounted to 34 months 57
days cumulative punishments, making in all about
11 years instead of the 8 years for which he was
sentenced, and 9 months 133 days concurrent punish-
ment. The concurrent punishments consisted of im-
prisonment in irons, solitary treatment, and separate
treatment, with their accompanying disabilities of low
diet and lack of exercise and privileges In the
second case, B was sentenced to 3 years’ imprisonment.
He has served 9 years, and has 18 months more to run.
Think of any mere justice, an individual
generally ignorant of law, and appointed
mainly for the purpose of fining drunks 55.,
being allowed to sentence an undefended
prisoner, in secret, to 7J years’ imprisonment,
with irons, solitary, low diet and other horrors
thrown in ! According to Hume Cook the Vic.
Government has lately invented another ghastly
idea, but this one isn’t intended as a punish-
ment ; it is merelj' an economy. No prisoner
serving a sentence of less than 12 months is
now allowed a light in his cell, so in the winter
he puts in 13 or 14 hours out of every 24 in
total darkness ! As a picturesque horror that
almost “ lays over ” the J.P.

.Reasonably Supposed to be Stolen.
Instructive item from S.M. Herald of Btli
August, 1899 :
At Paddington Police Court on Thursday James
MTntyre was sentenced to 14 days’ imprisonment on a
charge of having property in his possession supposed to
have been stolen. Our report of the case was read by the
person from whom the articles were purchased. He
communicated with the authorities, and the innocence
of the accused being shown MTntyre was at once
liberated.
This seems a fair text on which to base the old
question : Isn’t it time that every allegedly
civilised country abolished the practice of gaol-
ing persons for having property about them
“reasonably supposed to be stolen”? The
practice is as unjust and illogical as would be
that of hanging people for the murder of some-
body reasonably supposed to be dead—some-
body whose body wasn’t found and regarding
whom there was no conclusive evidence that he
had ever been alive. The whole theory of
goods “ reasonably supposed to be stolen ” is
just this : A zealous policeman stops somebody
who looks poor enough and uninfluential
enough to be run-in with impunity, and asks
how he became possessed of something.
Probably if he is very poor his proper-
ties are so old that he bought them
years ago in some other province, and it
is impossible to bring evidence about their
origin. Probably he explains their origin and
the policeman decides, on the evidence of his
own inward bobby, that the statement isn’t
true. The prisoner is run in. If he has ex-
plained where he got the article, and the
alleged seller is available, probably that
alleged seller isn’t communicated with—by the
police, at all events. The. prisoner, as a rule,
gets no chance to communicate with him on
his own account. Sometimes it is mentioned
in the papers that the police want an owner
for an old silver watch or some other properly
supposed to have been stolen, which is a direct
invitation for somebody who wants a cheap
watch to claim the article and swear the man
on whom it was found into prison.. And if no
owner or alleged owner can be found, and if it
can’t be shown that any such arLicle was stolen
from anybody, and there is no evidence of theft
whatever, then the charge is laid of being
possessed of goods which a policeman thinks,
or says he thinks, were dishonestly come by,
said policeman being unable to show any good
legal reason for his thinking. In short, the
charge is one of being a person who, in a
policeman’s opinion, looks like a person who
might have stolen something—which is much
the same as the charge of looking the way
Bonaparte might have looked if he hadn’t
looked the way he did look. “ Reasonably
supposed to be stolen ” is often just as logical as
burying a live man on a police charge of being
reasonably supposed to be dead.
Parliament and Referendum.
B. R. Wise :
One of the lessons of the Federal campaign is that
government by Referendum means government by news-
papers. By the introduction of the Referendum, they got
rid of legislation by responsible representatives of the
people. They gave up the government of the country to
people whom they did not know, and who were respon-
sible to no one, anti took it out of the hands of those who
were answerable. For his part he would rather
be governed by the worst Parliament than by the best
newspaper in the world.
B. R. Wise is steadily ripening into a fine
illogical Tory. This is his case cub up into
short lengths for convenience of carriage :
Government by Parliament means that one set of news-
papers calls furiously to the people to elect Smith because
he will vole for somethin}', and another set calls to them
to elect Jones because he will vote against something.
And the dazed, newspaper-ridden community finally
elects Smith.
Government by Referendum means that the same
newspapers call to the same people to vote for or against
the same thing directly, without the intervention of Jones
and Smith.
The Referendum gets rid, on certain points, of “ govern-
ment by responsible representatives of the people,” as
Mr. Wise says. And it substitutes government by the
responsible people themselves. In other words, instead
of Mr. Wise’s responsible office-boy conducting the great
case in which Mr. Wise is briefed, and being responsible
to Mr. Wise if he muddles it (which is little consolation
to anybody after it is muddled), Mr. Wise conducts the
case himself.
_ If the Federal Constitution had been adopted by Par-
liament, only, it would have been a case of the newspapers
yelling in the ears of Brown, M.L.A., and Smith, M.L.A.,
that they should do this or that if they didn’t want to be
utterly lost at next election, and also calling to the
people to fire out Brown and Smith if they didn’t
do this and that. And furthermore, Brown and
Smith, being just ordinary people like any others,
would be swayed in their own opinions by the
papers as much as any other people would be. Brown,
M. L.A., and Smith, M.L.A., would be guided partly by
the conscientious opinions that they borrowed from the
newspapers, and partly by what the newspapers told them
that the people wanted, and by what the newspapers told
them would happen to them at next election if they did,
or if they didn’t. And the people, with their opinions
largely borrowed from the newspapers, would be trying
to explain to Brown, M.L.A., and Smith, M.L.A., what
they wanted them to do, and the newspapers would act
as interpreters, and tell Brown and Smith what the people
wanted them to do. Only one set of newspapers would
tell Brown and Smith that the people vv.\ ited them to do
one thing, and the other set would tell them that the
people wanted the other thing.
And by the Referendum the people, with their opinions
largely borrowed from the newspapers, state exactly and
beyond all mistake what they do want.
The Referendum is B. R. Wise, barrister, stating his
case to the Judge. And Parliamentary Government is
B. R. Wise hiring another man to state his case, and not
allowed to tell him till three years afterwards (at next
general election) that he is stating it all wrong, but ges-
ticulating frantically in the background, while the editor of
the Herald tells Mr. Wise’s deputy that Mr. Wise is going
on like that in pure rage and frenzy at his deputy’s foolish-
ness, and the editor of the Telegraph tells the deputy
that B.R.W. is prancing about in that awful manner
through sheer joy at his agent’s cleverness.
In short, the Referendum is largely government by the
newspapers telling the people what they should do.
And Parliamentary government is government by the
newspapers telling Parliament what it should doj and
telling the people what they should make Parliament do,
and what they should do to Parliament if it doesn't, do
it, and also telling Parliament what the people want it to
do, and generally acting as a wrong interpreter all round.
Anyhow, B. R. Wise’s cheerful readiness to live under
the worst Parliament existing will probably die away be-
fore long. He probably doesn’t know how bad the worst
Parliament can be.
“Jack Shay ” makes a very straight-out state-
ment :
At Darlington (Sydney) in March last there died of
poison—whether self-administered or not there was no
evidence to show—one Leddy, who had some time before
bought a burial-allotment in the R.C. cemetery, and had
erected on it a tombstone bearing his name, date of
birth, &c., with the usual blank for date of demise and
age thereat. Deceased had not been particular as to
what church he patronised, and “ shuffling off ” rather
suddenly had not been attended in his last moments by
any clergyman. Consequently the R.C. authorities—
presumably consideiing him a suicide—refused to allow
him to be buried in the (paid-for) allotment. But,
as it turned out at the annual meeting of the St.
Mary’s R.C. Cathedral management on June 4, 1899
(Daily Telegraph, June 5) that “ under the will
of the late Mr. Leddy a sum of £2500 was ex-
pected immediately”—it was thereafter announced in
the parish in which Leddy died that at the expiration of
the regulation 12 months from interment, Leddy’s re-
mains will be exhumed, and, with bell, book and candle,
solemnly re-interred in his purchased plot of consecrated
ground. All which, summed-up, amounts to this : The
R.C. Church—having in the first instance declared, by
implication, that Leddy, being unfit for burial in conse-
crated ground, had gone straight toSheol—now consents,
in consideration of the sum of £2500, to reverse his sen-
tence of damnation ; and thus shamelessly discounts the
value of its pronouncements upon—from its own point of
view—the most important of all questions. Perhaps
when Cardinal Moran has finished with the mote in the
Protestant missions’ eye he may look into and have
something to explain about this tremendous beam in
Holy Church’s own optic.
The Bulletin, which, of course, does not
vouch for the allegations here set out, will be
glad to have the other side of the Leddy case,
if there is any.

Editor Bulletin. —Sir, — Re your comments
on the sentences passed by the Hobart justices
on two prisoners who escaped from gaol and
were recaptured (B. 5/5/’99), the following
passage in the preface to “Readiana” (Charles
Reade) seems peculiarly apposite :
I find I have gone a little beyond the mark in calling
the execution of Murdoch illegal. It is not prima-facie
illegal to hang a riian who kills an officer in the discharge
of his duty, but in this country law goes by precedent;
Murdoch garotted the gaoler, not with the intention of
killing him, but of escaping while the gaoler was dis-
abled for a time. The desire for liberty is as natural
and overpowering as hunger, and the prisoner acted
upon it with no murderous intention whatever.
He never left the neighborhood, sure proof he
did not know he had killed the gaoler ; and went into
tears when he heard the old man was dead. The people
who at that date misgoverned this nation had tempted
Murdoch to the act by leaving Hastings gaol insufficiently
guarded. When they hung the youth they had tempted
—hung him to hide their own fault —the spectators of the
execution were fewer than ever assembled to see a hang-
ing before or since, and the only cry that came from this
handful of spectators was “Murder! Murder!!” Just
three months after this butchery, an escaped prisoner
was brought before a Judge ; the Judge was invited by
the Crown to inflict condign punishment; he treated the
proposal with contempt. “ The prisoner," said he,
“ yielded to the natural and imperious desire of liberty.
It was his business to escape, and it was the gaoler’s busi-
ness not to let him.”
The underlining is mine.—J.D.P., Barberton,
Transvaal, 21/6/’99.

N.S.W. Labor-member Josiah Thomas, who
went over to Westralia to explain why his
party was against Federation in N.S.W. :
Of course we are in favor of direct taxation. Conse-
quently Federation will mean to us, instead of direct
taxation, a greater amount of taxation than we are
having at present. That is the reason why we object to
the financial portion of the Bill. It is not that we are
afraid of our surplus being taken by necessitous States,
but simply that we shall have our taxation—which is
mostly direct at present—obtained indirectly through
the Customs.
Now, apart from the fact—which Thomas must
surely know if he knows enough to tell a dog
from a mangle—that there is not a single word
in the Constitution committing the Federal
Government to indirect taxation, or any form
of taxation in particular, that gentleman is
telling a huge fish-yarn about the finances of
his own province. In 1898 N.S.W.’s indirect
taxation was £1,670,000, and its direct ditto
about £900,000 (more than one-third of it from
stamps). And it would have been less than
£BOO,OOO only the best part of two years’
land-tax was collected in one year. N.S.
Wales’s taxation never was “mostly direct,”
and when the Thomas crowd appeals for
votes against Federation on the ground that the
system of mostly-direct taxation will be des-
troyed it is eilher two asses or two frauds—
one of them because there is no such system ; to
destroy, and the other because there is nothing
in the Commonwealth Bill to destroy it. The
N.S.W. Labor-party went into Parliament,
partly to establish direct taxation in place of
indirect ; and Geo. Reid went into office to do
the same. They both failed so badly that, -in
1898, direct taxation in N.S. W. was less per
head^than in Maoriland, Tasmania, or S.A.
But they don’t like to talk about the failure.
The painful subject is dropped by mutual agree-
ment.

Hobart gives one Chinaman seven municipal
votes, and every landlord may vote in respect
of every cottage he owns. The occupier also
has a vote, but it is easy to see how tenant-
interests may be totally annulled by the land-
lord -interests at municipal elections. And
landlord rule has been eminently successful in
converting what is perhaps the most naturally-
healtliy city in Australasia into a city of un-
mitigated stink. That a place which professes
to cater for the tourist-trade doesn’t r'se up
and smother its civic authorities in the stnell-
fullest part of the far-smelled open sewer
known as the Hobart Rivulet, is a splendid
tribute to the presence of patience or the ab-
sence of nose in the Isle of Dreams. It was
stated in Parliament recently that, from Jan.
to July last, Hobart and suburbs produced 70
cases of typhoid fever, 220 cases of scarlet
fever, eight of diphtheria, and 14 of measles.
The hospital authorities say they can’t accom-
modate nearly all the scarlet-fever patients, and
Parliament has been moved to appoint a com-
mittee to consider the establishment of a hos-
pital, even temporarily, for infectious diseases ;
though Premier Braddon, with an eye on the
Treasury cash-box, opposed the notion with all
his hands and feet. He likewise pathetically
deprecated the “ growing tendency to proclaim
and exaggerate the unhealthiness of the city” !
But as Dr. Crowther, the only medical mem-
ber of Tas. Assembly, bluffly asserted that
there had been at one time 124 cases of typhoid
in Hobart Hospital, properly a 100-bed institu-
tion, the Premier backed down. If the com-
mittee doesn’t fall fast asleep until Xmas, there
is an outside chance of something being re-
commended in the way of an infectious dis-
eases hospital before the tourist season after
next.
Cardinal Moran states his ideas on the official-
precedence question :
That is a matter which was settled finally by Lord
Jersey. I never went to Govt. House functions until his
time, and, when he asked the reason, I said I would pro if
given ray proper position as a Prince of the Roman
Catholic Church. Personally, of course, it matters little
to me what position I occupy at a dinner-table ; but I
could not really go until the dignity of the position had
been placed beyond doubt. Lord Jersey communicated
with Downing-street, and now a Cardinal takes precedence
in all colonies where there is no Established Church. It
is not the question of the difference between dates of ap-
pointment so far as the Archbishop is concerned, for, as I
said before, it has been established in all countries where
there is no Established Church that a Cardinal is senior
to an Archbishop. There is no other rank equal to that
of Cardinal in N.S.VV., and that fact is recognised at
Govt. House, as everywhere else. To give an Archbishop
precedence over a Cardinal would be very much like
placing the captain of an army over a general. Of course,
you must understand I am speaking of places where there
is no Established Church. There is the crux of the whole
matter.
This attitude won’t greatly tend to popularise
the Cardinal. He may call himself a Prince if
he likes, but so far as the Australian States are
concerned he is only a Prince of the R.C.
Church in the same way as Primate Smith is a
Prince of the Anglican Church, and the Wes-
leyan boss is a Prince of the Wesleyan Church,
and the Dipper boss, whoever he may be, is a
Prince of the Baptist Church. What is the
matter with Cardinal Moran is that,
though a good organiser and a tjger to
work, he is utterly destitute of the social
savee, personal charm and high-bred court-
liness which distinguished his predecessors,
Archbishops Folding and Vaughan, and
which gained what amounted to precedence for
them wherever they went. In this respect he
might with advantage sit at the feet of the deft
and polished Archbishop Carr, who never un-
necessarily raises ugly questions and who,
when be does get into controversy, comports
himself like a swordsman and not like the mere
wielder of a bit av a shtick.

Now is the time for someone to give President
Kruger a copy of Francis Adams’s “ Songs of
the Army of the Night,” with the invocation to
the noble British soldier—
I stand and watch and wonder
When in the English land
This poor fool, Tommy Atkins,
Will learn and understand.
Zulus, and Boers, and Arabs
All fighting to be free,
Men and women and children
Murdered and maimed has he.
And the address to England :
We hate you—not because of cruel deeds
Staining a glorious effort. They who live
Learn in this earth to give and to forgive,
Where heart and soul are noble and fate’s needs
Imperious : No, nor yet that cruel seeds
Of power and wrong you’ve sown alternative,
We hate you, we your sons that yet believe
That truth and justice are not empty creeds 1
No, but because of greed and golden pay,
Wages of sin and death ; because you smother
Your conscience, making cursed ail the day.
Bible in one hand, bludgeon in the other,
Cain-like you come upon and slay your brother,
And, kneeling down, thank God for it, and pray !
Those lines are as true to-day as when written;
and doubters-are referred to the late R. Wal-
lace s article on “ The Seamy Side of Imperial-
ism ” in last Contemporary Review.
