Frank Harvey’s awkwardly-named drama,
“ Shall we Forgive Her ? ” on which The Bulletin
cast a rather favorable eye at Sydney Royal a
year or two ago, is revived this week at the
Lyceum. Serious consideration, interspersed with
drinks, leads this organ to an even more favorable
opinion than it formed in those bygone days.
Plarvey’s drama is a strong, well-constructed play
with Soul in it, ansi is miles above the ordinary
seedy ruck of rneloscream. It is the tale of a
woman with a Past (there is only one kind of
Past that a woman can have). She used to have
it in South Africa, but for unknown reasons it is
now shifted to a dry, glarv part of Queensland.
There she and the digger who should have married
her, but didn’t, live in a hut in the Dry Country,
and the digger and six other devils like unto
himself look for gold in a gully. The, stuff pans
out twenty curses to the ton, and no inure. The
woman casts off her seven dreadful diggers
in course of time, and returns to Eng-
land, where she marries happily and hopes
to hear no more of these days in the Dry
Country which isn’t going to federate with
N.S.W. because of something or other about its
Great Natural Resources. But after a year of
happiness and peace the Past puts its griniy hand
in at the door and demands money as the price of
its silence. Also the Past looks with the blood-
shot eye of beer through the window and wants
drink; and the broken boots of the Past step
softly on the carpet and refuse to go away ; and
the Past puts its greasy, battered hat on the table
and casts the shadow of its rancid head across the
poor woman’s happiness. It is a dreadfully coarse,
unshaven Past, and when at last the husband
learns that his dainty wife had been a Magdalene
along with this pernicious object in the Dry
Country, he fires her out, and settles down to
blindness and Roman fortitude. In the end
he forgives the lady, and just here the drama
has been greatly improved. At the Royal two
years ago he forgave her publicly in the garden
while she knelt on the damp grass ; and the
baker’s man passing with the loaf, and the
butcher’s boy carrying the suet to Mrs. Smith, and
the policeman, and the nursemaid, and the agri-
culturist going for a pot of beer, were all at
liberty to see the function over the fence. It was
a very public place to forgive anybody in. Hol-
loway has now thought better of it, and the lady
is forgiven indoors.
■* * * •
The most notable person in this really fine
drama is a mere side-issue—one Joanna, a poor
Christian relative of the worst description, who
listens at keyholes, and carries information and a
prayer-book, and can’t sleep for fear that she
hasn’t been as horrid as the Lord intended her to
be to the girl with the Past. Miss Watts-Phillips’s
Joanna, like everything else that Miss Watts-
Phillips does, is good, but a lady gifted by nature
with a more sour disposition might have put an
even finer edge on Joanna’s awfulness. John
Saunders gives a very fine rendering of the
hero-in fact, it would be difficult for the part
to be done, better; and Miss Dora Winton,
in the mournful streaks of her part, is about
as good as possible- But in the cheerful streaks
she is a comparative failure, her cheerfulness
being of the subdued and tearful order. Still she
does one thing which deserves great praise:
instead of repenting in the conventional longdark
cloak and hood, over a brown dress with no
collar, she covers her broken heart with a well-
fitting greenish dress of costly material, and the
head bowed down has a large, expensive hat on
top of it. Miss Alice Deorwyn also makes a
success as the kind but combustible old lady who
takes the part of the oppressed and is with diffi-
culty restrained from slapping J oaitna, and some
other people do their duty more or less well.
Also, two of them do it badly.
** * *
The “King’s Musketeer” will run till
end of next week at Her Majesty’s, and it is to be
succeeded on Saturday (26th August) by “ Secret
Service” —a drama which has struck Europe and
America with great force. The “ Musketeer ”
should be quite good enough to last until the new
drama arrives, and gets its things off, and is ready
to perform. Porthos alone should be sufficient to
keep it going till then, to say nothing of the
general gorgeousness of the large-booted muske-
teers, and the beauty of the Queen and Miladi, and
the winsomeness of Miss Rose Musgrove as
D ’Artagnan’s sweetheart. By the way—why is it
that in both the versions of “ Three Musketeers ,r
lately produced in Sydney the authors have been
at such pains to bqwdlerise D’Artagnan’s sweet-
heart ? In the original, she was Constance, the
fair young wife of shopkeeper Bonacieux. And
shopkeeper Bonacieux was fat, and squat, and
white, and flabby, and mean, and flat-footed, and
common, and given to sneaking round corners. He
didn’t mind his wife being D’Artagnan’s Magda-
lene ; he merely looked with a pale, fat eye on
the seducer, and tried to raise sixpence or so on
the information ; and in the end he was righteously
dumped into the Bastille for life on a charge of
being a shapeless blot on the landscape and an
object that Richelieu didn’t like to see in the
street. One version of the “ Musketeers ” white-
washes Constance by making her shopkeeper
Bonacieux’s niece. The other drops shopkeeper
B. altogether. Why it should be necessary to
make such a sacrifice to Respectability in a city
where 22 per cent, of the births are Not Respect-
able it is hard to say, but the imperative necessity
for whitewashing Constance appears to have
struck everybody concerned. And Constance is
whitewashed accordingly, and transformed into
an awfully nice girl who would refleot credit on
any Sunday-school.
mm # *
The “ Brixton Burglary,” which is mostly
the mystery of Diggle and Pontifex’s lost
clothes, has been contributing a good deal to
the gaiety of nations at Sydney Royal all the
week. One restful characteristic about it is that
nobody comes away feeling any call to be less
sinful than he was before. There are so many
plays which call on a man to repent of something
that one which doesn’t invite him to repent of
anything is welcome now and then. If there is
any moral about the “ Burglary,” it is that the
man who is eminently virtuous loses a great deal
of fun. No doubt Pontifex, as a married man,
shouldn’t have gone out with Higgle to have a
riotous time with the Bounding Sisters—especially
the married Bounder whose husband was away.
Is or should he have lied about it afterwards to his
mother-in-law—or if he did lie he should have
lied better. He ought to have spent a sober
evening at home with a brown-covered book,
and gone to bed at ten. Also, it was obviously
wrong in servant-girl Perkins to go out on a
stolen bicycle; the reprehensibleness of James, in
going to an orgy in his master’s suit, calls for
deafening condemnation; and the person who
stole Diggle’s clothes was the kind of person who
is a curse to any well-regulated community. And
yet a play in which all these people did the right
thing, and went to bed at ten, and met their
mother-in-law with a clear conscience next day,
would he as dull as a bucket of mud in four acts,
and if Brough insisted on keeping it on he would
go bankrupt. In a perfect world there will be no
comedy, for the public will demand its money back
when it hears that Pontifex has been out all night
with Diggle in the house of a strange woman, and
that James can’t tell the square and honest truth
about the clothes. Which accounts for the deadly
dullness of people like Brunker, M.L.A. Brunker
would die of grief if any one proposed that he
should go on the tear with Diggle.

At. Sydney Tivoli there is a “New Aus-
tralian Anthem,” composed by Paul Pelham,
musieked by Harry Ingham, and sung by Howard
Chambers. _ It is the weary kind of twopenny
anthem which says “ Hail ” very often, and when
the vein of inspiration runs low it “ Hails ” some
more. Bar Cinquevalli, the gem of the show is
Lottie Moore’s whirly dance, and her song of how
the parson is in the cemet’ry, the coons are in the
corn, the mocking-bird is sleeping, the rabbits
ain’t a-peeping, an’ we won’t go home till morn.
Monday night’s audience expressed its approval
of Lottie by passing up two sugar-melons tied
up with red ribbons, one pie melon without
ribbons, nine bouquets, two huge baskets of
native flowers, and a toy-train marked in large
letters, “The George-street Train.” The giddy
Valdares whirl and flee and do fresh tricks on
their bicycles, and Cinquevalli does all the
curious things he has done before, and some
new ones.
* *■ *
The “Kelly Gang’’ boom goes on the
awfulness of its way at Sydney Criterion, and
booms in the presence of audiences whose size is
unaccountable. How this crude, illiterate mass
of blood aud hardware should have run more than
two nights passes imagination. The management
has lately issued a solemn warning to other
managers not to use the play without permission,
and Williamson hasn’t looked at all well since.
Also Brough is trying to wear the outward sem-
blance of a man who didn’t want to borrow the
“.Kelly Gang” anyhow, and Holloway conceals
his disappointment as well as he can. Likewise,
an injunction has been applied for by the Criterion
man against another man who is accused of want-
ing to do an unauthorised “ Kelly Gang ” at New-
castle. Perhaps the law will see its way to in-
junct both of them.
** * *
“ The War of Wealth ” is waged as usual
at Melbourne Royal, and the rush on the Antipo-
dean Bank promises to last till the end of Bland
Holt’s long season. One incident in that sordid
sprint for boodle testifies to the exceeding goodness
of Mrs. Bland Holt. A strawberry blonde widow in
weeds of woe notifies the exhausted bank that she
will have to go home and starve if she can’t cash
her little cheque, whereupon Mrs. Holt passes
£2OO across the counter and commands the cashier
to pay the pleading relict aforesaid. Widows and
orphans had better make a note of this incident
with a view to making a pathetic picture
at the bank counter in some future hour
of need, when Sydney confidence is shaken
and hairy pastoralists are clamoring for their oof.
Also, The Bulletin would advise elderly capital-
ists of amorous character to keep a sharp look out
for the next crisis that they may be ready to earn
the coy gratitude of weeded young females by
saving them from liquidation or reconstruction in
the event of the till running dry. It doesn’t
follow that the Lord loveth the cheerful giver in
such cases, but the widow is pretty sure to catch
on, anyway.

The Farley trio, who make music under
abnormal conditions, old friends Howard and St.
Clair, and clever little Fanny Powers, are latest
additions to the bill at the Bijou, which is a long,
strong bill with no bald spots on it. The Keziahs
and the rolling contortionist are going away this
week, if they haven’t already departed; but the
stranger who stands upside-down upon a ’cello to
play the violin, comes to much the same thing as
the acrobatic sisters on the shiny ladder.

M‘Adoo’s Georgia Minstrels are getting
along tunefully at St. George’s Hall, and the
majesty of the tall basso is regarded with oon-
siderable awe. Also, the strange philosophy of
the cake-walk has some charms for the multitude,
and the colored comedian who remarks that the
gallery applauds with more vigor than the ground-
floor is undoubtedly correct in his observation.
** * *
Meanwhile Melbourne’s musical culture
progresses as it never progressed before. Scherek’s
Orchestral Concerts don’t quite fill the Town Hall
on Saturday afternoons, but they attract a re-
munerative crowd every time, and clever Herr
Benno has gone so far as to give them a symphony
of the lighter kind with gratifying results. Italian
Opera will begin to try its luck at the Alexandra
next Saturday week, and Marshall Hall’s series of
six Thursday Orchestrals at the Athemeum Hall
opens to-night (17th). This concert will be for the
benefit of Miss Elsie Hall, who will take charge of
the piano in a Beethoven concerto, and all
admirers of that gentle artist are expected to boom
the occasion.
All the unkindest things that Sydney said
about “A Stranger in New York” will probably
be echoed in Melbourne this week. Hoyt’s
anemic comedy trilled its occasional lay at the
Princess’s on Saturday night, and a broad-grin-
ning public who had mostly backed victorious
gee-gees at Caulfield, made desperate efforts to
enjoy the show. To say that Saturday’s audience
grew pensive towards the finish is to record the
dire failure of “ A Stranger in New York.” Bad
luck waits on bad judgment, as a rule, in every
line of business, and inasmuch as the Hoyt-M‘Kee
Co. includes but two clever comedians, and only
three other people worth mentioning, the present
scribe is not going to wet the management’s dis-
comfiture with his tears, anyway. There is no
visible reason why a foolish and comparatively
inexpensive production should expect any
better support from the masses than a grocer
gets from his unsatisfactory tea and currants.
Takes the ladies of the co. They have presentable
legs in a few instances, but regarded a.> singers,
dancers, or divinities in a country wl,. re good
voices are cheap and the resources of th Firm’s
ballet are practically unlimited, they amount to a
misguided speculation. Who was responsible for
this shipment of coke to Newcastle ?
* * • *
The very best item in “A.5.1.N.Y.” is
Hugh Ward’s picture of an aged buck, who
quavers a characteristic ditty. Ward, as a gen-
tlemanly ruin with a senile fondness for deali
girls, is reminiscent of John Hare, the then young
“creator ” of Beau Farintosh and similar charac-
ters in the comedies that Tom Robertson wrote
for Mrs. Bancroft. Unfortunately Ward’s admir-
able old sinner gets no chance to shine after 8.30
p.m., or thereabouts. He subsides into a feeble
applauder of “ variety ” turns, and it is saddening
to see him pat his artistic hands together at the con-
clusion of a sick song which somebody has sung out
of tune. Most of the singing is out of tune,
but the Hoyt-M‘Kee Co. are a mutual admiration
society, and they encore one another religiously.
Conor gives one bright song with due effect, and
he is always brisk and funny in his grotesque,
pigeon-breasted style. The rest of the perform-
ance ranges from acceptable to fourth rate, also it
is frequently a mere repetition of the “Chinatown ”
performance. Moreover, it is loaded up with
comments on the roaring festive “time” that
everybody is supposed to be having at an invisible
French Ball, and what with the alleged fun that
never comes to pass, and the melancholy sugges-
tions of a “celebrated brilliant wit” and the
doleful spectacle of seven ladies imploring a bald-
headed man to tell them naughty stories, “ A
Stranger in New York ” seems almost too bad to
be true. You go away from the garish scene
under a vague impression that it was all a dream.
* • • •
“ Aletes ” discourses to The Bulletin on
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray” :
What an attraction “Tanqueray” seems to have had
for women ? Was in the gallery the last night of the
recent production. Found the place full to standing,
and the curious sex preponderating everywhere. Counted
carefully several rows to get an average, with result—9s
women, 48 men. What brought them ? Was it the pro-
blem, the intellect, or the emotion of the play? Let us
leave it to the man who thinks he understands the other
half.
It is a great play, who can gainsay it? What a terse,
yet restless, atmosphere pervades it 1 If there is any-
where in English letters, outside Shakespeare, a more
convincing tragedy, I would like to have it indicated.
For we have here the very essence of fine tragedy. The
gradual accumulation of deadly circumstance, its irre-
sistible progress and acceleration, its final relentless
triumph—the triumph of the past over the present, of
matter over mind. And the problem—what is it? Is it
more than a figment of the brain, a moral abstraction, a
bumping over mental ruts that thousands of years have
grooved? “ There is nothing either good or "bad,” says
Hamlet, “ but thinking makes it so.” So is it thinking
that gives this problem its palpable horror. Nature
builds a woman with a big heart and not too big a brain,
endows her with the vitality of three ordinary women, and
sets her in the world—the world does the rest. But Nature
could have saved that woman, notwithstanding. The
smile, the voice, the soft clinging arms of a child—what
a rampart round a woman's soul! But how wanton this
self-same Nature often is. Humanity cannot break her
laws with impunity. Yet occasionally she evades them
herself, and it is still humanity that suffers. Thelaiter
is for ever crudely striking the chords of life, and if the
keynote is missing, malevolently withheld, that would
have resolved them into harmony, jangling discords
alone ensue, and the problem iooins ahead. So whatever
there he in it of sin, or shame, or despair, there stands
with the world the other guilty partner of the deed—
Nature, Providence, God, name it how you will.
Evolution is but the s'tory of infinite" trials and errors
with a never-failing modicum of success. Time is for
ever wiping out the blunders ; the success is permanent.
This problem will probably go in its own good season ; it
belongs only to a passing phase of Life. But—and herein
lies the revenge of the Eternal—the higher complexity,
to which the human machine is ever attaining, will
develop a keener capacity for suffering, and Lhe oppor-
tunity will not be lacking. The pain of the world is for
ever shifting its nature and Its locale, but ft never leaves
us. This is the Problem of Life itself, before which we
are helpless. But this at least is clear, that our duty has
been, is, and ever will bo—to cultivate the divine flower
ol chanty lu our hearts.
** * *
Iwo letters concerning Amy Castles ap-
peared in last Saturday’s Age. One bore the
name of Atny s father, Joe Castles, and the writer
—who may have been the said Joseph, or some-
body else had the amusing “cheek” to begin
thus: “The gentlemen who acted as hon. com-
mittee of the late concerts will no doubt be grati-
fied to hear that my daughter has now the oppor-
tunity of cultivating her vocal talent.” After
which the alleged Joseph went on to return thanks
for the enthusiastic services of Rev. Father
Robinson, who has graciously consented to let
Amy give a farewell concert—one more last fare-
well—towards the choir funds of St. Francis’s
Church. The Other letter stated some good hard
facts re the Castles boom. “More than three
months ago,” wrote “Bendigonian,” “anumberof
subscriptions were received, and the public took
tickets for Miss Castles’ benefit concerts on the
understanding that they were assisting in a laud-
able object, which has long since been achieved.
A few weeks ago the excuse was given when her
advisers were first approached with a view of her
assisting Master Fritz Mueller in his concert, that
her voice would not stand the strain. What do
we find, however? Simply that she gives con-
certs in Sydney, Adelaide, Brisbane, and will
give, for aught one knows, more concerts in Syd-
ney and Melbourne. This, apparently, is about
the best possible way of ruining the young lady’s
voice. . . . Surely when funds are publicly ob-
tained for a specific purpose the contributors have
a right to demand that good faith be observed.
The promoters having asked for public sympathy
and invited gratuitous assistance from our musi-
cians, it is only a fair and reasonable request that
this mad money-making policy be immediately
dropped.” But why call it a mad policy ? The
Castles boom has been a perfectly sane transaction
from the start, and further evidences of its sanity
may be expected to crop up at any moment.
At Poverty Point.
Celia Datnpier, the bouneinsr, short-frocked, 15 or 16
year-old violinist (from Maorilaml), who sailed for
Europe t’other day, is possibly going to make a big
name hereafter. Celia, for all that her present skill and
intelligence can suggest to the contrary, may even
develope into the finest lady violinist on earth. Her per-
formances in Melbourne made a number of older young
aspirants look very amateurish ; yet it is too early to say
whether she can ever be more than a brilliant mechanist.
There is little feeling expressed in her music thus far.
“Jack Shay”: Went to see the Cake-Walkers, the
other night, and heard one pushite comment to another :
“ Wot guys ! Like bloomin’ goals ! Fancy blokes making
such a show of 'emselves 1 ” The thing gave me an idea :
Fancy a manager taking to Kurope and America a troupe
of “push” blokes in velvet-faced, big-buttoned, bob-
tailed coats, bell-bottomed pants, red-morocco-trimmed
boots and plug hats, with a corresponding allowance of
“ tarts.” They might do a set of push “ lancers ” and a
“ cat’s ” waltzing match up and down a 12-inch plank -
the dancers perfectly rigid with humped backs and grasp-
ing eac h othercollar-aud-elbow style. It ought to “fetch”
the English costers.
“C.C." : To the average woman Mrs. Tanqueray is
simply a lost sheep who had a lovely opportunity given
her of re-entering the sheep-fold of respectability, and
threw it away for want of a little horse-sense; to the
average man she is a glorious figure of the Fallen Woman
who made a tremendous effort to be good, and died be-
cause the Fates were too strong for her. “ Man is fine,
woman superfine,” as the old nursery-game has it, yet
how is it that quite a number of “nice” women think
Paula a fool for giving the Captain away, while the
average Bad Man shudders at the idea of her doing any-
thing else ? Similarly, when the Myra Kemble co. played
“ Dr. Bill ” in Dunedin with a wealth of innuendo foreign
to its revival here, it was the men who declared it unfit
for the Frenchiest of French stages, while the women all
said it was just awfully nice. Is it that women’s instincts
are really less refined than supposed ? Did it require that
Paula should lead the man’s life to acquire the man’s in-
stinctive sense of what is impossible? What would Mrs.
Oortelyon have done in her place? Let things slide?
A gushing musical crank gets these few remarks off his
weskit : —“The people of Bendigo, where Amy Castles
comes from, got a sudden and delightful shock when the
wonderful Flora Batson, after warbling a jet-like soprano,
threw her head back and trolled out in a light baritone
‘My Old Kentucky Home, Good-night.’ Miss Batson did
the same thing at the Sydney Palace the last night she
sang there. It was something to be remembered. The
auditorium was draped in shadow ; a single shaft of
limelight streamed down on tin Led panel and silken
curtain ; and then Flora glided into the light, looked up
with a winsome smile, and sent her soaring soprano right-
up among the Arabian arches. She was answered from
the darkness of a box by Gerard Millar, and her beautiful
voice rang on like a bell ahove the boom of the surf.
When she ceased, and the storm of applause had subsided,
she floated into the light again. It turned crimson ; the
gold circlpt round her neck gleamed like a ring of ruby
flame ; and she stood in the crimson light and sang in

In “The IMly Gang,” at Sydney Cri., the Jerilderie
bank-manager is pourtrayed as a parsonieal person in a
frock coat, and he thus soliloquises: “ I wish I had got
that gold-dust, away out of the place. I’ve bought over
£5OOO worth this month." Going to Sheol for ice is not
in it with going to Jerilderie for gold-dust. If he said
that he’d bought £5OOO worth of wool and was keeping it
in the office-safe, and he hoped the Kellvs wouldn’t come
along with a portmtnteau and embezzle the lot before
next wool-escort left, it would put in a bit of convincing
local color, which would even appeal to the cockney Aus-
tralians of Sydney.
Adelaide Bowring, who, with her husband J. B. Steele,
came out to Australia in '66 under engagement to George
Coppin to support G. V. Brooke, lately died in England.
Steele and wife reached Melh. in the ship Western
Ocean, sailing some time before the ill-fated London,
in which Brooke was voyaging. They only heard of the
loss of the London and Brooke when the pilot boarded the
Western Ocean near Cape Otway. To guard against loss,
George Coppin insured the lives of Mr. and Mrs. Steele.
It is not known if he took the same precaution with
Brooke.

Mephan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc.,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is one of I. and E. Hall's patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

The Wilfley concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. This machine has a capacity of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt concentrator made.
It can he easily and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is £115.

* * * *
Unity, of Bendigo, ought to be among the most
substantial of that district’s div.-payers shortly—within,
say, four months. For some time past they have been
opening up a big and fairly rich formation at 2061ft., and
have enough blocked out to keep the stampers going for
months. The reef, if it is one, is of unusual size for
Bendigo; but as the gold is unquestionably there it
doesn’t much matter about the name. The interval
named will he used to make connections, &c., for more
economical working.

At an Associated G.M. shareholders’meeting in
London, to empower the directors to deal with 50,000
unissued shares, the chairman said they had already spent
about £100,000 in machinery, and it would take another
£30,000 to complete the new sulphide plant, which, in
full working order, would treat 400 tons sulphide per day.
The mills to treat oxidised ores would ileal with about
140 tons daily. It was intended, if possible, to maintain
an average value in each case. That of sulphide had not
been determined yet—it would probably be 2oz. The
average value of the oxidised ore—loz.—would be main-
tained. The anticipated production was exclusive of
tellnride ores sent to various smelting-works. These
would bring the total output up to 38,000oz. per month,
which, at £4 per oz., would make a monthly return of
about £159,000. A dazzling prediction, indeed. Over
a ton of solid £4-an-oz. gold monthly 1

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Premier Turner reports that the Victorian
revenue for the year ending June, 1899, after all adjust-
ments were made, amounted to £7,378,842. Expenditure,
so far as is known, £7,027,415, though there are still
possibilities that it may prove to he from £lO,OOO to
£20,000 less when all the accounts are in. Surplus
£351,427, with the possibility that it may ultimately get
up to 1370,000. Victoria has the largest increase of
revenue in Australasia this year, and the largest surplus.
Out of the latter £lOO,OOO is to be set aside for public
works ; £50,000 towards a sinking fund to provide for the
redemption of a £250,000 loan which falls due in 1903 ;
and £200,000 in reduction of the Great Deficit—which will
bring it down to £2,316,196. Considering that the de-
ficit got as high as £3,178,709, three or four years
ago, this is a big reduction. Victoria at one time
was supposed to have the biggest accumulated shortage
in Australia, hut N.S. Wales (which had a shortage at
30th June, 1898, of £3,098,590, since reduced probably to
about £2,948,590) now makes the worst showing in this
depressing respect. But Turner, after tolling through
much adversity, implores Victoria in general not to go on
the tear at the first sign of prosperity and not to assume
too much. His revenue was unexpectedly large last
year, partly because many rich men pegged out, and he
sadly remarks that these rich men can’t die over again
this year. Besides, the demand for reduced railway
freights and for a small reduction of the income-tax has
become almost irresistible. The maximum income-tax is
to be cut down from Is. 4d. in the £ lo Is. 2d. and a similar
reduction of one-eighth made all round, and freights on
agricultural produce are to be reduced by £55,009. So
Turner estimates next year’s revenue at £7,156,225 —
£222,617 less than that of the year just ended ; and ex-
penditure at £109,340 increase ; and assumes the surplus
at 30th June, 1899, to be only £19,470.
« * * *
Apropos of the mystery of the Special Assets
Trust Co. (Limited) —that branch of the Commercial
Bank of Australia in which all the worst assets were put
some time ago— The Bulletin accidentally comes across
a copy of the “Statement of Assets and Liabilities on
30th June, 1899.” It sets forth that the liabilities to
depositors are 63,443,909, and other liabilities £lO0 —
total, £3,444,009. Also that the assets (of which only
£14,080 are cash) are worth on paper £4,261,441, leaving
asurplus of £817,432. But there is no income and ex-
penditure account, and no hint or suggestion as to
whether the £4,261,441 of assets and alleged assets earn
enough to pay interest on the nearly 3) millions of
deposits; whether the shortage, if there is one, is covered
by the £30,000 per half-year which the Commercial
Bank has lately heen paying to the Trust; whether
the shortage is so large that it is swallowing up,
or not swallowing up, all the profits of the visible part of
the Commercial Bank that remain after paying the
preference div. ; and, consequently, whether the com-
bined institution (the Commercial Bank of Australia and
the Assets Trust Co. are really one, to all intents and pur-
poses, because the bank shareholders are liable for every
penny of the Trust’s shortage, if there is one, just as
much as if it was the hank’s own shortage) is prospering
greatly or going downhill, in a dangerous fashion. It
doesn’t matter much that the assets of the Trust are
worth, on paper, £817,432 more than its liabilities, if these
assets don’t earn enough to pay interest on the liabilities;
if there is a large and reliable shortage in the income,
then things are bad, despite the paper surplus. Audit
wouldn't matter much though the assets on paper were
not equal to the liabilities if these assets regularly earned
more in cash than the outgoings; the surplus in-
come and the" sum which the Commercial Bank hands
over half-yearly would.inake good the deficit in time.
At the Commercial Bank’s annual meeting the
Chairman referred to the demands—which have been
mostly The Bulletin’s demands—for information about the
Assets Trust, and said that “ he, for one, would not take
the responsibility of valuing these assets.” Well, he and
his fellow-directors of the Trust have taken it, in a fashion ;
they put down these assets in the Trust’s statement at
£4,261,441. But, as it happens, this paper for one didn’t
ask him to “value these assets,” and Chairman Harper’s
indignant assertion that he couldn’t do what wasn't asked
is quite irrelevant. The board has further stated on
various occasions that it can’t accept responsibility for its
predecessors’ more or less unlucky investments which are
now buried in the Trust—but no one asked or expected
that it should. There was merely an inquiry, on
behalf of the shareholders and public, as to whether
these unlucky investments were paying their way.
The chairman also remarked that “ certain kinds of
partial information would not be beneficial to the share-
holders,” wherefore one naturally asks : Why does he
insist on giving only partial information ? He also denied
flatly that the statement of the Assets Trust was kept
secret, but as the statement gives no information about
the income, expenditure, loss or profit, it might as well
be kept secret. Also, he remarked effusively that for
two years back the bank had made £105,000 a year over
and above all expenses. Which is quite true ; that part
of the Commercial Bank which is called “ the bank ” did
do so, as far as is known. But dividends on preference
shares have absorbed £40,000, and if there is a deficit
in the Assets Trust’s income which has more than
eaten up the remainder the bank shareholders might like
to know of it. The Trust Co. belongs to them, and the
defieit, if there is one, is their deficit. The question is
simply this : Have the Commercial Bank shareholders a
right to know what their net profit is, or have they not?
And if not, why haven’t they ?

There is nothing in particular to be said about
the report of the old Victoria Life and General Insurance
Co. This co. does no new business now, and just waits
patiently for the last insured and the last annuitant to
die out. During the year to 30th June, 1899, £10,457 was
paid in claims, so the insureds are dropping off reasonably
fast. The co. pays a dividend of 15 per cent. (£6000), as
compared with 12) per cent. (£5000) last year, and the
accumulated funds are diminished from £318,381 a year
ago to £314,320. Considering that the Victoria Life" Co.
does no new business—and new business is the kind that
costs money—the expenses seem very high. They run to
fully 33 per cent, of premiums, or over 10 per cent, of total
income. The Victoria Life Co. has nothing to do now but
keep an eye on its pile and die respectably, and this
seems a rather expensive death.. Also, seeing , .hat
£356,000 worth of actual investments yield only £10,447,
or £2 18s. Bd. percent, per annum all round, the expen-
sive eye which is kept on the funds might produce better
results. The fixed deposits, Maori land State Debentures,
Victorian Government Stock, Melbourne Board of Works
Debentures, and loans on policies, must surely average 3)
percent. And jf they do, then thp inference is that the
£159,9.85 of “mortgages and other securities” and
£24,635 of “ properties ” only yield about 2) per cent.
Are the mortgages uot feeling well just now ?
* * * *
The Victoria Insurance Co. issues, for the year
ended 30th June, 1899, the first respectable and decent
and perfectly sane balance-sheet it has published for a
long while. The following statement of the doings of this
once great but (of late years) woefully mismanaged co.
will explain the writer’s meaning ;
Profit. Dividend.
June, 1893 .. £34,054 44f per cent. = £22,468
„ 1894 .. 24,400 „ „ = 22,468
„ 1895 .. 20,741 40) „ = 20,221
„ 1896 .. 17,058 35f „ = 17,974
„ 1897 .. 18,010 „ „ = 17,974
Loss.
„ 1898 .. 23,336 Nil.
, Profit.
„ 1899 ~ 14,597 about 9 „ =. 4,494
Profits .. .. £105,554 Dividends .. .. £105,599
During the period 1893 to 1897 it was well known that the
assets of the Victoria Insurance Co. had depreciated
greatly. The situation became so bad that a footnote
began to appear in the co.’s balance-sheets to the effect
that the assets were, taken at book value—which was
practically the same as saying that their values were
partly bogus. Yet the co. continued to pay away practi-
cally all its profits (in 1806 it actually paid away more
than its profits) in stupendous 35) and 40.) and 44) per
cent, dividends, and made no serious attempt at provid-
ing for depreciation out of current earnings. In 1897,
while paying a 35) per cent, dividend, it broke in on its
reserve fund to partially provide for the depreciation.
In 1898 the great slump had to he faced at last. The
whole contingency fund of £25,000 : the accumulated
profit balance of £10,174 and £8162 of the re-insurance
fund were written off at one swoop. Depreciation to the
amount of £20,000, which had been kept dark while the
40 per cent., dividends were being paid, was admitted and
the reserves went down all at once from £BO,OOO to about
£36,000.
The improvement this year lies in the fact that
the Victoria Insurance Co. has at last cut down its divi-
dend to about 9 per cent., and carries forward a balance
of £10,103. If it keeps its dividends down to 9 per cent,
for the next seven or eight years, and no unusual disaster
strikes it, it will be a big', strong concern again ; if it gets
back to the 44) per cent, insanity, it won’t. Everything
depends on whether the management is clothed and in its
right commercial intellect again. It would have been a
still better sign if £10,001) of the balance had been
put at once to reserve lest the management should
feel like blewing it next year. The Victoria is a
very small affair compared with what it used to be ; as
an evidence, the year’s income from investments is £2445,
whereas it was £5376 in 1894, which shows a grievous
melting away of the assets. The Australian Mutur.], of
Sydney—which was a poor, paltry concern in the old days
of the Victoria’s greatness, has now more than twice its re-
serves. Still the Victoria has a magnificent business (the
year’s premium income reached £127,798), and it may
easily be the strongest fire company in Australia again in
a few years if the management doesn’t fly off the handle
any more.

The Victoria General Insurance and Guarantee
Co. reports that its profit for the year ended 30th June,
1899, was £4511, and it pays a dividend of 15 per cent.,
which absorbs £3OOO. Some recent doings of the
Victoria General are as follows :
Profits. Dividends.
June, 1593 .. £6283 15 per cent. = £3OOO
„ 1894 ... 6477 20 „ = 4000
„ 1895 ... 5629 20 „ 4000
„ 1896 ... 4987 25 „ «* 6000
„ 1897 ... 3689 20 „ 4000
Loss.
„ 1893 ... 5011 10 „ ■ 2000
Profit.
„ 1899 ... 4511 15 „ ■ 3000
Profits £26,565 Dividends ... £25,000
During three years out of the last seven this co. paid
away more than its earnings—once in the frantic effort
to pay a 25 per cent, dividend ; once in order to pay
20 per cent.; and the third time it paid 10
percent., and thereby went over £7OOO to the bad in a
single year. Apparently the frenzy after risky and sensa-
tional dividends is common to both the Victoria com-
panies, which is probably accounted for by the fact that
they are under the same management. The Victoria
General Co. also admits (just as the Victoria used to do)
that its assets are not quite what they seem ; the second
largest of them is “Loans on Mortgage at Book Value
(depreciation allowed for in Contingency Account)
£11,630.” This seems to suggest that the contingency
account of £4OOO is already partly or wholly swallowed up
by losses, and is therefore either partly or wholly a mere
paper reserve. It also suggests that, though the Victoria
General professes to have earned in the last seven years
£1565 more than it has spent, it has really been going to
the bad, when depreciation of assets is allowed for. The
Victoria General has £20,000 paid-up capital, a miserably
humble reserve-fund of £5OOO, and £3005 profit balance
(total reserves, £8005), together with whatever is left, if
anything is left, of its contingency fund. At June,
1893, it had £5OOO reserve and 18722 profit balance (total
£13,722). Its premium income this year is £11,692 ;in
1893 it was £12,702. This year its income from invest-
ments is £913 ;in 1893 it was £1854. Its profits are
£l7OO less than they were in 1892. So, altogether, The
Bulletin feels justified in calling it a small, weak, and
recklessly-managed institution, which is gradually fooling
away a very fine opportunity.

The very remarkable Montgomerie’s Brewery
Co., (Melbourne) has decided to end its heart-breaking
career in liquidation. Considering some of its recent
doings that is, probably, the only way out. A few past
records of the co. have heen as follows :
1892 Profit... ~£7679 1896 Loss ... ..£4147
1893 „ .. .. 3461 1897 „ .. .. 6041
1894 ~ .. .. 1160 1898 „ .. .. 4194
1895 Loss .. .. 1611
The loss for the first half of ’99 is said to have been only
£l6OO, so things were really coming round—only they
came round too late. The co. was an inflated affair from
the first. It paid £25,001) for the goodwill of the awful
business, and when the bad days began it was paying in-
terest on an overdraft of £78,U00 incurred in spreading
the business out too far, and securing hotels, many of
which were only a millstone round the neck of the luck-
less concern. It would have been a paying concern all
along but for the spreading out and the overdraft. The
visible losses are considerable, but the rotting away of
the assets is the worst trouble. The bank that holds that
overdraft will probably be sorry now.

B.H. South, which has paid £20,000 in divs.
during the six months, has made only £12,690, even
when credit is taken for £13,304, which is the amount
realised for surpluses in respect of former shipments
and sales. It began the Half year with £9391 cash
in hand ;it closed the term with £4B. The directors’ re-
port is painfully scanty ; the only reference to finance is
this : “ The sum of £3969 2s. Id. has been expended in
necessary development work, £20,000 paid in dividends,
and £3620 7s. lOcl. spent on plant and buildings.” Not a
word to show that only a part of the £20,000 was earned
and that the £3620 is not charged against the half-year’s
work—though it is fair to say that the South Co. always
makes a reasonable deduction for depreciation. Two
causes have heen at work making for poor results—neces-
sary alterations of,machinery and poorer and harder ore.
The latter was, however, by far the more important. This
and the preceding haL-year compare as follows :
Ore treated. Value.
ToDec.,’9S .. 42,845 tons .. £57,627
To June,’99 .. 43,150 tons .. £51,691
It is expected that recent alterations in plant will make
some difference ; but it is not likely to be much—as far as
can he seen, they will no more than counterbalance the
continued deterioration of the ore. Still, manager Green-
way expects to make some improvements not yet an-
nounced ; and the directors say :—“ The process of zinc
extraction may be said to have advanced beyond the ex-
perimental stage, and your directors will, it is expected, be
called on shortly to consider the erection of plant for
treating their accumulations of zinciferous material.”
With that rather cold comfort and the prospect of 6d.
divs. quarterly instead of shilling ones, shareholders
must be as content as shareholders reasonably can be in
such circs.
• • • •
B.H. South does not give its shareholders any
figures showing the assay values of ore ami concentrates
respectively ; and from the report no estimate can be
made of the quality of the concentrating work. But of a
recent week it is published that 1777 tons ore was mined,
worth 26'3 per cent, lead, 7'7oz. silver, ami 16 6 per cent,
zinc, and that the concentrates produced were 408 tons,
assaying 64'3 per cent, lead, 12 6 oz. silver, and 7'9 per
cent. zinc. The metal contents of the ore was thus 468
tons lead, 13,6320 z. silver, and 295 tons zinc. The concen-
trates contained 274 tons lead, 51410 z. silver, and 32 tons
zinc ; while the tailings, slimes, &c., contained 194 tons
lead, 8541 oz. silver, and 263 tons zinc. A small quantity
of slimes, &c.,is re-treated ; from these in this particular
week 68 tons was saved, assaying 48'2 per cent, lead,
9'3oz. silver, and 13'6 per cent, zinc—a product of very
little market value, owing to the high percentage of zinc.
Dismissing this product, therefore, and referring to con-
centrates proper, it is found that the process saves 274
tons lead and fails to save 194 tons, saves 51410 z. silver
and fails to save 85410 z., eliminates 263 tons zinc and fails
to eliminate 32 tons. Yet the South has been concen-
trating longer than any other B.H. mine, and it has the
advantage of having as its metallurgist one of the most
painstaking and experienced and energetic men on the
Barrier. The figures indicate what a makeshift concen-
trating is after all ; though it ought to be added that,
apparently, the South's ore is unusually hard and refrac-
tory.

other mine—so hard that it speedily grooves the toughest
steel rollers. Yet here is the cost per ton of mining
there, including breaking, trucking, hauling, timbering,
and management, along with the quantity broken, for the
half-years ending on the dates named :

Modifications in the system of mining are responsible for
some of the reductions (in some mines the cost used to be

good deal, too. Still, the South is by no means the
miners’ worst mine.
* * * *
Broken Hill co.’s now have 7800 employes, of
whom 5860 are on the mines, 1300 at Port Pirie works,
and others at smelters elsewhere—Port Adelaide and
Cockle Creek mostly. B.H. Prop, heads the list with
2900 hands; the Central (Sulphide Corporation) and
Block 14, about 1500 and 1050 respectively. The weekly
output of ore averages just under 26,000 tons, of which
B.H. Prop, contributes 8300 tons.
* * * *
Just when the Broken Hill mining co’s. and
open-cut contractors are advertising in every capital for
able-bodied men, the district Ms.P. beg help from the
Govt, for local unemployed. As a matter of fact, there
are not many unemployed, but they are persistent. Pro-
bably half of them are men who, for some past offence, or
supposed offence, are blacklisted at the mines ; the
others can’t stand the incessant racket of mine labor, and
there is no other local industry to mop them up. That
there is a black-list still is quite certain ; and the most
odious feature about it is that a man doesn’t know that
his name is on it until, after weary months of fruitless
searching for work from one mine to another, the truth
forces itself on him. Then if he is wise he clears ; the
White Cliffs opal-fields and the Westralian mines have
absorbed scores of them. And a fact worth noting is
that managers, leaving Broken Hill for other parts, have
there given some of these blacklisted men the first chance
of a job.
• * • •
The Bulletin office often lias a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
lienee certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents —
owners will be advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not be
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.
* • • *
The very cheekiest thing The Bulletin has
met with in the N.S.W. dredging racket up to date is the
prospectus of the Little River Gold Dredging Co. (Braid-
wood, N.S.W.). It is in 48,000 shares of 10s. each; and
16,000 of them, fully paid up, are to he allotted to the
vendor in payment for “the applications and interests
herinafterdescribed.” The vendor, apparently, when the
co. was floated, hadn’t an inch of dredging claim to Sell,
but he had applied for a great lot of land, and his chance
of getting the laud, or part of it, or none of it, is sold to
the co. for 16,000 paid-up shares. The co., apparently, is
even to pay the cost of the application, for among the
expenses it is to meet are “certain payments made.and
due to the Crown ” —amount not specified. The shares, it
is said, have been largely over-applied for, and if that is
so the vendor would prohably have no difficulty in selling
his 16,000 paid-up shares at once if he wanted to. If the
boom is as vigorous as it is alleged to be, the vendor, it
would seem, can get his money whether there is any lease
granted or not. And supposing he did sell them, and
then the leases applied for were refused—there is, appar-
ently, no possible evidence that they won’t be! Then the
vendor would have, perhaps, £BOOO, and the co. would be
out of a great deal of money, and the only dredging area it
would have to dredge in would be the area its advt. takes
up in the papers. The man who sells the lease of an
unproved dredging area to a co. for a large sum was bad
enough. The man who sells the fact that he is asking for
a lease is worse. The only thing wanting now is the pros-
pectus of the man who wants to sell to a co. the fact that
he is thinking about asking for a lease when he gets fully
rested. The Little River Co.’s prospectus, by the way,
enumerates certain agreements that have been made, anil
says “it is believed the above are the only contracts the
dates of and parties to which are required by sec. 48 of the
No-Liability Mining Co.’s Act of 1896 to be specified in this
prospectus ; but, in order to prevent any question, appli-
cants .for shores shall be deemed to waive any further
compliance with that enactment.” Which off-hand way
of abolishing the Act seems to make it just possible that
the shareholders may find themselves hampered by some
very surprising contracts of which they, at present, know
nothing. Anyhow, they agree, it would appear, not to
complain, whatever contracts may turn up.

Tmon River dredge (N.S.W.); More explana-
tions given for not cleaning-up. Public now told that it
would only represent a fortnight’s work or so, though the
dredges have been operating more than two months. No
excuse whatever given for the market-value of this pros-
pective venture (which “ is bound to see £20”), and no
reason mentioned for the very heavy market-fall of last
week. All the Sydney newspapers seem to be barracking
lustily for the show. There is a mystery somewhere, and
the eiean-up will eventually be a sensation—of some sort.

From Col>ar, the chief gold-copper camp of
N.S.W. ; Occidental, 2240 z. for a three weeks’run of 20
stamps—probably representing a shade over 4dwta. ;
cleaning up cyanide plant this week. Probable total gold
value £2OOO, of which fully half profit. Old tailings run-
ning about 2)dwts., more than half of which is profit;
still at least 30,000 tons to treat. With plenty wafer
this co. (which has just much increased its dain-cupacity)
could easily pay 2s. Od. a year in divs.
Great Western.—After one small attempt at
decent management this co. has gone back to its miser-
able ways—sunk a main shaft within 80ft. of the ore-
channel and then abandoned it to return into the old
shaft which is as crooked as a ram’s horn, and renew the
old system of gutting and dragging out the good streak
of ore by the hair of the head. The new little tinpot
cyanide plant is also in keeping with things generally.
Still another expert has been up. examined and no doubt
admired the working of all other local mines save the
G.W., which is most of the time under offer, but, alas I
never sold.
The Young Australia is getting into fair work-
ing shape. Has a good main shaft, and is equipped with
winding, batr.eiy, and cyanide plant. Important develop-,
nient made a few weeks ago, and that of last week estab-
lishes at least 12 months’ work on an ore-body which will
be taken for a width of over 20ft. Some phenomenal
assays have been mentioned, but even tin-owing these aside
the outlook for divs. is very bright.
Cobar Chesney: Main shaft being cut down to
bottom level, 560 ft. ; will take about five weeks to com-
plete. Levels run out connected with winzes, and every-
thing ready to maintain an output of 300 tons a day if
needed. All the plant on the ground, but it will be fully
three months ere it is running ; when complete, it will be
probably the most compact copper-concentrating plant in
Australia. Co. will have spent over £30,000 before it pro-
duces a ton of copper, but none of it seems to have been
wasted, and it is going to mine in a big way.
Cobar Gold Alines is now equipped with the
finest milling and cyanide plant in N.S.W.—in fact, there
is no other mine within streets of it. The cyanide plant
is the biggest in Australia ; it was designed by Butters,
one of the big Rand cyanide.men, and has costover
£lO,OOO ; over £2OOO was expended in the riveting of the
vats, All the other additions to the 100-head stamp mill
are in keeping—the electric-lighting plant, air-com-
pressor, winding-engine, rock-breakers, &.c. It is quite
easy to see where the £30,000 represented by these addi-
tions has gone. Another call of £lO,OOO just made, and
the local shareholders are responding to a man. The
mine is ready to supply 300 tons a day, and a start will
be marie at the beginning of Sept.; it will probably be
long ere another stoppage occurs. The battery is in the
wrong place, it is true, and but for this serious mistake
the whole outfit would be an object-lesson in mining
equipment.

The unaccountable girl who makes pro-
miscuous charges against men is an every-day
occurrence ; the unaccountable man also turns up
occasionally. Miles, the Ashfield (N.S.W.) sig-
nalman, who planted an obstruction on the line
in front of the Melbourne express, and then
rushed to the station with a story about having
surprised an unknown man in a fiendish device to
wreck the train, is a very ordinary and harmless,
specimen of the tribe. He didn’t lay a plot,
against anybody in particular; by his own admis-
sion he built up his little scrap of fiction “ for
notoriety.” In his own small way he wanted to.
be some kind of a hero and get his name in the
papers. Most likely he felt he could be a decent
hero if he had the chance. He never got an oppor-
tunity to save any one from drowning, for people
don’t drown on the railway-line. He hadn’t the
luck to rush nobly out of a burning building
with his hair on fire and a saved girl under his
arm, and, not being at sea, he had no show to
strike a noble attitude on the sloppy deck as the
ship went down. When the yearning to excite
admiration comes over a rich man very badly, he
sends a large cheque to the hospital; but a poor
man who isn’t a cricketer, or a pug., and who can’t
write an open letter to John Norton, must either
wait patiently for his chance, or make it himself.
Miles (after 16 years of apparently faithful ser-
vice) made it in a small, foolish way by putting a
bit of iron on the rails and then rushing to save
the lives of the passengers on the express by giving
notice of the danger. He meant no harm, and did
no real injury ; all he wanted was to be a bene-
factor, and be recognised as such. If he had re-
sisted the temptation he n ight, in time, have so
completely got over the desire to be a benefactor
that he could have watched all his relatives drown-
ing without any excitement. But he gave way to it.
The Forrest Govt, is the only one in Aus-
tralia that has appointed a commission for Paris
Exhibition. Other less reckless Governments
wisely hold aloof, the frightfully extortionate
price demanded for floor-space proving an insuper-
able objection. Venn, M.L.A., a pompous indi-
vidual, married into one of the Six Families, is
president of the W.A. Commission. He formerly
occupied a seat in Forrest’s Ministry, but was
kicked out “m his nightshirt” for insubordina-
tion. He is intensely vain, but very wealthy, and
the astute Premier created the Commission for
him. Haekett, proprietor of the West Aus-
tralian, who is Forrest’s boss in all things, is a
member of the Commission, which also includes
little Illingworth and Morgans. Haekett attends
more functions than any other half-dozen men in
the country. He is member of parks and gardens,
museums, hospitals, public libraries, and water-
works boards, of select-committees galore, is great,
high deputy past panjandrum of all the Free-
masons, and rarely misses any dinner, reception,
concert or anything that happens to be going on.
How he manages it is a profound mystery. Some
say there are two Hacketts.
Coolgardie Exhibition has cost the Forrest
Govt, over <£17,000 to date. At a recent meeting
of the self-styled commissioners, they voted them-
selves gold medals, and as there are about 60 of
them, and the medals will cost (say) a fiver apiece,
the bill will be still further swelled. Exhibitors
who assisted the show by displays of valuable
machinery receive diplomas only. Begun in folly
it ended in shame —opened with champagne and
closed with “shypoo.”
• » * *
Victoria spends on charity £193,294 annu-
ally, of which the Govt, contributes <£92,470 (in-
cluding .£46,000 for reformatory schools). Of the
whole, no less than <£41,803, or nearly 22
per cent., goes in secretaries’ wages and collectors’
commissions. Women appear to be far the best
managers, the local ladies’ benevolent socie-
ties running their charities at an average cost of
2\ per cent. only. Govt, reformatories are run at
about 9Jj per cent. ; free dispensaries at 62 per
cent. ; and one awful institution —the Royal
Humane Society- expended «£66 15s. 6d. in chari-
ties, while the secretary’s salary mopped up £250
per annum. Many of these alleged charities are
simply intended to provide billets for those run-
ning them. About time the Govts, of all the
States took on their own shoulders the burdens
they have so long and so cheerfully dodged—the
providing for the sick and the poor and the orphan
and the widow.
* * * *
N.S.W. Colonial Secretary Brunker’s
effort to make sons and daughters support their
indigent parents promises to be a somewhat drastic
measure. If one member of a family supports an
indigent parent, provision will be made enabling
that member to sue his brothers and sisters for
proportional shares. But in all cases the law will
only be put in motion against children having a
superfluity after providing for the maintenance of
themselves and families. A son will not be
excused from payment by pleading that he has
offered to take his parents into his own house.
m m m m
N.S.W. Minister Hogue, instead of cast-
ing about for pretty phrases of his own make for
the suppression - of-juvenile-pauper Bill, over
which he says he is burning midnight oil, should
ask the assent of Parliament to the main clause of
the English Prevention of Cruelty to Children
Act of ’B9. This Act renders liable to arrest,
without a warrant, “any person who causes or
procures any child under 16 to be in any street for
the purpose of begging or receiving alms, or of
inducing the giving of alms, whether under the
pretence of singing, playing, performing, offering
anything for sale, or otherwise.” This would clear
the crowds of youngsters from Sydney streets —by
netting the parties who use them. If there be
■children there without motion from adults in
hiding they can be picked up under a section of
■existing N.S.W. law.
* * • •
A curious specimen of the vag. species
lately turned up at Brunswick (Vic.) Police Court.
He was a wild, unkempt individual, and in
evidence it came out that not only did he abstain
from work but he insisted on sharing the meagre
allowance his aged mother received from the
Benevolent Society. Then some one got the
ancient lady admitted into a charitable institution
and the unfortunate son was thrown on his beam-
ends and duly dragged before the court. The
■court in indignation rose up and heaved 12 months
at him, and instead of falling down dead under the
weight of it this nonchalant vag. only remarked :
“Ah ! Twelve months ! Thanks. What’s to
become of my furniture ? ”
* * * •
One Australian potentate who holds many
■distinguished offices, and pretends to fine manners,
■culture, and Chesterfieldian courtesy, is rather
thinly veneered. Any day of the week he may
be seen industriously manicuring his finger-nails
while counsel is addressing him. For this pur-
pose, indeed, he carries about with him a regular
set of mother-of-pearl-handled instruments, and
■one or other of these is always in use. Even
when in the chair at a very impressive recent full-
dress function he could not leave his nails alone.
He appeared in gorgeous millinery, and during
the principal address sat beside the Governor,
busily paring and filing his nails.

A. Dent, on
teeth
Compare the
teeth of various
children, and try
to account for the
difference. Arti-
ficial teeth for
Australian chil-
dren are becom-
ing quite com-
mon; and the tak-
ing of “babes”
to have their
teeth scraped,
regulated, filled,
etc., is now a
regular thing.
Writer knows
several instances
of children under
12 wearing arti-
ficial teeth. And
now someone
proposes that in-
cidentally to a
youngster’s
schooling its
teeth should be
“ regulated.”
(Nothing new,
this.) Some ob-
servations:
Father and
mother with good
teeth : children
from 5 to 15 have
had nearly all
their second
teeth extracted,
and are wearing
substitutes.
Again, parents
are both lacking
full number of
teeth, but pro-
geny, from 15 to
30, have all more
than usually
go od sets of natural teeth. Now, in the former casei
the home abounds with “patent” foods all guaran-
teed to form flesh and bone; whereas the second
family are, and h ave always been, meat eaters. The
first lot of children were suckled on “ bottle ” and
weaned on “ patent ” pa p, but the second lot were natur-
ally suckled and weane d on a mutton-bone or a strip of
beef. Country people, also, who are reared and live on
natural toothsome food, are stronger in chewing-tackle
than city people, who favor “ ready-cooked,” “ ready
digested,” “ready assimilated” “patents,” and whose
teeth, as a rule, either fail to form or fail to last. All
this favors the conclusion that the general failure of
teeth, is not so much from lack of stamina as from want
of use. [A good deal in this, though the induction isn’t
wide enough to be trustworthy. Many hold that
“science” has taken away more from hygiene with one
hand than she has given with the other. —Ed. B.]
Melb. Board of Health, having found
tubercular bacilli in samples of the milk supply,
set on the trail a vet. who traced the trouble to a
Templestowe cow with a heart weighing 3flb.
(chiefly tubercle) over the average. The animal
had such a fine exterior that Templestowe, when
the animal’s destruction was ordered, arose in its
wrath and protested. Moral: Never judge a cow
by her overcoat.
* * * •
Pointed out by a Sydney meat-inspector
that a great many of the carcases he deals with
have had the neck-glands carefully removed, and
that, though in those cases he may be perfectly
certain that the animal had shown disease in that
part, he is—so long as no traces of the actual cor-
ruption are left—quite powerless to interfere !
Which shows it to be urgently necessary that
the fact of any portion of a carcase usually re-
tained having been excised should legally be made
prima facie evidence of disease.
OUR BOARDING-HOUSE AGAIN.
It was the Sabbath, and the conversation had naturally turned -upon grave and solemn
subjects, “ I have never,” said the mean man, as he helped himself to the last
banana on the fruit-dish, “ I have never been inside a church but once in ten
years.” “And how came you to go then?” asked the husband of the lady-
acrobat, as he picked his teeth with the nut-crackers. “I went," said the mean
man, finishing the banana in two bites, “ I went to get rid of thruppence with a
hole in it, which I had tried in vain to pass off for months!”
At a Federation meeting in Melbourne,
recently, Watt, M.P., quoted Brunton Stephens’
lines :
“ Till all our sundering lines with love o’ergrown
Our bounds shall he the girdling seas alone.”
And the Argus gave them thus :
“Till all our sundered lives with love o’ergrown,
Our bonds shall be the gurgling seas alone.”
“ Gurgling seas ! ” And the Argus has been so
indignant lately about the mishandling of loci]
poets. Many’s the poetry-book the Argus never-
read.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin - .— After what you have
printed on the matter, pro and con, 1 think the
Gregory lunacy affair may safely be left to settle
itself. But I would like to say a word about the
one really scandalous feature of the matter—viz.,
the “nobbling” of the Melbourne press. Because
Mr. C. A. Topp is leader of the Vic. Bar, as well
as permanent head of the asylums department,
the law court reporters were seemingly anxious to
“ spare the feelings of Mrs. Gregory ” (she being
Mr. Topp’s sister) by preserving an all-round
silence re her husband’s appeal to Justice
Williams. Topp family influence shut the case
out of the papers, and thereby excited suspicion.
The correspondence in The Bulletin, whilst
quite dispelling any suspicion of Toppian injus-
tice to Gregory, has served the useful purpose of
throwing light upon a matter which certainly
needed publicity, but which the “respectable”
press had endeavored to keep dark.—X.

Sydney Daily Telegraph, having, ap-
parently, made a good “strike” with its special
“ Building Trade ” column, it will be interesting
to observe at what interval of time the Morning
Herald will consider it compatible with its
dignity to follow the example. Although Granny
never by any chance hits on an idea of her own,
she is careful to bring along her “crib” in a
casual sort of way, and when, as it were, nobody
is looking.
Things in the building-line now boom in
Melbourne. All over the city new buildings are
going up and old ones are being altered and
added to ; and in the suburbs—especially Bruns-
wick—dwellings are being erected on every piece
of vacant land. Every man you meet in Mel-
bourne is prepared to show the exact spot on
which Hordern and Mark Foy are going to build
—and every one differs as to the locality.

The accident to the Perthshire has been
followed by a curiously large 1. umber of similar
mishaps, the case of the Waikato being about the
fifth broken shaft reported since amongst Aus-
tralian shipping alone. This last accident is the
most serious of the lot on account of the vessel’s
lonely position. Moral : Twin-screws.
* * * •
Melbourne item : Annie Harvey had been
sentenced to 24 hours’ imprisonment for drunken-
ness. Discharged at 9 o’clock on a bitterly cold
night. Hungry—a dish of hominy at 4 o’clock
has little sustaining force five hours later—ill-clad
and penniless, she wandered into Fitzroy Gardens.
A passing constable heard her, with bitterest
irony, crooning “ Home, Sweet Home ! ” and
advised her to go thither. Knowing that if she
fully stated the case to the officer the only result
to her would be twelve months’ “hard” for
vagrancy, she held her peace and wandered away
to pass the long, cold hours as best she might. In
the morning a gardener found her under a bush
dead. The cost of an inquest was at least 255., and
the funeral cost something besides—£l at the lowest
estimate. It is but poor economy to fire a home-
less, thinly-clad woman out of gaol with no money
late on a bitter winter night, and then spend 455.
of Turner’s surplus on her inquest and funeral.

Cole, of Melb. Book Arcade, has .opened
a photographic studio on his top floor, as an outlet
for the enthusiasm of busy Mrs. C., who is chant-
ing the praises of a magic hair-restorer, the recipe
for which was imparted to her under circumstances
of a 3-vol.-novel character. Mrs. Cole couldn’t rest
until she got that studio. She lies in wait now-
adays for bald-headed men, and, having persuaded

shall be re-taken gratis as soon as they have
raised a crop of hair. No man ever quite despairs
of recovering his lost foliage, and the scheme
offers peculiar attractions for those who would
like to figure as a “ Before, and After,” subject on
an advt. sheet.*

“ How is it that the
Chow, who used to treat
his moke kindly, is now
getting frequently fined
for cruelty thereto?”
Well, isn’t the Chow a
strongly imitative ani-
mal?
The Deceased Husband’s Brother Bill is
getting through Maoriland Parliament. It has
long been lawful for a man to marry his late
wife’s sister—so many men wanted to marry the
late wife’s younger and less faded sister that the
male elector wouldn’t he happy till he got it.
But until woman got a vote nobody cared a straw
how much she wanted to marry her late husband’s
younger brother. The woman-vote has changed
all that. Besides, M.L. is much troubled about
its declining marriage-rate and birth-rate, and if
any woman who would otherwise remain a widow
can get a chance to marry her late old man’s
brother, and have three children, or if any man
who would otherwise be a lone bachelor is willing
to take over his brother’s widow, it would be a
pity to hinder them. Maoriland’s complaint is
that it is getting very hard to persuade anybody
to marry anybody at all nowadays, and if any-
body is willing to marry somebody, the State
should give every encouragement.

The far-reaching possibilities
of phthisis, in its new category as
an actively-infectious disease, are
only just beginning to be recog-
nised. The well-known Dr. An-
drew Wilson has recently been
calling attention to the risks
healthy passengers on long
voyages run through possible
cabin.mating with the con-
sumptives so often to be
found voyaging for cure or
amelioration. Beyond doubt
the mail-steamer authorities
will shortly be forced by pub-
lic opinion or direct legisla-
tion to provide separate and
special accommodation for the
phthisically-afflicted.
“From 1888 to 1897
the average N.S.W. mortality
from phthisis was three a
day.”—Dr. Tidswell, before
the Tuberculosis Commission.
East Melbourne has for
some time been infested by a
band of idiotic men and youths who
parade the streets at night with
their shirts outside their trousers,
two rows of torches, a man bearing a
big wooden crucifix at their head, and
behind them the fatuous crowd which
would run miles after a three-legged
calf or a Highland regiment. Re-
gardless of the fact that people may
be sick or dying in the surrounding
houses, this insane plagiarism of
the Salvarmy makes night hor-
rible with bad singing and the monotonous
blare of a badly-played cornet. One of these
nights an infidel with a sick wife or child will
expostulate with a gun, and then people will howl
about the “decreasing respect for religion.”
* * * *
Judge a’Beckett had something to say in
Melb. Divorce Court last week re lovely woman’s
capacity for reckless lying on behalf of herself
and to the detriment of the other fellow. When
the woman happens to be the other fellow’s wife
she is entitled to charge him with the exes, of her
defence, which is usually, says Judge a’B., a per-
jured repetition of the yarn she has told to her
proctor. In the ordinary way she lies with im-
punity, like the average co-respondent who is
supposed to be gallantly attempting to shield the
“ honor of a lady.” A decree nisi for the hus-
band ends the matter. But when the decree
doesn’t come off, owing to circumstances over
which the wife had no control, the Law must take
her perjuries seriously, says Judge a’Beekett.
Wherefore he committed a certain giddy
lady for trial at next Melb. Crim. Court on
a charge of “perfectly useless false swearing.”
This extreme measure will, it is hoped, tend to
deter the fair sex from too much untruthfulness
in the face of overwhelming evidence. Woman is
always a Mrs. Partington where her precious
Virtue is concerned. With her little broom of
bristling perjuries she hopes to sweep back the
tide of evidence.
“ Woman doesn’t reason, she only feels,”
is a new, kind, apologetic axiom, as far as it
goes. How about the unreasoning woman who
doesn’t feel ; the mere attractive, smiling fawner
with no heart, no impulses, no anything save a
few practical ideas and feminine deceits ? What
is Nature’s apology for her ? She doesn’t even fill
an animal want, for the altogether unfeeling one
never goes astray, and rarely succeeds in luring a
man right up to the altar. But she engages the
attention of one, two —perhaps half-a-dozen men
in turn, who finally discover her worthlessness,
and charge it to the discredit of the whole sex.
And she is said to be dangerously frequent these
times. The New Woman is, more or less, the
same old, unreasoning creature, minus all the old,
admirably-foolish impulses.

“ Old Jack ” : Alfred Deakin, at a recent
“ pleasant Sunday afternoon,” had, as usual, a lot
to say about the promise of the future, as ex-
hibited in the “emancipation of the sex.” But
even Deakin didn’t dare dive into possibilities.
The “ sex ”■ is so rapidly—er—becoming the other
fellow that, in the coming century, the lying-in
hospitals will perhaps be occupied by—well, it’s
too big an idea for this bald-head. Let it rip !
* * * *
There was a lot of pluck in a Broken Hill
mine the other day. Hundreds of tons of loose,
sandy overburden poured through the timbers,
straining and displacing them, so that the flow
increased every second. A couple of miners were
caught; one was buried instantly, but the other, a
young miner named Lovett, managed to keep on
his feet and scramble up inch by inch as the stuff
poured in. At last, though, the inrush mastered
him, and, a fixture now, he was buried deeper
and deeper. For anyone to go near him was
desperately dangerous; and when his mates
attempted it he implored them to keep back.
“ It’ll be bad enough for two to go under —don’t
make it more,” he said. They listened to
him—for they knew how deadly treacherous
this moving ground is and kept back.
Then an old man hurried up —he had
been working on the same shift. They told him
it was his son who was caught, but they pointed
out the awful danger of going near. “ He’s my
boy, and I’ll try,” was the answer. And then
there was a magnificent scene—a young man pro-
tecting himself on his hands and arms from still-
falling debris, begging the old man to keep back,
and finally appealing to him to think of those at
home; and an old man inspiring the others to
risk everything. It is good to know that young
Lovett was able to drag himself out, and by the
help of the others to reach safety.
* * * *
A New Australian writes thus from Lama
Rugua :
Luckily the Paraguayan Govt, offered us 100 acres
apiece. We divided up the land and so obtained free
land and free individxial action. Laws we have none ;
therefore we get justice by arbitration with the people as
umpires. As to church and chapel, we let everyone run
his own if he likes. To sum up, we are happy because we
are free.”
This is about the twentieth time the Paraguay
pilgrims have achieved happiness and freedom
since they abandoned Australia. First they got
it in Communism ; then they tried a shandy gaff
—half individualism, half Communism ; noiv they
have achieved their millennium by a return to
pure individualism. Bless their simple hearts !
They could have had as much of that as ever they
wanted over here in this wicked land.

article was, he added, written by

There was once in Christ-
church (M.L.) a very fat citizen
who was said to be able to go
to sleep standing on the kerb-
stone. Woke up one day from
one of these snoozes, and be-
gan whistling for his dog. After
he had whistled himself dead dry,
he commenced to move and
tripped over his faithful canine,
which had also been asleep, stand-
ing ’neath the shadow of its master’s
vast rotundity.

“ Strong Joint Be “ B.E.’s ” paragraph
in Bulletin in 5/8/’99. Are not the characteris-
tics of the guttersnipe pretty well the same all
the world over ? The Austral genus gamin is, at
times, (despite “ 8.E.” to the contrary) very smart
at repartee. Instance: At Red fern railway-
station, t’other day, a station-inspector, noted for
his strong personality and bull dog temperament,
was sitting in the Melb. express. Newsboy on
platform kept bothering him with “ Buy a News,
sir?”—and was answered brusquely, “Go to the
devil! ” The youngster, quite unabashed, putting
his thumbs in an old torn waistcoat, critically sur-
veyed the old chap and replied, “ You can thank
God, sir, for your grey hairs.” Wasn’t that humor ?

“ Alba ” on gamins :
Dear Bulletin.— If “8.E.” (5/8/’99) will take the
trouble to read my screed of Bulletin, 8/7/’99, he will
find that I didn’t “ eulogise ” the Austral gamin, or anv
gamin whatever. Writer merely said things about the
Rushcutter’s Bay small boy, and the bay isn’t Australia.
Also my par. is merely a peg for “ 8.E.” to hang his
gamin on. And why does he drag in the Dublin omad-
haun ? For three centuries the gamin has been boosted
by continental writers, and every penster who has
sneezed in the dust of the Moulin Rouge yearns to roll
the gamin-log a trifle further. The gamin"bye and large
is, phew ! a master of iniquity at eight, at nine a pimp, at
ten too jaded to call a fiacre or steal a cigarette. The
Paris hospitals used to be full of him. The brilliancy of
the London guttersnipe is embodied in his best joke (he’s
been using it with English insistence for a decade),
“What cheer, guv’nor? Who’s yer ’atter?” varied at
intervals with Voltairian quips from Sloper or Comic
Cuts. So far, the Australian kid hasn’t been written
about; also he isn’t a long way off and doesn’t call his
mother a “cow” in mellifluous French, therefore he fails
to interest. Ask any unbiassed French or English teacher
in Australia whether young Cornstalk isn’t as keen and
humor-blessed as the old-world hopefuls. Have seen him
lurk around the various Australian quays to pelt Irish
immigrants (no offence to “ 8.E.”) with meat-fed derision
—derision may not be humor, but it’s a near relative—
and the flittering immigrant generally responded with
a brick or us “bit av a shtick.” “B.E. needn t have
phe omadhaun) for a detnn’d little fawn, and when he
j sn q fawning he’s touching his hat to anything fat that
comes along with a brown. So: “ yes, sorr ; no, sorr ;
yes, sorr; ah-h-h-h no, sorr.” Perhaps the peculiar
development of fawn arises from scarcity of spuds in his
I'ttle inside. Anyhow, “ B.E. needn t compare him with
our decently-fed young scamps who jibe and say blanky
in the shadow of the Premier himself.
