A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, August 15, 18110.
My Dear Myee,—
Considerable agitation since definite news got
abroad of the vice regal September gaieties. Two
Beauchamp balls are fixed for Sept. 9th and 14th,
and a garden-party for Sept. 31. Weigh against
this tlierumored overhauling of the V. H. visiting-
list and you will understand why some people are
only just beginning to be really unhappy.
About these evening parties—one hears such
curious accounts. So far press and prestige have
been severely uninvited, which drives one on to
hearsay. The clergy, staffs of nurses, M’sP., have
all been up to spend evenings. Many pretty
society women, who think things , must be dull
without them, haven’t had their company re-
quested yet. It reads like the style of entertain-
ment in an Edition de Luxe of old English
evenings given to the Chatelaine’s Sunday-school
class, or the squire’s more respectable tenants.
Lady Mary and Lady Bertha sing duets. Mr.
Victor Corkran plays the piano. Printed cata-
logues of His Ex. ’s treasures fill in the blanks.
Then supper and early adieux, naturally.
Everyone feels that Govt. House is trying very,
very hard to be Nice. Nobody ever felt so posi-
tive on that point about any other Gov. But con-
sider human nature for one minute. Then you will
understand—as well as anything of this kind can
be uinlerstanded—why the evening-party people
wonder if they ought to take it as a slight that
they have been asked to duets and catalogues that
Society has not been asked to ; also, why the
Society people can’t make up their minds whether
Govt. House has been tossing then* a compliment
or otherwise in leaving them out of the catalogues,
etc. In the meantime, there are two very worried-
looking young men in this city—the Gov. and his
secretary. Why doesn’t a cousin, or a sister, or an
aunt advise His Ex. that to take life seriously at
2(5 may be leaving nothing but frivol for old age?
Then, if that does not touch him, nothing will.
Speaking of sisters, Lady Mary goes home in
October. A certain young lady and . her mother
journey Londonwards by the saii.e boat. Lis Ex.
will then be sisterless. What offers?
Have you seen His Ex.’s contribution to the
“Souvenir Book,” published by the Press Bazaar?
The cruelty of it: written in French. It borrows
Alphonse Karr’s sentiment about thorns and roses,
“For my part, I am glad that the thorns have
roses.” Signed, “ Beauchamp.” For our part, we
are glad H.E. has noticed the roses. Signed,
“All Women.” Considering all things, that
autograph - book is the gem of the Fair.
It has been turned out beautifully, in
blue covers and shiny paper, and sold cheaply
for half-a-crown. You get a glimpse into all sorts
and conditions of minds, musical, theatrical, vice-
regal, medical. Mr. and Mrs. Brough and Harry
Rickards are the only theatricals represented. The
Broughs quote passages from the “Physician”
and “ A Pair of Spectacles ” and ’Arry unburdens
himself of a good and heavy sentiment about
philantliropy. Colonel Bell breaks into gaudy
language: “For your holy cause, I fain would
harness the very storms, and coin the hurricanes
into golden syllables.” Next, please. And you
come upon Lord Brassey’s Browning quote.
“It is not what man does do which exalts him,
but what a man would do.” Now, remember that,
Victoria. For the point of a quotation is in its
application. Artists make a fine show, but chiefly
of work we have seen before. Wiegand and 11.
Goring-Thomas are the only composers who re-
sponded. But the poets have come out strongly ;
Henry Lawson alone is missing. His democratic
soul revolted, and no persuasion could induce to
poetry under vice-regal patronage.
Last Thursday saw the opening of the Fair.
His Ex. who did the deed on a long legged plat-
form situated at the far end of the Hall, had to be
politically engineered through the dense crowds
before he could mount. Some of the V.R. party
were swallowed whole by the mob. G. H. Reid
to the rescue. He bravely fished up Lady Bertha
from the crowd, and hauled up two other platform
ladies later on. On the structure were Lord
Beauchamp, Ladies Mary and Bertha, Hon.
Violet, Mr. Corkran, George and Mrs. Reid,
Mayoress Harris, Mr. H. Levy (Chairman of
Committee), Mr. Druce (secretary or general
manager), “Mister James,” of the Herald, and
ever so many other Bazaar representatives. Mr.
Clarence Backhouse, the Fair’s architect, should
have been there, but was not. On the other hand
—but that is a different story.
Stall-holders who looked forward to testing their
bazaar smiles and fancy prizes on His Ex. were
keenly disappointed. The day before the Fair
they all received orders not to ask the Governor to
buy. He had sent along a cheque for £3O
on condition that nobody asked him to buy as
much as a “dip.” But, although he did not
linger over erewelled slippers and just as unkind
smoking-caps, every stall in the Fair was blessed
by a brief visit and a hand-shake.
And, re this, I witnessed one strange little
pathetic incident. His Ex. had shaken hands
with the head of one stall, and had passed on ;
suddenly the stall-holder was smitten with the
recollection of a fat, hard-working lady friend,
delving away at the eatables and things at the
back of the stall. t She tore breathlessly after His
Ex. and urged him to come back for a minute.
She hauled her assistant up to the counter, intro-
duced her emphatically by name, and watched the
slim, vice-regal hand close briefly round her friend’s
fat fingers. Of course everyone at the Fair is
charitable. But that was the only woman who
sho.wed it in a really large way.
Why does Lady Mary alternate so puzzlingly in
her mode of dressing ? One day she is smart and
fresh ; on the next she isn’t. At the Fair she
worn a sedate bluish-grey silk, with a little white
tucked yoke and blank lace insertion running
round the skirt. She tips her prim hats well over her
nosoandfavorsthelargein millinery- barly Bertha’s
amiable face was becomingly lightened with a
white silk under tine black net and lace. Among
other visitors there was not much onecould notice as
the Fair frocks put all others in the shade with their
dazzling colors. Fkkeman’s Jol'iinai. tea-room
sported a brilliant shade of rose-red linen with
•white caps and aprons. Mrs. Aronson's attrac-
tive Sydney Mail, store wore black frocks and
hats and large blue aprons branded with the
paper’s name. The 1 Ikhai.D stall was draped in
art green, and waited on by the Misses Curnow
in cream frocks braided with narrow black velvet.
And I liked the black and white striped skirts
and little white lace Etous worn by the Art Union
stall-holders.
Among many charming Fair figures I noticed
mostly a pretty, fair-haired doctor’s wife in a grey
dress with white muslin apron and highly-be-
coming.white Dutch cap, serving egg-beaters on
her badly-situated “Dutch kitchen Mrs. Harry
Levy, at a mauve-tinted soap-stall with a host of
pretty girls in Ion" eel skirts of pale heliotrope
cashmere, tucked silk blouses of the same shade
and largo black hats with strings; Mrs, Willie
Mitchell, selling cigars and cigarettes in cream
voile skirt and silk blouse with a light boa and
rose tints in her hat; Mrs. Lassetter at the same
stall in trailing cream silk with a turquoise hat;
Miss Linda Rundle belpingMrs. Toohey in a sweet
little white book-muslin frock sweeping the
ground with frills and tucks to the knees, and
finished off with a cerise rosette, at the point of
the fichu, and a big plack picture hat, and the Misses
Slee in pretty white muslin frocks and little
toques of pink roses. Actresses were conspicuous
by their absence. Miss Henrietta Watson was
there in clinging black, depreciating her powers
of drawing bazaar coin. “ I can only sell to
Chinamen,” she said. “Other people just'stare
at me and don’t buy; n.y fame hasn’t reached
the Chinamen.” Miss Vara Renny, from the
Brough co., and Miss Roxy Barton were
also serving in black frocks. Miss Inez Bensusan
was in black and white at the art-union stall.
Miss Grace Noble was billed at Mrs. Mitchell’s
stall, but was not there on opening day.
By the way, Mrs. Willie Mitchell’s stall was
more than it looked from the front. You passed
through—if you were a man—and found yourself
in a beautifully-alluring smoking-room with low,
red lights, and oriental tapestries, and velvet
chairs, and women suffering from repulsed
dolls and pin-cushions looked on from the other
end of the room and wondered despondently why
they had never thought of that. To sum lip,
there is much to see at the Fair, but not as much
to buy as one could wish were one inclined that
way. Somestalls, particularly those of Miss Eadith
Walker, Mrs. Fairfax and Miss Harris, - have ex-
quisite needlework to offer. Others can show you
nothing worth looking at. As far as the outside
of the stalls go Mr. Clarence Backhouse has given
everyone the opportunity of an excellent artistic
setting. But the inner sides of the Exhibition,
behind the stalls, are blank and ugly, and a row of
advertisements round the gallery entirely thwarts
the aesthetic impulse of the Fair as a spectacle.
Surely, surely, the art of conversation is passing
into the dead language. The indication is strong
in “novel evenings.” Nobody entertains now
unless the guests can be offered a game to play and
a prize to win—even a prize for the worst player
has to be given as an attraction. To talk away an
evening is impossible. There is nobody to talk to.
An agitated hostess is for over bearing down on
you with a pencil and paper asking how many you
have guessed ? Everyone is rushing about wildly.
There is not even need to provide seats, for no-
body has time to sit down. You must play, play
all the time. A party without a game is con-
sidered an awful bore, and conversation con-
tinues to draw nearer to its death. No doubt
there will be a revolution, or a renaissance soon.
But, in the meantime, “ How d’you do ; have you
got a pencil?”—“Thank you, I have a pencil, but
I have not a programme,” is all the talk that oue
cares to shine in.
And hostesses are racking strange things out of
their brains. The very latest asks guests to bring
along “a portrait of yourself at the age of 10 or
11.” The photos, are mixed, and the guests are
handed pencils and programmes and set to work
identifying a red hook-nose with a little white
blob, and so on. Rather painful for the red hook
when an ingenue exclaims, “To think that your
nose was like that once! ”
I hear that an even-newer novelty consists in
Setting all the females of the party to take their
oots off, mixing them up, and letting the men
pick out the owners. But the dangers in that are
obvious to anyone with a foot over seven.
Speaking of hostesses, there was once a girl of
18 and a woman who didn’t like her. The other
woman was married, and her husband talked too
much to the girl. One day a riding-party, in-
cluding the girl, came to the “other woman’s ”
house. Of course, there was afternoon-tea.
The girl could not reach the sugar-tongs, but
helped herself from the bowl with her finger-
tips. The other woman called to the
servant to take away the bowl and fill it
with fresh sugar. Which was done ! The
girl coloured, everyone felt uncomfortable, the
party decided to leave. Just as they were starting
the girl remarked quietly that if the sugar was
spoilt by her touch, to use the cup and saucer she
had used would contaminate the whole house, and
picking up the cup and saucer (a very expensive
cup and saucer) she dashed both against the wall.
Then she turned on her heel, and walked out of
the house. The wilted, sun-smitten Australian
girl has evidently a little energy left in her still.
In some ways, women haven’t advanced.
This story is told of a clever University girl with
a great grip on mental and moral science. She
married. Shortly after, her husband caught a very
bad cold. Being a grad, with honors, she doctored
him herself, but the cold wasn’t seared by her
philosophy and logic, and got harder and harder.
One night it sounded so agonizing that the Bride-
lb A. determined to try something daring. “I
shall put a mustard leaf on his lung,” she said to
herself. She got up in the middle of the night,
found a mustard leaf, took the candle, and left the
room. When she returned the patient asked
suspiciously what she had been doing. “I went
to get the medical book, darling, to look up where
your lung is ! ”

a boil, and add to it one well-beaten egg and level tea-
spoonful of corn-starch (moistened with a Tittle cold milk),
two teaspoonfuls of sugar. Boil up once and serve.

guage while Home Chat continues to re-
organise the tilings we can say witli safety.
According to this omnipotent authority, Society’s
Small-talk is going to be swept and garnished
with a cruelly careful hand this season. Dear me !
For the. Warning is headed “Society-Mistakes
some Women Make,” and all the words that come
to the tip of one’s tongue irresistibly just now—
with the new Governor and other tilings—are
shown up in their true vulgah light. “One never
hears,” says Home Chat, “what an aristocratic
man,” or “what an aristocratic woman!” The
only expression permissible is “ She looks
thoroughly r/rande dame ! ” or “ What a well-bred
man !” Alas for Potts Point imparting the flavor
of bad language to that “ grande dame / ” Also,
’tis bad form to say that anyone is stylish; you
must put it that “she has great style.” The
wickedness of having “my photo taken” won’t
appeal to Pott’s Point (which has its “ photo
took,”) but people from everywhere may feel
ashamed of themselves when Home Chat points
to the unspeakable awfulness of asking “Do you
cycle?” From these articles you can gather
that London society has its own sore point.
“Don’t speak of ‘ The Waleses,’ of ‘The‘Con-
naughts,’ in alluding to the Royal Family.” Don’t
say “Thank you, I will take some of yours,” when
the hostess asks you will you take beef or chicken.
Don’t say, “I am very partial to champagne.”
Then they do say these things in London!
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The ‘First-born of Egypt’ (as Shakespeaie
called ‘the Naioest’ of his day) took care that
Melb. Hunt Club ball (Aug. 10t.li) was just as
frilly as when Mrs. Freeman-Thomas held the
reins and vowed she’d show the Natives what a
proper ‘Pink Dance’ should be. This year, as
heretofore, ‘pink’ (i.e., rod) lamps, draping*,
etc., shed a rosy glow over the painted, decorated
faces, inseparable from powdered hair.
“Sporting ‘pink’ failed to transform Gov.
Brassey into a rollicking fox-hunter —it showed
him up as a tired-out Patriarch, wearing a jaded
heart on. his gaudy sleeve. Lady B.’s rich black
gown, displayed, an under-dress of jetted net over
ivory satin. Ladies Raglan and Violet Somerset
and Mrs. Pakenhaih, following their leader in all-
black single file, between two rows of welcoming
committee,’had the air of mourners at a State
fir.ral. Lengthy Aide Nevill’s pink coat-tails
seemed yards long—there was no end to ’em.
“Fair-haired Mrs, Goo. Chirnside, in shim-
mering snaky black and diamonds ad lib.,
was one of the successes on view. Miss
Florence Stanbridge, the Daylesfoicl heiress,
sported the white satin and ‘ pink ’ scarf of com-
mittee. Her imported frock was smartened by a
side panel of arum, lilies in folded satin. Gentle
boy-aide Cecil stood about with a fashionable
dancing-mistress, whose style of beauty took
kindly to powder and patches, and who, given a
few more inches of height, would have shone out
resplendently.
“ White satin was the feature of the hour. A
blonde beauty in gown of same, with two gradu-
ated flounces of impressive lace, fly-away waist
scarf of turquoise tulle, bouquet of red carnations
and plumes, blue, black and white on hair, looked
the whole company in the face in righteous
triumph. Handsome Miss Berry unwisely coif-
fured her powdered hair in classic style. Floured
thatch can be coquettish or stately, but “ artistic ”
methods don’t go with it, somehow. It flourished
in Salons, not studios.
“ Ball-room etiquette has turned topsy-turvey
since old-young ladies were new. Now, naioest
spring-chickens pace the floor ‘on their own,’
arms twined behind lest folks should call them
flat-chested. If plump arms are parader’s
speciality, akimbo attitude goes well. He-dancers
shake off partners when and where it suits them.:
Sure as you spot a flntterer escorted back to
mamma’s side every time, you bet she's not a
somebody.
“ ‘ Praise Him from whom nil blessings
flow,’ that the support of a male arm is no
longer inseparable from ball-room locomotion.
Many a woman has been embittered for life
through famishing all the evening with delicacies
going to waste in the next room, just for want of
an escorting arm, in a sleeve smelling of moth-
powder. To this very day, in the back-blocks,
where fashions change slowly or not at all, a pasty-
faced youth offers his elbow as impressively as if
it were first prize in Tatt.’s sweep. At a recent
extra-smart local hop, a young and handsome
heiress met half-way and rounded up all dilatory
or short-sighted liar tilers fumbling through the
crowd on her tracks.
“At a ball given by His Excellency and Her
Excellency at one gubernatorial establishment,
beer was liberally supplied literally in buckets—
stable buckets—to the immense disgust of the
high-livers below stairs. At another “treat”
given by the same august couple a few weeks back
to a portion of the local military, seven minutes
and a half were allowed for refreshments, with
the result that many of the warriors declined to
join in the stampede, and went empty away.
“An Australian capital last week’witnessed its
most exalted personage driving a pair in a manner
that would have earned a vegetable-hawker three
months for negligence. Pedestrians scattered and
went indoors, cyclists dismounted and did like-
wise, and other drivers followed the advice of old
Coroner Youl, ‘ Pull hard to the left, shut your
eyes, and say as much of the Lord's prayer as you
can remember.’ Eventually things looked so
serious that the groom at the side of the exalted
personage was forced to the awful extremity of
taking the reins of his master’s hands in order
to get the owner home with a whole neck.
Master is evidently bent on falling some more.
“ Information went out to the councillors and a
few friends that the Mayor had expressed a wish
that as many as could should wear the kilts of
Scotland at the Caledonian knee-show in (he
Town Hall, and there was a wild
round on Wednesday to hire them whei°
ever they could be procured. But as few
of these outsiders appeared on Friday night
in the abbreviated pleats, it is evident
the trying-on had unfavorable results, or the
knees of Englishmen and Paddies were not built
for parade. Hon. W. Knox, who showed a broad
expanse of shirt in the first part of the evening,
and a big strip, of leg when lie had metamorphosed
himself into a Scotchman in the second part, laid
on quite the most swagger of all the braw
Icelanders on show.
“ A Melb. doctor wrote to Henrietta Watson
asking if her stage-throes (‘ King's Musketeer ’)
represented the effects of any ‘known poison.’
Miladi, answering cheerfully in the negative,
pointed out that on the boards ‘effect’ is the guid-
ing star of every situation. Truth is almighty,
but you’d simply empty the theatre if you at-
tempted realistic bilious, colicky, or convulsed
poison-symptoms. Still, she said, if her corres-
pondent undertook to coach her in the fatal finale
of a genuine poisoning case, no less suitable for
stage-production than her present ‘ business,’ she
would be delighted to include realism in tier bag of
tricks. After all, it was probably some antique
rare poison which released the world of Miladi,
considering that she extracted it from an heirloom
sapphire.
“ At a recent seance in Melbourne, a young lady
who had come in the disguise of an elderly aunt
to test the proceedings, got a nasty ‘slap across
the mouth ’ from the ruffled spernt of a Red
Indian. She made an inquisitive grab at the
vision, and having divested it of some gauzy
veilings and, a black feather, exclaimed— ‘ You
wicked wretch, T have exposed you ! ’ whereunon
the soul of the Indian hit out as aforesaid. The
credulous ‘ circle ’ cried in the darkness for some-
body to turn on the gas, and when the light came
the medium (late of Sydney) was discovered in a
state of partial undress and furious excitement.
But still the game of ‘manifestations’ goes on,
and the business of this particular lady—who is
tall, stout, and most ungliostly—will be all the
brisker for the advt. An exposed medium com-
mands the warm support of good spiritualists for
the next few Weeks. They rally up with their
half-crowns, and invite the impostor to convince
them more than ever, and the impostor supplies
them with peaceful manifestations until their
faith is fully restored. Human nature, I am
afraid, has a fine capacity for believing in any-
thing except plain hard Truth.
V ours affectionately,

PEOPLE YOU KNOW.
No. 2.—The Postman.
A rural postman writes : “I desire to add this
unsolicited testimony to the sterling qualities of
Dr. Tibbies’ Yi-Cocoa. I am a rural postman,
and exposed to all kinds of weather. I have used
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Oocoa daily this last eleven
months, and I believe that I can do my work with
less fatigue since I have used it. I can honestly
recommend to my fellow postmen Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-
Oocoa for its sustaining qualities.”
Of late years many new manufactures, rejoicing
in a multiplicity of names, have been brought
before the public notice, but there are none which
have bounded into favor so readily as has Dr.
Tibbies’ Yi-Cocoa. Throughout the country its
sales have increased with great rapidity, and an
article which only a few years back was unheard
of, has now become one of the most popular
beverages of the people.
Merit, and merit alone, is what is claimed for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and the Proprietors are
prepared to send to any reader who names The
Bulletin (a post-card will do) a dainty sample tin
of Dr. Tibbies’ Yi-Cocoa free and post-paid.
There is no magic in all this. It is a plain, honest,
straightforward offer. It is done to introduce the
merits of Vi-Cocoa into every home. Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, as a concentrated form of nourishment
and vitality, is invaluable; nay, more than this,
for to all who wish to face the strife and battle of
life with greater endurance and more sustained
exertion, it is absolutely indispensable.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
S.A. has a 100-year-old pioneer in Mrs.
John White, who completed the century July 25.
The old lady came to S.A. in the Forties, some
time before Premier Kingston formed his present
Ministry.
J. H. Symon, Q.C., S.A.’s foremost lawyer
and Federal aspirant, who is travelling England
and the Continent, has been in bad health ever
since he left Australia. He is believed to have
made a small fortune out of the recent Westralian
market rise.
Rabbi Davis, tvho entered his 71st year
this week, was born in London, but has spent 37
years in Sydney. The Sydney Synagogue is
largely the work of his hands. Before coming to
Australia he officiated as Rabbi at Portsmouth
(Eng.) and in Jamaica.
North-coasters have been highly tickled
over the shindy between Perry, M.L.A. for Bal-
lina, and Premier Reid, and Perry’s invitation
to “ come outside.” Perry is usually the quietest
little man breathing, and is about as good for a
“scrap” as Fat George is for 100 yards in
“ evens.”
King O’Malley has definitely given
S.A. politics best, and has gone back to Mel-
bourne and life-assurance agency. He will, how-
ever, be heard of again in Adelaide, because,
through Glynn, M.P., he has issued seven writs
against various political opponents claiming
damages.
Premier Kingston’s friends are again very
concerned about his ill-health. The usually
robust S.A. Premier was so broken down some days
ago that he was even forced to absent himself
from Parliament, and his medical adviser predicts
a complete collapse unless the S.A. democratic
leader takes a holiday.
R. E. O’Connor, whose 48th birthday
passed the other day, took his M.A. degree at the
age of 20. While a law-student he worked at
journalism, his best work going into the defunct
Echo, a Fairfax evening production. He is one of
t ie few N.S. W. politicians who have not changed
their principles with the times.
A. B. Robinson, commercial editor of the
Age— -known in the city as “ little Duppy ” —is
off to London on a holiday trip. Duppy is very
much trusted in Collins-st. Bankers defer to him,
and he is probably the only man in Melbourne who
will dare to enter the board-room of a bank while
the directors are sitting, without the formality of
an introduction. Likewise, he is David Syme’s
right-hand man in business matters, and is as
solid as he is fat.
It was characteristic or the deceased
N.S.W. millionaire, Thomas Walker—he really
was a millionaire, for he left his daughter quite
two millions—that he would never, if he could
possibly avoid it, use a new sheet of paper even in
writing an important business communication ;
his plan was to tear off and use the waste leaf of a
note that he had received. Also, he would never
use a stamp if he could have a letter sent by
hand.
Premier Reid’s appointment of him-
self tjN.S. Wales Attorney-
was a judicious
move—for two reasons. First,
because if he were to go out
of office he would go back
to the Bar with an Attorney-
General’s prestige—which
usually means a good deal,
however little it may mean
in his case. Junior work he
cannot now accept, and as
a Q.C. his A.G.-ship will
help considerably. Second-
ly, if he remains in office,
anything might happen.
For instance, if the Chief-
Justiceship, or a Puisne-
Judgeship were to become
vacant, the Attorney -
General of the day would
have the right of nomina-
tion, and it has been a good
tradition with Attorney -
Generals that they have
rarely been able to discover
anyone better fitted for vacan-
cies than themselves. So G. H.
Reid would fall into a legal and financial haven
of rest, and would be another example of the pro-
verbial luck of members of his own Cabinet in
securing well-paid positions to retire into when
the electors have no further use for them. Be-
sides, he would be the only one in the running—
as neither Want, O'Connor.. Pilcher, nor Barton
will take judgeships.
At latest, George Dean, the North Shore
wife-poisoner, was in Goulburn Gaol. He expects
his discharge at the end of seven years ; and is
likely to get it, as most authorities agree that the
sentence of 14 years would have been upset had
prisoner had the means of appealing to the Privy
Council. Dean has now served over four years.
Foster Rogers, the man who troubled last
Vic. Assembly with his Wealth Tax scheme,
seems to have buiot forth again in the form of a
sixpenny magazine. The first number of “ The
Commonwealth ” published in Melb., the other
day, contained four weighty items by Foster
Rogers, and the facsimile of a post-card written
to the same Rogers by Gladstone. So it is clear
to the proverbial blind man that the amusing
Rogers intends to “ put up ” for the Federal Par-
liament.
The daring printer ! S.A. Governor Ten-
nyson has lately presented Adelaide Library with
all that could ever be found of the original MS.
of the Dedication in which, in ’5l, the poet ac-
knowledged the gift of the Laureateship—“ the
laurel greener from the brow,” &c. The only
verses are the 2nd, sth, 6th, and Bth ; and the
MS. shows that it was the printer and not the
bard who numbered the stanzas. Probably the
poor man had just been “ setting ” an auction-sale
catalogue.
Governor Beauchamp’s front name is
William. The N.S.W. public has already decided
that it can’t call him “Bill,” or “Billy -as it
certainly would if he had a square jaw, owned
“horses” and smoked a briar-root. But it has
not yet finally made up its mind whether Jd.Fx.
is to be William or Willie. At present it looks
rather like Willie.
A certain influential Rockhampton citizen
called upon R.C. Bishop Higgims during his
recent stay in that city, and when Dean Slattery
came out to say the bishop was engaged, he found
the caller cuddling one of the hotel maids. Oh,
if that's what you want his lordship about, said
the Dean, “come on over to the church and 111
fix you up myself.”
Earl Beauchamp will lay the foundation-
stone of a new C. of E. building at Nowra
(N.S.W.) on 22nd inst. Has any N.S.W.
Governor laid a church stone since Gov. Mac-
quarie laid that of St. Mary’s on 20th Oct., 1821?
The same Governor had previously laid the
foundation-stones of St. Andrew’s, Sydney, and
St. Matthew’s, Windsor.
The hairy pastor Bevan has gone off again
—to America this time, to attend a Congregational
Congress. Within the last three years he has
made two prolonged tours in England and M.L.,
and his flock begins to wonder what it pays him
£lOOO a-year for. Apparently for having a rollick-
ing good time and doing his best to keep up his
resemblance to Henrick Ibsen.

Ex-Gov. Henry Norman visits M‘llwraith
pretty often, so London letters tell, and the two
argue the Federation question for all it’s worth.
The old Governor, who was always a clean-handed,
honorable warrior, is an out-and-out Federation-
ist, but his opinions have been very little quoted
in Bananaland. They’re too busy picking up
Barlow’s and Unmack’s pearls of wisdom up
Queen-street way.
The Tweed and Brunswick Advocate
(Murwillumbah, N.S.W.) is now run by two
partners rejoicing in the names of .Lean and
Luney. But things might be worse: sometimes
the poor country editor is both.
John Moffatt, pioneer of copper-mining
in N.Q. and of tin-mining at Herberton, is a
curious combination of oddities. Taken in trie
rough, he wears the belt as the best all-round
mining man in Australia. Probably not as
big a speculator as Lansell ; he does more
good with his money. Moffat opens new
fields, and Lansell, after his sad experi-
ence in that direction, confines him-
self to th>* further development of
old ones, 9-10ths of his money go-
ing into Bendigo mines and
much more coming out of them,
by the way. Moffat leaves
Brokenhillionaires behind and
Mt. Morganaires standing at the
post—in fact, the latter are too
miserable a mining crowd to be
reckoned in the same category
with Moffat. Originally a store-
keeper at Drake, N.S.W., then
a tin-buyer and smelter in the
N.E. districts, he afterwards es-
tablished tin-smelting works at
Herberton. Opened up the tin-
mines for many miles around,
then opened Chillagoe some 10
years ago, and subsequently Mt.
Garnet; in both of which enter-
prises he still retains very large
interests. Some nine years ago
he sold Montalbion mine in
England—cash consideration,
.£27,000. Two shafts had been
sunk a considerable distance
apart in the property, and the
lode being of same size and
quality in each, the assump-
tion was that it would be
found likewise between. Work
proved otherwise —each shaft,
it turned out, was sunk on a
pipe of ore, instead of in a con-
tinuous lode, leaving next to
nothing between. Moffat
handed back the £27,000, to the amazement of the
English speculators, who must have nearly died
from shock. His friends swore at his folly, but
Moffat vvas adamant, and the money was re-
turned. A hobby of his is equipping and sending
out prospecting parties all over N.Q., giving each
man a share in his discoveries. Many have taken
advantage of his enterprise in this direction. He
is also fond of sending veteran Scotchmen on a
trip ’Onie. One might travel a long way in
North Queensland before finding a man who
begrudges John Moffat his huge fortune as the
founder of the Chillagoe and Mt. Garnet groups
of copper-mines.
Dr. Breton, who lived in turn in Queens-
land, S.A., N.S.W., and Vic., and did good sur-
gical work in all, has died at the age of 71. He
was son of Colonel Breton, who had chaige of
Tasmania’s soldiery and bulked large in early Tas-
manian history. The doctor leaves a family of
eight, two of whom reside in N.S.W.
John Colton, aged 76, one of S.A.’s ex-
Premiers, is, at time of writing, dangerously ill.
John, who is very much like General Booth in
appearance, is one of the pillars of S.A. Method-
ism, and was known in politics as the “ holy sad-
dler.” He was an ordinary politician, but he gave
Kingston his first Ministerial billet in 1884.

M‘C. and F. to report on various Cresswellian
matters, particularly as to the notorious tattoo-
marks without which no long-lost Roger could be
genuine. The medical examiners, men of the
highest surgical and personal repute, reported
that Cresswell had no tattoo-marks on the arms,
nor any marks or scars indicating the obliteration
of tattoo-marks. Also, they reported him to be
two inches shorter than the “ real Sir Roger.”
“ J.A.C.” : Having known Ricciotti Gari-
baldi well, let me supplement your par. of 29/7/’99.
Ricciotti landed in Melbourne, over 20 years ago
(under a pseudonym), with his wife (Irish) and
family. His first employment in Australia was
lumping coal on the Melb. wharves. Next tried
the drapery business, and then received a tem-
porary engagement as extra-secretary in the Ser-
vice Govt. We next find him engaged in construct-
ing a colossal incubator, capable of hatching 500
doz. eggs at a time (the story goes that it exploded,
with great slaughter); then he was a pastry-cook,
and afterwards a fishmonger. All failures ; be-
cause he selected always those enterprises for
which he was specially ■unfitted. It may have
been forgotten that during the Franco-Prussian
war, at the battle of Dijon, he captured the only
flag lost by the Germans during the campaign.
During the recent demolition of Dunedin
(M.L.) gaol (the first of its kind built in the South
Island of M.L.) to make room for the new
Supreme Court, it was found necessary to disin-
ter the bones of three murderers who had been
hanged and buried in the gaol yard. These were
duly dug up and left about promiscuously, whilst
awaiting authority for removal to the “ conse-
crated” kirk-yard.” Meanwhile, someone stole the
skull of one of the hanged—Captain Jarvey.
Jarvey was one of the first men executed in Otago.
He poisoned his wife under particularly revolting
circumstances, and the poor victim was at the last
fully cognisant of her husband’s guilt. She con-
fided in her daughter, pledging her to secrecy, and
the gallant captain might have escaped but for
too early shaping his course for another petticoat.
The daughter resented it, gave him away to the
law, and now, when Gabriel toots his horn, the
captain will have some difficulty in picking up his
moorings without his “top gear.”
Mr. Edward Priestman, indefatigable bar-
racker for the alleged nobleman in Parramatta
Lunatic Asylum, has published a book entitled
“ The Tichborne Mystery. On the Verge of Solu-
tion,” which it certainly isn’t. All the same, Mr.
Priestman’s carefully compiled budget of facts,
statements and theories will nearly convince any
old student of the Tichborne Case that Roger
Tichborne didn’t get drowned at sea, but came to
Australia in the original ship “Bella,” said
“Bella” having been unlawfully converted into
the “Osprey ”en route. Granting the probability
of Roger’s landing a,t Melbourne and subsequent
acquaintance with sundry Australians who knew
him under various names, the impossibility of the
supposed Tichborne being identical with the
lunatic known as Cresswell in Parramatta Asylum
remains almost as strong as ever. It seems more
likely that the mystery surrounding Cresswell has
been created by the Asylum doctors, who were
unfavorable to the frequent demands for his
release.
Chief-Justice Griffith is quietly moving in
Queensland law circles for the abolition of the
barristerial wig and gown. Among, the leading
men in the profession the opposition is small, but
the dimmer lights blink sulkily and threaten to
splutter.

Aboriginalities.
They were on strike, and squatter Baijove
thought to starve them into submission by stop-
ping rations. Next day he strolled into the hut
and peered into an oil-drum hanging over the
fire. The oil-drum contained two fine legs of
mutton with a quantity of pig-weed on top.
Afterwards he said to the storekeeper: “It’s no
use stoping the wations; the bwutes are eating
gwass.”

“ Splur ” : Have a bull-terrier and a
black-and-tan. The latter is much given to bury-
ing his food, and I once saw him carry a piece of
meat around for some time, until the other dog
hid behind the tank. Then the b. and t. dug I
hole and buried the meat, but, noticing the bull-
terrier foxing him, promptly dug it up again and
carried it into the garden. The bull terrier can-
tiously followed, and hid between the rows of
peas, and the other dog, fancying the coast clear,
once more inhumed the meat and departed
whereupon bully sneaked out, rooted up the
spoil, and devoured it.

“Con.”: Be Grabben Grullen methods of
exchange. Got a job once prospecting for spuds
for a dilapidated cockey ; and not having struck
payable potatoes within the fortnight he paid me
off with a pig, and I left with my booty in a
sugar-bag. Called at Jones’s half-way pub. and
“cashed” my porker, getting a “ bonmve ” pig,
a blue shirt, and a headache in change. On
breasting the bar next morning the blear-eyed
Mrs. Jones said, “Now, you bummer ! —you
have squandered your own pig and the bonnive
and run three “ slips ” into debt besides, so clear
out as soon as you like ! ” I took the road again,
thirsty and pigless.
* * * *
What the evaporation is in a dry season in
the Australian way-back is shown by the figures
of the Broken Hili Water Supply. On June 30 of
last year the reservoir—a creek dammed back-
contained by measurement 1,966,482,000 gallons.
On December 31 it contained 1,157,491,000 gal.—
808,991,000 gal. less. Yet only 87,482,000 gal. had
been supplied to consumers. The loss was thus
more than eight times greater than the quantity
sold. And that is not allowing for the couple of
showers which reached the reservoir, either. The
loss would probably amount in an average year to
sft. of water.

“Ben Sun”: The fag-end of the road v.
rail competition in Yic. is a grim scarcity of work.
Teamsters travel nights and Sundays (several
passing now, 12.30 a.m.) and have to cringe to
storekeepers for the job. Two years ago these
men used to swear from their load tops at
sprightly, willing horses ; now, most of them
crawl beside, and flog, skeletons. What cruelty
for a crust! —and when is the promised prosperity
coming along ?

“N. 8.”: Our little township of Bear Hill
(N.S.W.) was recently thrown into a flutter of
social excitement over the marriage of Algernon
Norman Biljim Smithers to Miss Virginia Hogg.
The bridegroom is a well-known and widely-re-
spected ’possum-trapper. The ceremony took
place at the local Anglican Church, which was not
prettily decorated for the occasion. The bride-
groom was tastefully attired in a pair of new
brown canvas shoes, white socks (also new), old
grey moles, a soft shirt of indistinguishable color,
and a white tie. The latter was whispered to be
a present from the bride. He had discarded his
characteristic cardigan for a vest and bob-tailed
coat, borrowed for the occasion from a friendly
miner. When the ceremony was concluded the
happy couple returned to the bride’s late resi-
dence ; her luggage, consisting of a tin dish, an
old oven, and some parcels of clothes, was packed
on a wheel-barrow ; and the happy pair started on
foot over heavy roads for the bridegroom’s heredi-
tary humpy, seven miles away in a sylvan dell.
A number of well-wishing friends accompanied
them nearly two miles on the way and played
operatic selections on a horn, nine pairs of tin
plates, and seven kerosene-tins. The affairfis re-
garded among society people as the most pictur-
esque event of the kind that has taken place in
the district.

Memo, for the author of “Hymns, Ancient
and Modern ” :
MUSIC’S FATAL CHARMS.
THE TUNES THAT KILLED THE CATERPILLARS.
[DAILY MAIL TBLHGRAM.]
Nmv York, Monday.
American farmers are reported to have just discovered
that music is an important aid to agriculture. For the
past two months orchards throughout the East have been
suffering from a plague of caterpillars, which have been
defying all attempts at extermination. A few days ago,
a farmer’s wife in the Catskill district happened to blow
a horn under an apple tree, and was astonished to see
showers of the caterpillars fall to the ground. The news
spread through the town. A band was hired to play
lively tunes in the afflicted orchards. The effect was that
the trees were speedily cleared of caterpillars, which
were piled in heaps and destroyed. The farmers are now
buying cornets, trombones, and drums, or employing
bands to assist in fighting the pest. Hundreds of organ-
grinders are hurrying from all the surrounding districts
to grind the caterpillars to their doom.

“ Aloombah ” (Q.) : The aboriginals up
here make blankets from the inner bark of the
“kurrajong.” It is stripped and pounded with
stones until it becomes very pliable. These
blankets are very warm.

“ Cecil F.” : Ever seen a bull-frog smoke?
Lost ten dollars in odds against the event once in
Penang. Modus operandi: Open
frog’s mouth, insert end of lit
cigarette, and the animal, in
the effort to swallow air (frog’s
way of breathing) will inhale
and then £ hale smoke. Quaint
to see a big fellow squatting on
the table, puffing away and
making no attempt to “clear”
—the novelty of the situation
apparently paralysing him.

Rev. Chalmers vigorously
denies that all the Papuan tribes
deserve the appellation of
savages. Many of them (he
asserts) are well up in the scale
of civilisation, living in settle-
ments and practising agricul-
ture. Mr. Chalmers also draws
attention to the extraordinary
length of the houses of the Fly
River natives. One of these
measured 692 ft. in length, and
formed the residence of many
families, each of which had a
section assigned it. Com-
munism with a vengeance !

An aboriginal recently paralysed a
N.S.W. country bookseller by asking for a
ballroom-guide. Explained that he had
come from Gippsland, and was teaching the
local darkies the “poetry of motion.”
“ Pukaki ” : There is probably greater
race-diversity in Maoriland than in any other pro-
vince save Mongrelia. To begin with, Maoris
intermix freely with the whites. Plenty of men,
especially army officers and native Judges, have
married Maori princesses, owners, in their own
sweet right, of thousands of broad acres. (Strange
to say, no missionary seems to have thought of
this dodge.) The most considerable foreign ele-
ment, of course, is the Austrian, who muster
some 5000 on the gumfields. At Akaroa there is
a considerable French population. Capt. Stanley
(brother of the Dean), who brought about the
annexation by Britain, was a bit too “previous ”
for the French ; but, as it was, two shiploads of
Breton emigrants were landed. Half of Akaroa
town is still occupied by them. There are large
German settlements in Nelson and Taranaki.
(The German church at Upper Moutere is the
most picturesque building in M.L.). Norsewood
and Dannevirke districts are given up to Scandi-
navians, who, like the Germans, date from Public
Works Policy era. In Taranaki are large num-
bers of Poles, locally known as “squareheads.”
Writer once taught a school at which were three
English children, ten Poles, three Italians, several
Germans, and a couple of Swedes. Could
manage only by carefully sandwiching nationali-
ties at the seats. Then, as Italians couldn’t speak
Polish or German, and vice versa, the youngsters,
while in school, had to talk English to one
another. Then, of course, there are the Chows,
chiefly on the goldfields. In Wellington, how-
ever, they have practically monopolised the retail
grocery trade. Lastly, in the scows that trade
round Auckland, there are a fair number of Fin-
nish sailors.
“ Ducks ” : lie that story (8.,
8/7/’99) of sailor falling from foretopgal-
lant-yard without much injury. Knew
a man to fall from the spanker-gaff on to
the poop. “ Hello ! ” said the skipper,
“ where the are you from ? ” “ Liver-

made a smart passage. Are you hurt ? ”
“No, sir!” “Go to the steward and
get a glass of rum.” The man got his

on him as if having the drink was the only
unusual part of the business.

“ G. 8." : A recent Melb. lecturer
on the Transvaal difficulty said that the
elder Boers regard the younger as degene-
rate “ because they take off their boots
when they go to bed.” Recalls an old-
time squatter who used to go to bed fully
booted and spurred. In a weak moment,
he got married, and, getting drunk at the
incidental festivities, turned into the
nuptial couch with his usual equipment.
Then he dreamt a dream, kicked out
vigorously and lacerated his tender
bride with his spurs. Of course there
were ructions, and, as he used to say, with a
regretful sigh, when telling the story, “From that
time out, sir, I’ve had to wear a Ulanky night-
gown ! ”
* m 9 m
The tragedy of a thin Chinaman. At
Hokitika two youths sat in a sportive fashion on a
very thin Chinaman and he died. Medical
evidence was to the effect that “ deceased was
exceedingly thin, had scarcely any fat upon his
body, and were anybody to sit on him violently or
with much pressure the result would probably be
peritonitis.” Died, in fact, through having his
unprotected interior economy sat upon. A trial
for manslaughter is impending, and the West
Coast is only going to sit on stout Chinamen ip
future.

“Jonathan”: Be pulling mutton : birds
out of holes. They are also caught at end of
season by stretching great lengths of net along
the beach. The birds make for the water, and
being too fat to fly fall helplessly against the
net. Trenches are sometimes used to intercept
them. Oats (tiger and native) are as much
dreaded by the catchers as are the snakes.


One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia —are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. ha ve now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

Scene : Queensland political meet-
ing. Squatter candidate holding forth.
Voice from the crowd: “Yes, but what
we want to know is—What are ye goin’ to
do for the wurrukin’-mahn ? ” Candidate :
« Well, I am prepared to vote for any well-
considered measures tending to the social
improvement of ’’ Voice : “ Yes, but
what are ye goin’ to do for the wurrukin’
-mahn?” Candidate : “If an Eight-Hour
Bill is brought forward I am prepared to
give the matter my most serious considera-
tion, and any measures tending to the
social improvement of” Voice:
“That’s all right ; but what are ye goin’
to do for the wurrukin'-mahn ? ” Candi-
date : “ If the Government is prepared to
bring in a Bill tending to maintain the
present rate of wages, without at the
same time dislocating the industries of
the country or trenching upon the just
rights of the employer, or is inclined to
otherwise promote the social improvement
of ” Voice : “ But, look ’ere, the thing
we want to know about is just this—what
are ye goin’ to do for the wurrukin’-
mahn?” Candidate’s carefully - acquired
political platitudes fall from him like a
garment and he relapses into plain bullock-
language: “I’m prepared to give the
blasted working man 20 bob a week and
his tucker, and if the blasted working
man doesn’t like it I’ll kick him off my
dash dash dashed property, and follow
him ten blanky miles with a gun to see
that he doesn’t fire the grass. And if
lie copies back after that I’ll darn well get him
three months hard labor. I’m prepared to do all
that for the working man. There’s nothing mean
about me when once I get started.”

* * * *
Westralian gins have a unique way of
adorning themselves. Now and again the bucks
go hunting, accompanied, of course, by their
womenfolk, who, as soon as an animal is killed,
saturate their heads with its blood, and then
twist their hair into rat-tails and let it dry.

Contents of deceased Barcoo-tourist’s
“portmanteau”: Set of dice (faked), Queensland
railway-guide, three fish-hooks, shirt, moles, oil-
stone, sewing-gear, rifle (Barcoo). All rolled in
squatter’s tweed, roll strapped at each end, and
towel tied to straps for a sling. Correct caper!
Swag made into a long roll, doubled, ends tied
together and worn military-fashion, shows pro-
vincialism.

A Chicago man has in-
vented a “ snoring-gag,” con-
sisting of a small pad to be
placed in the mouth, and se-
cured by a strap to prevent its
being swallowed. Writer once
saw a clothes-peg placed on a
snorer’s nose in a bush pub. Stopped
the music, but woke the performer, who
thereupon “performed” otherwise upon
all within his reach. But he slumbered
not again.

Alex. M. : Be often-expressed sur-
prise of those whites who have tasted

there is between reptiles and fishes in the

Selector Tretheway’s cow at Tomin-
gley, near Dubbo, died suddenly, and, on
being opened, it was found that a hat-pin,
Gin. long, had pierced the heart. The vet.’s
opinion is that the cow picked up the pin
with a mouthful of grass.

Sunday in the bush. Dinner over,
and Marion, arrayed in a riding-habit for
a canter, when Ah Mooey, the Chinese
storekeeper from neighboring town, rides
up, and Marion perforce has to “ get
Mister Ah Mooey some dinner.” She
dumps the beef and cold potatoes on to
the table, slops out a cup-and-saucer of
tea, and is making off, when her hospitable
and observant mother remarks: “ Marion, set a
knife and fork for Mr. Ah Mooey.” Marion,
bawling from the back-yard, “ Oh, get the yellow
cow two bloomin’ sticks ! ”

The Union Co.’s s.s. Ohau. which foun-
dered with all hands some weeks ago during a
fierce gale in Cook’s Straits, had 14,145 ft. of
timber loaded on her decks when she left Grey-
mouth on her last voyage. The court of in-
quiry decided that the deck-load was in no
way responsible for ?he Ohau’s disappearance.
Now it would be interesting to hear from some
court which is prepared to say that it is really
better for a vessel to have a deck-load than not—
makes her safer, in fact.
AN ARM-AUXILIARY BIKE MOVEMENT.
What appears to be a noteworthy modifi-
cation in bike-structure is the invention of Mr.
Archibald Forsyth, of Sydney; who, being medi-
cally ordered to cycle for fat-reducing purposes,
and finding that his legs were not equal to pedal-
ling 18£ stone up-hill, had his machine furnished
with a wincli-like contrivance (as seen in accom-
panying picture), which, being geared to the
pedals, allows the arms to take a share in the
propulsion.
From Denmark, last season, J. Bull bought
1,485,336 cwt. of butter; from France, 390,144;
Sweden, 279,441; Holland, 269,680; Victoria,
145,358 ; Maoriland, about 100,000 ; N.S.W.,
41,703; S.A., 3312; Q., 2865—t0 say nothing of a
few score shiploads from U.S., Ireland, Canada,
Finland, &c., &c.
Apropos the effect of Mt. Lyell smelter-
fumes, this extract from a paper read recently at
the meeting of Tas. Royal Society. The view is
supposed to be from Mt. Huxley:
The great volume of smoke from the smelters at
Queenstown was seen streaming to the S.W., assuming a
long, narrow band-like form, and stretching, as it
seemed, right through to Heemskirk, where over the
ocean it separated into two distinct arms or canopies
east and west from the main stream. It was a most
peculiar occurrence, and unless I had seen it I could
hardly credit a volume of smoke travelling intact for
such a great distance without dissipation.

“Scotty W.” : In “Ziska,” Marie Corelli
makes “ one of the most glorious of full moons”
rise about midnight. Always thought full moon
rose about 6 p.m. [Mistakes about such masters
are common with much more considerable writers
than the shallow and ill-informed Corelli. Full
moon must rise at sunset, because her “ fullness ”
is due to the fact that she is at this time in “ oppo-
sition.”]
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

A. McCallum : According to the P.M.-G.’s answer to
Affleck, M.L.A., there is newspaper postage in Sydney,
just as The Bulletin stated—and the very exorbitant
postage of Id., too. It costs Id. to send a paper from
Circular Quay to King-street Q 4 mile), and nothing to
send it to Albury(3Bo miles). A Victim : What earthly
difference does it make ? You take a ticket in a 100,000
sweep, and, if it fills, you have one chance in 100,000 of
the big prize. If it closes at 50,000, the prize is only half
as large, but you have twice as good a chance to win it.

The Philippine Curse is indeed coming
home to roost in the U.S.A. The returned troop-
ships brought home with them, among other germs
and diseases, a delightful sort of insect known as
the “ Kissing Bug.” because it always bites on the
upper lip. The bite is poisonous and unless
promptly treated is likely to cause death.
ROM HIS PECULIAR POINT OF VIEW.
“ Norra nosher word, M’ria — you're not beshide
yourshelf. ”
Slab of evidence given in a recent North
Melb. maintenance case by a young woman of 20,
mother of twins : —“ On Oct. 31st of last year
defendant enquired about a waggon which stood
in the right-of-way at her father’s house. The
same night he seduced her. As a result twins
were born on June 29th last. She never saw
defendant before 31st Oct., nor had she seen him
since until this morning.” Doubtful which to
admire most —the casual, negligent way in which
the young Australian female takes up the duties
and responsibilities of motherhood, or the calm
assurance and cool persistency with which that
interesting female identifies a man whom she had
seen once only, three-quarters of a year previously.


DO EXPLANATIONS EXPLAIN? — From the Scriptures.
Here you have an Ancient-Egyptian ex-Attorney-General trying to explain to a small boy the principle of the “screw” (of an Absentee Attorney-General).
“Why, seems to me,” says the small boy, “to quote an historical cablegram, ‘it’s worse’n ever.’”

Mr. Higgins remarked it had been his unpleasant duty to oppose
the bill, because he thought it unworthy of the people of Australia.
There was no organised opposition to the bill at the last referendum, aa
it was thought to be useless. The majority of 15 to lin fhvor of the
measure was not, however, obtained fairly. The poll did nob
express the true sentiment—the unbiassed federal feeling of s's' s'
Victorians. Certain illegitimate -and unfair devices s' / //
were used, to swell the vote in this colony. Had s' sty / / SSJ
the minority in this colony been treated to i s
fair play? He found in the Abo us of the / sSs s
15th of July an announcement that at the
request of the secretary of the Australasian
Federation. league the Premier had de-
cided to issue a gift to those who voted at
the referendum poll on the 27th, of July
in the shape of a memento card prepared
by the Government printer.
What was a gift to those who voted
for the bill would be an insult and a source
of grief and shame to those who voted
against it.

Tasmanian Assembly lately followed the
example of Maoriland, Victoria, and 8. A. by
passing a measure for the establishment of a State
Land Bank, but the House of Refuse —90 per
cent, of the members are private mortgagees—

A Maoriland Scot went “hame tae Embra,”
and returned to his province with a full Highland
outfit, costing .£500 in guid M.L. gold. It was
the oomplete accoutrement of a bold Scottish chief,
and all of the ancient time, comprising claymores,
phillabegs, skene - dhus, haggises, cairngorms,
Scots - wha’ - haes, sporrans, bannocks, ghillie-
oallums, and all the other raiment and iron-
mongery with which the warlike Scot adorned
himself when he marched “ ower the Border.”
But the envious Sassenach did not give him a
chance of parading his bravery in his adopted
home they seized it for debt along with his
other effects, and sold it in a brutal 19th century
auction-room for less than a tenth of its value.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

“ C.E.P.” to The Bulletin from Foots-
cray, Vic. :—
Re your inquiry about Thatcher’s songs, a friend of
mine has shown me a copy, printed in ’65, containing “The
Bullock Creek Picnic,” which your correspondent spoke
of, and describes the incident of the leeches and the dis
may of the ladies thereat. The scene is laid at Loch-
wood, near Bendigo, Vic. My friend does not care to part
with his book, but I have copied a couple of the verses.
There are seven altogether, and the tune is “ Kitty
Jones.”
They ducked and dived and floundered, and fche water
they did dash,
And then, just for amusement, themselves began to
splash.
The bottom was fine gravel, and the water very clear,
And Mrs. G. exclaimed with glee “ Oh, ain’t it stunning,
dear?” B
The gents, as I have said before, sat 'neath some shady
trees,
And as they kept upon the watch, enjoying the cool
breeze ;
But they started up in terror, for they heard a fearful
shriek
Proceeding from the ladies, as they bathed in Bullock
Creek.
Regardless of their dishabille, the spot they soon did
reach,
As one of them from off her leg was trying to pull a
leech ;
There were two upon her shoulder and a whopper on her
calf ;
And the gentlemen maliciously at them began to laugh.
Says Mrs. , “ For shame, you men ; retire again, I
pray.
And you, you nasty auctioneer, just turn your head
away,
And stay beneath the trees there till you hear another
shriek ” ;
And then she adds, “ I wished I’d never bathed in Bul-
lock Creek.”

“ The discussion on the Commonwealth
Bill was then dropped, and the House went into
committee on the Dog Bill.” —Westralian Parlia-
mentary Report.
The Commonwealth Executive will num-
ber seven members, with a total salary of £12,000;
the N.S.W. Executive numbers 10, with total
salary of over <£13,000. The knife !
The Federal .flag which Hobart Federalists
fondly hope will be adopted as the national rag of
the Commonwealth is a white expanse of calico
with the Union Jack in one corner, and the
Southern Cross in blue stars. Hie !
Mildura, whose front door looks out on
N.S.W. and whose back door opens in Victoria,
while S.A. may almost be seen from the side
entrance, put up a Federal voting record—Yes,
512; No, 2. And these two probably didn’t
mean it.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) ;
We favor elective Ministries. When we are compelled
to retire, which we will do with the best grace possible,
we will suggest that the House should elect our suc-
cessors, as they are empowered to do under the Consti-
tution.
Of Treasurer Turner’s surplus the increase
of probate-duty over the estimate provides no less
than £135,000. The rich men have died for their
Country-
True patriots these, for, be it understood,
They left their country for their country’s good.
The open satisfaction with which the
N.S.W. Leg. Council received the ex-Attorney-
General’s attack on the Labor-party in the matter
of the recent appointments to the Council should
about finish the present alliance between the two
extremes of N.S.W. politics.
N.S.W. Minister Lee says he gives a shill-
ing a day less for unskilled labor on contract work
in Goulburn district than in Sydney, because the
cost of living is greater in the metropolis. This
seems indirectly rather rough on the great
Foreign-trade theory. Free imports are alleged
to provide cheap food and clothing and other
necessaries. Sydney is the place where the free
imports come to, and, if living is dearer there than
in an inland district—well, why is this thus?
Desperate attempts are being made to
prevent the Commonwealth Bill from
going to the people in W.A. The
Groper landowners know that
three-fourths of the metropolitan
electors and nine-tenths of the
goldfields population are in favor
of immediate union, and so the
present Parliament, which is in
no sense representative, is being
worked for all it is worth to
thwart the aspirations of the ma-
jority. The Premier has turned
a complete somersault, to the dis-
appointment of Federationists,
but he is not the only pebble on
the,beach. Hackett, who “ runs ”
Forrest, is in turn controlled by
his partner, Charles Harper,
acting - Speaker, a highly- re-
spected agriculturist and fruit-
grower. Piesse, the ponderous
Groper who is Commissioner for
Railways, is another fruit-
grower, and openly admits that
his apples have brought
him in £7O and per ton, but,
under Federation, his price would
not exceed <£lo or <£l2. In all
probability Federation would not
make half the difference he im-
agines, but the scare is in. the bones of the
old Groper, who fancies change must mean ruin.
Throssell (Minister for Lands), Lefroy (of the
Mines), and Chief-Secretary Randell,are all big
landowners.
Those N.S. Wales M’sP. who so
worded the Payment of Members Bill that
the salary becomes a refund of expenses,
not wages for specific Parliamentary duties,
meet now their Nemesis when they want
their political letters franked. If the £3OO
a year be a refund, then it covers, or should
Cover, Parliamentary letter services.
Sacredness of majority rule is a cardinal
principle of N.S.W. Labor-party, yet on the
amendment upon the Federal Address (which'
amendment, if carried, would have shelved the
address at the very least), the following “ solids ”
broke away from their leader and voted against the
expressed wish of the 25,000 majority : —Griffith,
Sam Smith, Brown, Holman, Edden, Hughes,
Law. Which should be remembered when next
these statesmen uphold on the hustings the
nobility of the labor-platform.
Noticed : That the Secret Conference
Sydney D.T. now gives prominence to Federal
letters and places anti-Federal letters in obscure
corners.
It. D. Meagher, M.P., seems to make it
his mission in N.S.W. Parliament to follow W. P.
Crick (his former partner) in debate, and “deal it
out ”to him. Such tactics are, to say the least of
it, injudicious, for memories
are certain to be aroused
which the public, no
doubt, have allowed
to slumber. No one
particularly desires
to revive the past, or
makeany man’s pun-
ishment eternal, but,
if Meagher chooses
to revive it every
time Crick speaks in
Parliament, he has
only himself to blame
for the consequences.
In football, a man is
occasionally selected,
not for his play, but
in order to “ lay for ”
a skilful opponent all
day, but it ought not to
be tolerated in politics.
It has long been one
of the loud assertions of
the Sydney foreign-
traders that the increase
in the hands employed in
Vic. factories was mostly
an increase in female em-
ployes, thereby demonstrat-
ing the dry-rot|of Protection.
N.S.W. appears, on the same
principle, to have got the dry-
rot of Freetrade badly. The
number of hands employed in
N.S.W. factories in ’9B (52,518)
was 1079 more than in ’97; and
this increase was made up of 340
males and 739 females.
Bigjohn Forrest’s character as an honest,
if not too-brilliant, politician is getting some-
what blotted over the Federation business. The
latest expose places him in a particularly bad
light. Whilst the members of his Government
and other anti-Federalists were making a great
point of the S.A. Government’s “ ominous
silence ” with regard to, and therefore its pre-
sumed hostility towards, a trans-continental rail-
way through to Fremantle, John himself had in
his possession a letter from the S.A. Premier em-
phatically approving of the project. The matter
became public through a copy of the letter being
laid with other correspondence before S.A. As-
sembly. Forrest’s excuse for this mean decep-
tion was that he intended to use the letter when a
suitable opportunity presented itself. Most folks
would probably consider the “ suitable oppor-
tunity ” to be when everybody was demanding in-
formation about the matter.
IN “PUSH” SOCIETY.
MILKYO: “ Blokes is gettin’ too timid to take any
risks, football ain't what it useter, and steeple-
chases is gettin' quite ’armless nowadays.”
BUTGHERO : “ Give me a good old bike flutter, with
a lot on 'em laid out, and plenty of gore — that’s
wot I calls sport.”
Sydney Telegraph, in that column which
is sometimes signed “Outis” and sometimes
“Niemand,” lately rose up in bitter objurgation
against Old-Age Pensions. And when necessity
arises the paper will doubtless say that it isn’t
responsible for signed articles, just as it did when
its signed column declared that the last Federal
Conference must be held in secret, and then the
rest of the paper called on the people to reject the
Bill because it was a “ Secret Conference Bill.”
Why not have the whole paper signed, and then
nobody would be responsible for anything ?
The largest strike in Westralia up to date
is that of iOOO men employed in the timber in-
dustry at Yarloop. The infamous Truck system is
the trouble —as usual. The men’s case is that,
though their wages are good on paper, they are
compelled to make their purchases from the co.’s
stores at such exorbitant rates that the wages, in
practice, are very low. A proposal to reduce
them, without any corresponding reduction in the
price of stores, brought on the crisis. If Bigjohn
doesn’t put some more velocity and some more
real purpose into his Truck Bill another ten-inch
nail will soon be ready for his coffin.
PORTABLE PROPERTY .
BILL : “ Harry struck a patch marrying that girl .”
JACK : “ Why, has she got money? ”
BILL : “ Well, not exactly cash; but Harry tells me
that she has golden hair, sapphire eyes, ruby
lips, pearly teeth, ivory cheeks, silken lashes,
marble brow, and a silvery voice; so he ought
to get a few bob on her if ever he’s hard-up.”
While it still had a faint hope of stemming
the Federal tide, Sydney D.T. joined in the jibes
and sneers of the anti-Billite party at Premier
Reid’s batch of appointments to N.S.W. Leg.
Council in order to ensure the passage of the
Enabling Bill. Last week it strongly objected to
Jack Want’s strictures on the inclusion of Labor
nonentities in the list, and the difficulty it now
finds itself in must be pleasant reading to the
Premier, who was cute enough to see that these
appointments would some day disturb the
placidity of the Leg. Council and Labor-party
alliance.
Bigjohn Forrest returned from the Pre-
miers’ Conference an enthusiastic Federationist,
but when he found that his colleagues were mostly
hostile, he first cooled and then froze hard. Now
he probably begins to realise that he has wandered
into a very tight place. James’s motion demand-
ing that the Federal .Constitution should be
definitely referred to the people for acceptance or
rejection was defeated by 22 to 14. But the 14
represent more electors now than the 22, arid when
the re-distribution of seats comes along the Fede-
ral party will almost certainly be in a majority.
And yet the anxious John can’t bluff off that re-
distribution much longer.
Murray Smith’s recent sneer at Premier'
Turner’s long list of titles came with an envious
ring from a man who himself bears the somewhat
mangy decoration of C.M.G. Mangy, by reason
of the paltriness and absurd vanity of most of its
Australian wearers. All the same, Turner ex-
hibited the seams and stitches of his slop-built
personality when he waxed so indignant at Smith’s
jeer. His plodding, hard-working, determination
of the past few years, his great talents for admini-
stration, and his constructive abilities, have quite
obscured the fact that Turner rose into politics as
a suburban councillor, and will continue to own
the soul of a suburban councillor to his dying day.
The beastly ignorance of our well-meauing
fellow-man
Perth, Friday.—The fourth meeting of the Joint
Select Committee on the Commonwealth Bill was held to-
day, the press being admitted for the first time. The
committee was chiefly occupied in going through the
Bill, and selecting those clauses for future discussion to
which any member raised objection. The clause relating
to coinage, under whioh no State will be allowed to coin
gold, and which would mean the abolition of the Perth
Mint, was made special reference to.
The Commonwealth Bill says simply : “ A State
shall not coin money,” but that doesn’t neces-
sarily mean that the existing mints at Sydney,
Melbourne, and Perth shall all be broken up. It
probably only means that the Federal Govern-
ment will coin gold at those places. If the fact
that a State can’t coin money means the abolition
of the Perth Mint, then it is already abolished
and always has been. A State can’t coin money
now. The British Government sends out its
officer to coin money in the State, but that is
quite different. The Federal prohibition against
a State coining money is only a repetition of the
law which has been in force all along.
At a recent Brisbane Federation meeting
a man who said he was a carrier wanted to know :
“If all the trade of Brisbane goes South, how do
you expect me to make a living ? ” Answer from
the platform : “If all our trade goes South you
will be kept occupied for a considerable time
carrying it South. And when you have got it
there you will probably have to fetch it all North
again as back-loading."
S.A. Ministerialists are in danger of split-
ting over Household Suffrage. One section opposes
the extreme expedient of a dissolution if the Coun-
cil again blocks the reform, and the compromise
of a .£l5 suffrage is suggested. Premier King-
ston, backed solidly by the Labor-party, de-
clines to listen to the latter idea. Laborites
favor a dissolution, and claim that they can
thereby increase their strength.
Ar,ei-Federation “pome” from Brisbane
Telegraph:—
Say—No Federal powers shall fetter us if you firmly taka
your stand ;
We won’t have Federation here, we want freedom for
Queensland ;
Their promises are savory baits for all those who do not

Into the tempters’ snare don’t fall while still standing on

The exhortation in that last line is in the very
finest Telegraphese.
The decision of Victoria’s House of 48
Rich Landlords to no longer oppose the Bill for
placing the Assembly franchise on a one-man-one-
vote basis is the first definite good result of the
democratic Federal Constitution. The 48 Rich
Landlords have rejected that Bill continuously for
seven years or so. But having agreed that Vic-
toria should be bossed by a Federal Parliament
with both Houses elected on a one-man-one-vote
basis, they couldn’t well keep up any longer the
pretence of believing that one-man-one-vote was
“ destructive to the best interests of the country.”
So they changed front with all the grace of a long
crocodile getting hurriedly round a corner.
The left-off politicians of the East come in.
handy to preach anti-Federalism in the West.
Vosper of Queensland, Mint-Sauce Jones of Vic-
toria, Tommy Walker of N.S.W. are three speci-
mens. The East is so dead tired of Jones and
Walker that there isn’t a river from the Tweed
right round to the Glenelg that it wouldn’t be
pleased to see both of them fall into any day.
And the West, which professes to scorn the folks
from t’other side, employs these political wrecks
as the shining lights of local provincialism. If
the W.A. provinoialists can’t raise any brighter
lights of its own than Jones and Walker, it must
be as dim as the horn lantern of bygone days.
Tas. Assembly has decided to perpetuate
itself for six months beyond its term, or as much
of the six months as may elapse before Federation
is fixed up. Members will draw no salaries for
the extra term, but will retain all other Parlia-
mentary privileges, and will not meet to transact,
business unless in case of extreme urgency during
the period of extension. When the first Federal
Parliament is elected Tas. Legislature is to be cut
down by a non - political commission to 32
Assembly members and 16 Leg. Councillors. All
this dislocation of the blessed Constitooshun to
avoid the unpleasantness of having to ask newly-
elected M’sP. to abolish themselves voluntarily.
The N.S.W. Colonists’ Anti-Bill League—
the affair which ran the great anti-Federal agitation
in N.S.W.—has been settling its accounts. One
unfortunate proprietor of a little country paper to
whom ss. was owing received this letter: —
Referring to the account rendered to the Colonists
Anti-Convention Bill League we beg to say that we have-
in hand for distribution a sum which will allow of our
sending you the enclosed cheque for 2s. 6d. in settlement.
If this be not agreeable to you, please return cheque. !
There was 6d. exchange to pay on the cheque (the
league hadn’t even the grace to save the struggling
archimedean lever the loss of 20 per cent, of his
div. by sending stamps in place of the dignified
cheque), so the proprietor got 25., less 2d.', for
posting account. And people wonder why some
country editors Drink !
The complaint of one Naseef Habib, of
Lebanon, and some other brown men from the-
land where the cedars used to grow, that they
were denied letters of naturalisation, raised a dust
lately in S. A, Assembly. . John Downer pleaded
on behalf of Habib and co. on the irrelevant
ground that they came from the land which pro-
duced “ Christ and Homer ” —which was espe-
cially irrelevant seeing that Christ came from the
Jewish land of Judea, and no one knows
what land produced Homer. A Labor-member
promptly suppressed old John by asking if it was
good enough to let in all the tag-rag and bob-tail
of Asia Minor on the off-chance of catching
another Homer. Probably the best compromise
would be to pass an Act that any yellow or brown
or black poet who stirs the soul of S. A. with his
lyre shall be naturalised right off.

The mining boom, which has recommenced since
the Boer scare comparatively died down, has distinctly
benefited the Sydney Safe Deposit. Brokers and buyers
alike see the folly of keeping valuable scrip either in office
safes or private residences, and there have been large
numbers of visitors to view the massive strength of the
Deposit vaults, with a proportionate increase in the
number of clients. We are' requested to state that the
management freely invite a visit. The Deposit is situated
in Ash-street (back of Palingand Co’s), off George-street.*

Government Notices.
ÜBLIO SERVICE BOARD.
50 Young-street, Sydney,
'23rd May, 1899.
A COMPETITIVE,EXAMINATION of Candidates (male
and female) for ADMISSION to the CLERICAL
DIVISION of the PUBLIC SERVICE of the COLONY
will be held in Sydney, and at various country centres,
on the 18th December next and four following days.
Candidates will be allowed to attend who are not less
than 16 years of age on the first day of examination, or
not more than 21 years of age on the 31st July, 1899.
Persons already employed in the General Division of the
Public Service will, however, be admitted to the
examination provided their age does not exceed
thirty years. Application to attend the examin-
ation must reach the undersigned not later than
the 20th November. The signatures of persons wil-
ling to superintend the examination held in centres out-
side the Sydney radius should be forwarded to the Secre-
tary of the Board of Examiners, 7 Richmond Terrace,
Domain, Sydney (from whom forms for the purpose may
be ob(ained), not later than 20th November, 1899.
Further particulars respecting the examination, rates
of pay, &c., may be obtained on application to the office
of the Public Service Board.
The regulations regarding the examination, and copies
of the papers set on the last occasion, can be obtained at
the Government Printing Office and the principal book-
sellers.
By order of the Board,
J. \Y. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

During the week The Bulletin office was
brightened by the arrival of an advance copy of
the twelfth edition of the Australasian United
Steam Navigation Co.’s handbook of information
for 1899-1000. The handbook is liberally starred
with interesting illustrations of the chief cities of
this great country, also some maps, and the 172 | p.
are filled with interesting information that could
only be acquired otherwise by a tremendous lot of
walking and searching. And life is so short that
the handbook saves a lot of valuable time and fits
nicely into the long-felt want since last year’s copy
went astray.*
