FULLNESS OF BODY
Is a desirable thing when applied to whisky, and
that is one of the characteristics of “ D.C.L.,” a
fine, well-matured, mellow blend of the finest
Highland Whiskies—ask for the “D.C.L.”
(Black Bottle).*
Yic. Council’s resolve to pass the One-
man-one-vote Bill spoils the favorite gag of a
rather liberal-minded member. Asked at ban-
quets to pass the mustard, &c., &c., he always
replied with dignity : “Sir, we pass nothing in
the Legislative Council ! ”
“At Casino, N.S. W., Rev. Schurr was
presented with a purse of over 100 sovs., subscribed
in small amounts by people of all denominations
in various parts of the district, on his appointment
as Vicar-General of the diocese. In the pioneering
days, the abbe did a great deal to assist the early
settlers.” The present (writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent) represents but a very minute fraction
of the good gold given away by the good abbe to
all classes and creeds during his exile in the Big
Scrub. It won’t be long before the purse-contents
are distributed among the abbe’s poorer parish-
ioners—and stragglers of other denominations and
of no denomination. A truly grand old man,
“ whose house is known to all the vagrant train,”
who shares his scanty meal with the swagman at
the roadside, and who even in his 73rd year has
the energy and heart to travel through the bush
scores of miles at night to attend the sick. The
Abbe, who comes of a distinguished family, is an
Alsatian by birth, and was formerly Frofessor
of Languages at Maynooth Col'ege, and afterwards
at the Catholic University, Dublin.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
What Australian champion-trotter Fritz would
have done under Yankee conditions of track and handling-
will never be known, but there is small doubt he would
have been little, if any, behind the best of them. At
the recent Forbes (N.S.W.) show he covered a mile in
2min. 14sec. Fritz is probably as easy a racing trotter to
drive as ever stood between shafts. He knows no gait but
the trot, and cannot be driven to a break ; the nearest
approach to a break he was ever known to make was in
his maiden race at Kensington (Sydney), when he jumped
over a broad white-wash line which had been marked
across the track. Writer saw Mr. Buckland drive Fritz a
mile in 2.1G holding the reins in one hand and smacking
the wonder on the rump with the other.
• • * •
A. J.C. people and clubs generally are seeing to
it that Ikey Mo’s existence is not the bed of roses it used
to be. He’s being knocked about from post to pillar and
is compelled to go through a lot of form now ere being
allowed to bet. Laying the odds at pony-meetings or
having the remotest connection with anyone who does,
damns him in the eyes of the N.S.W. governing body.
Latest “infliction”; he must go to office of club and
“sign "or will not have the right to put the bag on.
Further, his clerk must pay a iicensing-fee every year,
and, worst of ail (to some), poor Ikey may not in slightest
degree be associated with a betting-shop under pain of
having his licence cancelled instantly. The two biggest
“shops” in Sydney have determined to keep on. One
belongs to the leviathan of the province, whose voice will
consequently be heard no more at Randwick or other
registered meetings. Shows what gold-mines “shops”
must be. Great weeping and wailing in the camp of the
leather-lunged just now.
* • * •
Since the days of Carbine, the Melbourne crowd
has never roared so heartily as when Domino came gal-
loping down the straight, at Caulfield, an easy winner of
the £1000 Steeplechase. The performance was not abso-
lutely wonderful—Redleap and Daimio are still ahead of
the Ballarat grey in brilliant records—but it was a fine
achievement by a horse that runs often and seldom fails
to get home. A good animal in the hands of a sportsman
who can afford to be honest is a fine satire on the policy
of what are called “clever” and “patient” owners.
Domino, within 12 months, has won eight races out of 11
attempts. He started his turf career in Aug.,’98, as the
hero of a Hunters’ Steeplechase, after which his owner,
Mr. George Russell, rode him three times over the big
sticks, winning twice and coming to grief the other time.
This was Domino’s only fall up to date. Later, he won a
Steeplechase at the Alelb. Cup meeting, and finished third
tothelightly-weighted Pirate, two days later. At the Aus-
tralian Cup meeting, last March, the genuine goer took two
prizes under very heavy burdens. Then he won the
£1000 steeple at Onkaparinga. Next time he appeared in
public Mysore beat him by half a head for the V.R.C.
Grand National, and last Saturday he gave his backers
another turn by winning the V.A.T.C. National with 13st.
51bs. on his grey old back. Neither Redleap nor Daimio
was raced sill the year round like the sturdy champion
from Ballarat. They were kept for winter coups, whereas
Domino has been picking up stakes and benefacting the
public all the time. A pity that there are no w.f.a.
jumping races for the benefit of boss jumpers. The turf
which a Domino kicks up. with his heels has a sweet
smell, and even Ikey Mo, disbursing the “quids” from a
shrunken bag, feels that for once in a way he is paying
his respects to a real good ’or»e.
• * • *
All cycling Melb. turned up at Caulfield on
Wednesday to see Gargurevitch, the racing cyclist, start
his career as a jockey. “Gargy” was understood to
have up his sleeve a Tod Sloan invention that would
revolutionise racing, but, after all the talk, he came out
on Wyalla for the Amateur Race with his mount saddled
up in the old familiar style. Used to steeply-banked
tracks and safe corners, “Gargy’’spun round the turns
in a manner indicative of supreme contempt of skidding;
but after getting a lead of 30 lengths at one time he fell
back and finished fifth. It is a point for the white-shirt
brigade of shamateur athletes to chew over, that Gargy,
the professional cyclist, is accepted without question as
a gentleman-jock.
• * * •
Bendigo (Vic.) Australian Cup, concluded Fri-
day last, was prolific in surprises. The most notable
were the unthought-of Nabeal’s performances. In first
round he put out Czardus, a 7-to-l-on “ certainty,” and
subsequently accounted for bottled-up Kilmainham and
his kennel mate, Dandenong Ranger, both hot favorites
and winners respectively of N.S.W. Waterloo Cup'and
Plate last June. (Two weeks ago a Melb. sporting paper
reported these hounds in the vet.’s hands, and sure ab-
sentees. They didn’t run as if sick, and more money
couldn’t have gone on ’em if they’d been at their best.)
Nabeal went down In deciding course to Gamecock, a
N.S.W.-bred dog by Gameboy from Luoilla.
* • • •
Expected that Chesney and Kiora will be
shipped for London within the next day or two. Former
was sold for 2000 guineas and an extra 500 first time he
wins. Not a bad bargain for Mr. H. Hawkins, who quite
lately got him for 1100 guineas. Surmised that “ Mr.
Jersey” (Mrs. Langtry) is the buyer. Apropos, Mrs. L.
is playing in luck with Australian nags. There was the
Goodwood double (Stakes and Cup), a week or two back,
won by Merman, and on Tuesday of last week same horse
got away with Birmingham handicap stakes, while, a day
or two before, Uniform appropriated Lewes Handicap
(1000 sovs.), which horses from this section seem to have
a weakness for. Chesney ought to do at least as well as
any of the others—he is strongly put together and sound
as a bell.
* • • •
Gozo, whoso stock have won .£12,795 during the
year ending July 31, heads the list of winning sires in
Australia, hut he has been closely pressed by Lochiel
(£12,209): the young sire. Bill of Portland, comes third
with £8320 (mainly won by Bohadil); the expatriated
Trenton and Carbine furnish fourth and fifth places with
£7807 and £6654 respectively. Malua closely follows
with £5989. At the end of the half-year (Jan. 31) Gozo
was easily first with £10,327, Lochiel being a bad second
with t 6829. Since then the above figures show that Gozo
has been “marking time," whilst the gallant son of
Prince Charlie has come with a “ Ilieland ” rush.
• * * *
The sire Glenloth, winner of the sensational
Melb. Cup of *92, was recently sold in Melb. for 65gs. In
that “ swampy ” year two of Mr. Forrester’s—Ronda and
penance—filled the second and third places. But-since
then, with Gaulus and The Grafter, the “squire” of
Warwick Farm has had sweet revenge.

In the Kent match, last week, Bradley and
Burnup were instrumental in the taking of 18 out of 20
Australian wickets. Their averages were Bradley' 8 for
81, and Burnup 8 for 51. Each of them made one catch
off the bowling of Hearne and Mason respectively, also it
happened that Bradley made two catches off Burnup’s
bowling.
• • • *
The final international football-match, England
v. Australia, on Sydney Cricket Ground on Saturday,
caused 10,000 enthusiasts to face heavy rain-squalls,
sloppy ground, and bleak weather. The home team
battled determinedly against a strong wind and blinding
rain in first half. Though the ball was in their territory
almost from the beginning, and in the 25 most of the
time, they stopped the Britishers from scoring. Time after
time Pastor Mullineux’s men came with an avalanche-
like rush, but the Australians braced themselves up and
hurled the opposing side back, while the shivering, shak-
ing mass of lookers-on warmed up and cheered again and
again. Wind and rain with them, it was expected that
the Australians would make a big show in second half,
but hard work through slush and mud had told its tale
on faulty condition, and the Englishmen seemed to play
better than at any time since their arrival. Once or twice
Australia seemed on the verge of scoring, but it was
only a flicker. 20mins. from the kick-off Cookson (Eng.)
secured a smart try, and Adamson converted beautifully :
s—nil. A free to the visitors followed, and Adamson
missed a hard shot at gaol (Gralton got his leg hurt here,
and play stopped 7mins.). Less than 60secs. later Bucher
scored splendidly'. Adamson converted : 10—nil. Boland,
“ off-side,” gave a free to England, but Adamson failed
in hisattempt, though immediately' afterwards he was
successful through a similar offence on GraltoiVs (Q.)
part—right under the bar almost. Full score : England
13, Australia nil. English play all round was exception-
ally good, and condition ditto. Throngs on the hills
loudly hooted referee Corr, without justification, and
crowds in the grand-stand applauded vigorously in re-
sponse. Gralton (Q.) was cautioned for losing his temper
and striking at one of the opposing side. The Aus-
tralians got demoralised towards the end.
• * * *
Rev. Mr. Mullineux chin-wagged again on
Saturday night at the Sydney farewell banquet to the
British footballers. His latest took the shape of a few
knife-thrusts for pin-pricks suffered. “Deliberate
breaches of the rules ” were laid at Australia’s door—
among others (1) “The trick of holding people back
when they are coming away from the scrum.” (2) “ Two
Australians (in the line-out) forcing an Englishman away
while a third Australian gets through with the ball.”
(3) “ Putting an elbow into a man’s face in the scrum.”
“I am told,” added the leather-chasing parson, “that
the Australian remedy for this is to bite the hand, but we
haven’t yet mastered the art of cannibalism.(4)
“Shouting after an opponent for a pass." “It is the
lowest thing I have ever heard of,” said the rev. gentle-
man—“ it is taking a mean'advantage.” Subdued mut-
terings from all parts of the room punctuated his rever-
ence’s speech, but the tact of chairman Calvert came to
the rescue. “ Gentlemen,” said he, “ Mr. is going to
try and throw a little harmony into the proceedings by
singing a song.’’ The other 21 of the 22 visitors are very
popular, but their skipper never caught on—he has been
in a chronic state of growl right through.
* * » *
The defeat of Essendon by Colling wood on
Saturday still further solidities the -prospects of Fitzroy,
which has now a clear lead of 7 points over every other
club. Which means that the ’Roys can lose one and draw
one match and yet be ahead, and as they are unlikely to
do either with the matches now in front of them, the
premiership of Vic. Football League seems practically
decided.
• * • •
St. Kilda football club is absolutely the most
consistent Australian athletic institution. It is now
three seasons since it has won a match ; during ’9B and
'99'it has scored a solid loss Saturday after Saturday, and
not even succeeded in drawing once ; last week it reached
the pinnacle of greatness by getting two points against
Geelong! The despised Football Association possesses
teams that could donkey-lick St. Kilda almost as Geelong
did, yet the superiah League declines io admit them to its
ranks on the supposed ground that they are not “class.”
* • * »
The middle-weight boxing-championship of N.S.
Wales is still in abeyance. Sydney police locked up both
Dunn and Conlon, last Wednesday, on a charge of “ being
about to engage in a boxing-match.” Ye gods! what
next? Members of gymnastic clubs, college boys and
others who “ put ’em on ” for trophies are not safe after
this. Dunn and Conlon were to go 20 rounds, and who-
ever had the surplus points then would have got the ver-
dict. Stipendiary Magistrate Isaacs bound them over till
next Quarter-sessions, saying he did so reluctantly ; that
boxing-matches should be stopped altogether or allowed
on proper lines—better course would be for police to
supervise, and step in if a dangerous stage was reached,
etc. When “ Chiddy ” Ryan and Jim Barron were, three
years ago, before the same magistrate, .on a similar
charge, he told the police that limited-round boxing-
contests were not illegal provided no stake was in
evidence and gloves were regulation size (8oz.). Autho-
rity was quoted in support; yet now an opposite view,
and with reluctance ! Why? Worth mentioning in this
connection that the 10-round boxing-match between
Lenton and Casey, at Golden Gate Club (Sydney), on
Saturday night, came off undisturbed, and drew 500
spectators.
* • • •
The heralded champion middleweight pug. of
Australia, Tim Murphy, reached Sydney from Tasmania
on Friday, and is simply spoilin’ for a fight. Tim’s latest
victim was Albury Clifford who (just returned from a
trip ’Ome with all the veneer of the old country upon
him) went under in 3J4 rounds. Murphy is a brother of
that mass of whipcord and wire “ Torpedo” Billy, whom
he resembles much in face and build, though a deal
taller. Writer recolleots “Tim ” playing Rugby for Vic-
toria against N.S.W., four or five years since ; also, heard
of Jim Quigley (since dead) saying then, after a trial
bout: “ That cove (Murphy) ’ll be a champion ’fore long.”
Quigley was a good prophet.
* * * *
In two rounds plump and plucky Fred Preston,
of Melb., succeeded in finishing off Joe Cullen, the Vic.
pug. who returned from England a few weeks back with
upturned pants and a curled stick on his arm. The fight
was willing and pretty while it lasted, but towards the
end of the second round Preston swept a left swing on
Cullen’s jaw that sent him down for considerably more
than his lOsec.

“ down under ” with Isyds. of a margin between himself
and next man (Lister), u'hoin Derbyshire, the previous
holder, couldn’t give two yards to ?

Mr. E. 0. Westlake, sub-editor of the
Australian Stab, whose hopeless illness was
chronicled in last week’s Bulletin, died before
this paper came out. He was one of the most
popular and capable of his craft, and had, before
joining the Star, ably served in turn Argus,
Age, and Sydney Daily Telegraph.
One of the crew of the Edina put the re-
cent disaster in a decidedly warning light when he
said: —No rule is observed between the Edina
and the Excelsior as to the side on which each
should pass in going to Geelong and returning.
We pass the Excelsior one day._on our starboard
side, and the next on our port side.” Under this
happy-go-lucky system there was bound to come
another day when the two boats didn't pass at all.
John Haynes’s crusade against the Federal
Bill received a shock on thedivision on his amend-
ment on the Address. Exactly 100 members were
accounted for when N.S.W. Assembly divided,
and but 23 voted with him; Crick and Lyne being
the only persons of any note on the side of the
ayes. Of the 25 absentees 20 at least would have
voted against the amendment, the only absolutely
certain “aye” being John Norton.
Tucker, who has a second time beaten the
O’Malley for a S.A. Assembly seat, could not
carry out all his campaigning owing to his father’s
death ; but in a land where sympathetic woman
votes, misfortune of that sort is likely to injure
the other candidate—as it is said to have done in
this case. Tucker, sen., was a young sea-dog
who reached the province in its flrst year, and
was aftervvards one of the party who made the
first soundings in the Port Adelaide “ river ” —as
the awful entrance to the “ harbor” is miscalled.
“Miles”; Authoritatively rumored that
the changes Premier Reid referred to in replying
to Parliamentary questions re Col. Bayly are that
Bayly is to be reduced to the rank of Major and
Deputy-Assistant-Adjutant-General, the present
D.A.A.G., Major Boam, taking Bayly’s position
as Colonel Commanding the purely Volunteer
N.S.W. Forces. Major Boam is one of the heavy
“ paper - warriors ” of Victoria Barracks, and
putting so affable a personage into Bayly’s position
practically means making the Volunteer Com-
mandants, who have made all the trouble, their
own bosses. With all his faults, Col. Bayly is
a good deal of a “soldier,” and he loses his billet
through trying to make soldiers of an absolutely
undisciplined collection of “influential” toy-
warriors. Oh, for Federation and a strong, re-
lentless officer as Commander-in-Chief !
Talk about the feuds of the Capulets and
Montagues and of the fine, healthy hatreds
between the gintlemen of Tipperary and Ulster ;
they are sweet friendships compared to the eternal
Norton - Black - Meagher vendetta. At the
squalid Open-letter case at Darlinghurst Court,
Sydney, it was a sight for the gods to see
Meagher sitting cheek-by-jowl with Norton.
Whenever an idea would strike “ the brilliant
young Australian ” he would lean over and ap-
parently pass some remark to counsel for accused,
and every time some hideous allegation would
crash down on the battered character of Norton
he (the brilliant) would smile, put his hands
together gleefully, and glance at the back of the
head Norton had turned to him. Further along
the delighted face of George Black was bent down
over a huge,pocket-book, while at the end of the
row Crick leaned his rippling and expressive
countenance against his hand. And Norton—he
sat with his brown coat-collar turned up and a
shivery look in his peepy eyes, sometimes trying
to affect an air of nonchalance, sometimes
feverishly taking notes and then jerkily
handing them to the Crown counsel. There
is a. whole, stack of inorals protruding from
this indescribably smellful case. One of them
is that there is a sort of journalist who
should never mouth the word “concupiscence”
about a political enemy until he (the journalist) is
94 years old and has allowed the public to
utterly forget his own personal adventures, as re-
lated by himself in the witness-box.

ring barricades,
Get the best trotting-horse in the land and the Viceroy

You have rivals! You’re anxious to reach ’em, and give
’em particular fits !
Weil, send down to Sydney for Beauchamp and send up
to Warren for Fritz I
If you want to do things pretty nobby, and show all the
shows what is what,
You must send for the pride of Wonbobbie to show them
how flyers can trot;
If you want to be kind to the ladies, send down for a
genuine Lord,
And show them how handsome an “aide" is when
dressed in a busby and sword.
If you want to make cash without trouble plank down
your last threepenny bits
On the only reliable double—the Governor Beauchamp
and Frit* 1

“ Stoker ” : No need to go" West for danc-
ing undertakers (B. 29/7/’99). In a Vie. town,
the local “planter” attends all the “hops” he
can, and was recently elected on a “ social ” com-
mittee, in place of another Terpsichorean, whom
he had buried. Being a single man, and well-in
also, “Coffins”' has no difficulty in getting girls
to dance with him'. The local paper’s report of a
recent shivoo mentioned that Miss So-and-so had
been taken in to supper by Mr. Hearse, and in
next column was an announcement to the effect
that Hearse, undertaker and embalmer, will be
pleased to wait on families for orders. Yes,
pleased ! ”
It had been raining a week as it only rains
in Maoriland, and the children had great hopes
that the teacher would quail at the sight of the
ocean of slush and mud that lay between her and
the school. The fact that smoke was issuing from
the ehimney did not make them very hopeful,
though. The first boy was about to walk in when
he suddenly sprang backwards with an exclama-
tion of something like terror, and a dire whisper
of “ Inspector ! ” They executed a sudden move-
ment to the back of the wood-heap and held a
council of war. Bill said he didn’t b’leeve there
was any inspector about ’cos there was no sign of
his buggy. Mary said he wasn’t there ’cos it Was
only three months since ’zamination. The first
boy said it must be him ’cos no other man would
be sitti.n’ in front of the fire as though the place
belonged ter him. He then described the in-
spector as a little man in brown knickerbockers.
That settled his yarn, for the district inspector
was a long, thin man with a bald head and dense
crop of whiskers. It began to rain again, and the
children gathered up their courage in both hands
and marched into the school. There was a little
man in brown knickerbocker suit sitting in front
of the fire and he turned round and greeted them
with a pleasant smile. Whereupon the children
gasped, for it was the lady teacher in “rational ”
costume. That night the parents listened, dumb
with astonishment, to the news that Miss Smith
was wearing boy’s clothes. The rumor spread
like wildfire and a committee meeting was hastily
summoned to deal with the matter. At the
meeting committeeman M'Kenzie, who attended
in kilts, said “it Was nae decent o’ tha
lassie tae gang aboot in such on - Christian
style o’ dress.” “ What about yerself?’” sneered
a cynical Southerner pointing to M'Kenzib’s huge
; hairy leg which Sandy, had placed on the table
while lie was speaking. M'Kenzie took it the
wrong way. He thought his “ freen’ ” was under
the impression that the lady was wearing kilts,
and he threw oil on the smouldering fire by ex-
plaining that “ tha’lassie ’’ was not showing her
knees in kilts—good Skawtch kilts—but going
about wi’ them covered right up in “ eenfernal
breeches—short clothes like the callants wore.”
After much argument and angry discussion, the
skirt-party won by four votes, and it was decided
to inform Miss Smith by letter that she must be
decently attired in future. The committeman
who acted as secretary was a progressive, and he
refused to write to the lady on the subject ; he re-
fused to dictate to any woman what she should
wear as long as she was decently and sensibly clad
and caused no scandal. Also, he refused to lend
his fountain-pen to M'Kenzie (in kilts) to write
the letter with. M'Kenzie declared he would
make him lend the pen, and it started. ‘ It was an‘
awful battle, but stodgy, hide-bound conservatism
triumphed by sheer force of numbers, and carried
the night. Since then the teacher boards with a
progressive family, and in wet weather the school
is closed, as the lady has to wear a skirt over her
bloomers, and skirts are so awkward going through
the bush in wet weather.

How names eventuate. A “ Scawtch ”
friend of mine (writes a Bulletin correspondent)
went home from Borneo and married, and shortly
after his return the wife presented him with twin
girls. “Call them Jean and Flora,” said Jock,
but Flora the elder demurred, and wanted ’em
christened with “ real native names.” “All
right; call ’em Miriam and Leila,” said Jock,
jock-ularly, and went away up-country, Mean-
time, an itinerant sky-pilot happened along, and
the twins were “called” accordingly, but when,
on Jock’s return, he was told of this, he broke out:
“ Flora, Flora ! ye dinna ken what ye hae doon !
The bairnies are ca’ed ‘ biggun ’ and ‘ littlegun ”
and thefe's nae way oot o’ it ! ”

Neighbor Wolff and Others.
Almost every afternoon in pleasant weather I see
my neighbor Wolff sitting in an easy-chair in the
shade in front of his own door. Sometimes he
kills time by reading the paper, but oftener he
does nothing but watch the people and the vehicles
that pass. I frequently stop and talk with him a
few minutes, and he appears to be grateful for the
trifling attention. He is a man of about fifty,
strongly and sturdily built, and to look at him you
see no reason why he should not be up and about
as actively as the best of us. He is intelligent and
well-informed, has travelled considerably, and is a
man in every way worth knowing. His ideas on
finance and business seem to be clear and practi-
cal, and it strikes a stranger as odd he does not
grapple with his opportunities and pile up a big
fortune. Yet he never makes a motion in that
direction. He lets the chances all slip by, and
just sits there as though the world were no more
to him than the Klondyke goldfields are to the
resident population of a cemetery. Yet my neigh-
bour Wolff knows what money is, and appreciates
what can be done with it. Then why doesn’t he
“get a move on” as the Yankees say, and make
hay while the sun shines ? Take a peep at him
about nine o’clock at night, when he takes his cus-
tomary exercise on the pavement —the way being
mostly clear of pedestrians by then—and you will
need no verbal answer to the question. Stocky and
strong as he appears when in his chair, he makes a
poor show on his legs. Slowly, heavily, leaning
hard on his thick stick, he goes to and fro a few
times between the corner and his house—a dis-
tance of 200 feet may be—and then with a sigh
sinks again into his chair like a man who realises
he is out of the race.' A paralytic stroke two
years ago, and the constant fear of another —that
explains it. He is one of the multitude who are
in the world and not of it— as this woman was a
while since:—
“Erom my girlhood,” she says, “I way always
oppressed and hindered by weakness; I was
habitually tired, weary, and languid. Like all
such persons, I lived a kind of life to myself. All
pleasures or pursuits which called for energy and
vitality were closed to me. I had a feeble and
fitful appetite, and what I ate gave me great pain
at the chest and back, and a sense of coldness,
deadness, and weight at the stomach. At times
there would be also a gnawing, sinking feeling.
“ As I grew older I was much troubled with
palpitation of the heart and loss of sleep. Year
after year I dragged along in this miserable way,
and, being too weak to walk, was drawn from
place to place in a bath chair. Every effort was
made to obtain a cure. I consulted doctor after
doctor, and took many kinds of medicine and
treatment, but got no more than temporary relief.
One doctor said my heart was diseased.
“My sister urged me to try Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. Without any great hopes of its effect
in my case, I nevertheless procured a bottle
from the Stores in High-street, and began to
take it as directed. The result was a decidedly
improved appetite, and I relished and digested
what I ate without any pain or distress following.
As I continued to use this medicine the heart
trouble was less frequent and finally ceased alto-
gether. My nerves soon became stronger, and I
could sleep well. In a few weeks more I was
belter and stronger than ever before in my life , and
have enjoyed good health ever since. My friends
were almost as much surprised and pleased as I
was at this remarkable result of taking Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and you may make my statement
public if you think it will do good.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) Mary Ann Welch, 1 Constitution Hill,
Luton, Chatham, February 6, 1895.
My neighbor Wolff may or may not recover
from his paralysis, but all cases like Mrs. Welch’s
—indigestion, with consequent weakness and ner-
vous debility—should be curable by the remedy
which cured her. It opens and renews the diges-
tion, which is the fountain of life, and puts a
wholly new aspect on the world around us. From
having been laid aside as cumberers of the ground,
we become once more workers with others and
sharers of their pleasures.

“Clean Out of Tone.
CLEMENTS TONIC,
The Final Resource,
PROVES
Thoroughly Effective.
The Case of a
Distinguished Journalist.
(BY OCR SPECIAL REPORTER.)
** It is a splendid remedy—none to equal
it,” were the words that greeted our
reporter’s ears, as he was quietly taking his
mid-day meal in the Caf6 Esclienhagen, in
Brisbane. “Yes,” continued the voice at
the next table, “he was just a mere shadow
when I left him in Melbourne, and I
thought him a goner sure, and I was never
more surprised in my life when, a month or
so afterwards, I again met him in Sydney.
He then presented as fine a figure of an
athletic, well set-up man as you would find
in the City.”
Shortly afterwards the pressman, who
heard the above conversation, knocked at
the door of The Pines, Sexton Street.
Brisbane. On presenting a newspaper
man’s magic card, he obtained immediat ■
admission to the private study of Mr.
Graham Wilson. That busy journalist am :
litterateur was immersed in work.
“With your consent I should like to
interview you at once,” began our reporter,
taking out his note-book.
“That altogether depends upon win l
subject,” answered Mr. Wilson.
“I heard you had been seriously ill, and—
“Oh, if that’s the subject of your
questions I’m sure I shall be only too glad
to answer them. In fact, I am alwaA s
talking about my illness, or, rather, abo ‘
my wonderful recovery. ”
You are pretty well known as
journalist ? ” queried our reporter.
“Fairly well known,” said Mr. Wilsoi .
leaning back comfortably in his arm ahaii.
‘ln Sydney I have been on the Sydney
Morning Herald , Daily Telegraph , Sunday
Times, Truth, Evening News, and the Town
and Country Journal, and in Melbourne on
The World, and the Federal Australian.
besides editing The Singleton Argus, 2'he.
South Qippsland Chronicle, The Bendigo
Evening News, The NarrabriAgt, TheMolornj
Express, and The Mudgee Independent—a
pretty fair list, I opine. ”
“ I should think so—a record list,” replied
our reporter. “.And now, Mr. Wilson, for
some particulars of your ill health.”
“I first of all went clean out of tone.
That was when I was in Melbourne.
Of course I know why—it was the strain
of my Parliamentary and other work
and engagements, involving too much
night work. All these made me weak,
and predisposed me to the attack of
pleurisy with effusion, which swept upon
me like an avalanche. You know if you
are out of tone, and your nerves all wrong,
you are merely inviting disease, and you’ll
generally find some malady will accept the
invitation. If when I first began to go
wrong I had taken some proper remedy,
my system would, perhaps, have been able
to resist that terribly weakening pleurisy.
In the end I was laid up, and for a time
was in considerable danger. An operation
was performed. My life trembled in the
balance. However, after a long illness I
seemed to have passed the worst stage of
the complaint, and the pleurisy gradually
disappeared. But, unfortunately, that
disease left behind something worse than
itself—a most distressing weakness. Every
morning I found myself with a dull, aching
head, and it required a most strenuous
effort on my part to crawl out of bed, and
to dress. My breakfast I never could
touch. To sit down to this meal was a
mere farce. Of course I would try to eat,
but after a mouthful or two I was forced to
put down my knife and fork, and give the
matter up as a bad job. In the morning,
and indeed during the whole of the day,
there was a nauseous and bitter taste in my
mouth, which nothing would rid me of.
The consequence was that even when sheer
starvation had made me slightly hungry,
I could not enjoy my food, for it all seemed
to have the same bitter flavour. It was
as much as I could do to walk ; I never had
such a sensation before. In fact, I was
always tired. My limbs ached, my back
ached, my head ached, and my eye balls
ached, till I felt like one great ache all
over. At last I made a startling discovery.”
“Yes,” exclaimed the reporter, deeply
interested.
“I found out,” continued Mr. Wilson,
“ that instead of improving I was gradually
losing flesh every day. This I ascertained
by weighing myself. Like any other man
under the same circumstances 1 became
frightened. I immediately changed my
physician.”
“You did employ doctors at this time 1”
interjeoted the other.
“Oh, yes, of course, many doctors.
Well, as I was saying, I sent for another
doctor at once—a great expert too.”
“ What did he say ? ”
“He said,” replied Mr. Wilson, “that
my whole system was out of order, nerves
and all, and that only patience and great
care and the best nourishment could pull me
through. And it was quite true. I had
indeed fallen into a shockingly nervous state.
Sleep had forsaken me, and I began to think
I should never close my eyelids again in
healthy slumber. If you had entered my
bedroom at any hour at night at this period
of my illness, you would have seen my
restless and never ceasing turning in bed,
trying in vain to get to sleep. I was
slowly wasting away day by day. Not the
attentions and treatment of all my medical
uen could arrest my decline, nor could all
their medicines, nor all the other advertised
cure-alls that I foolishly used, do me one
atom of good. A deep despondency seiw»'
me. Then something happened.”
“ And that was ? ” asked our reports.
“The advent of a bottle of Clements
i onic into my bedroom. How it got there
L was at the time too ill to even ask, but
[ only remember my attendants gave me a
few doses. I afterwards heard they looked
upon it as a final resource. Even after this
small quantity of the remedy I instinctively
felt I was improving. But the first tangible
signs of improvement appeared in a day or
so after this. I found that I was more
■heerful in the day time, and slept at night.
Then, in another day or two, I began to eat
again ; just a lreile, it is true, but that little
with appetite, and my food did me good, for
it now caused me uo pain or inconvenience
whatever. After this my recovery was a
steady progression. I never failed to take
my dose of Clements Tonic at the proper
hour. Talk about eating 1 I can tell you it
did make me eat; but then,” said Mr.
Wilson, with a smile, “ 1 had a lot of lost
ground to make up. In two months, to
cut the story short, I was as well, if not
better, than I had been for years, and
stouter and heavier than I had ever been
before during my whole life.”
“This will appear in our paper, Mr.
Wilson.”
“Of course, I know that; you seem to
forget I have interviewed many people
myself, and you may be sure I shouldn’t
have granted this interview just to gratify
one man’s curiosity,” returned Mr. Wilson,
with some show of irritation at the
reporter’s interposition.
“ The fact is,” he continued, “ I should
like more of the public to know about the
value of Clements Tonic, and in doing this
I feel I am to some extent a public bene-
factor. Of course, as a newspaper man, I
have had a wide experience of a great many
so called remedies, and their various tricks
and devices, and I know most of them to be
utterly worthless and mere catch-penny
advertising dodges, and you may be sure if
I didn’t know Clements Tonic to be a first-
class therapeutic preparation of proved
value, as well amongst the general public
as amongst large numbers of the leading
medical men of Australia, I wouldn t for
one moment allow my name to be connected
with that remedy. I may tell you that 1
consulted a doctor, a friend of mine, con-
cerning my recovery, and he told me ui
confidence that he was not at all surprised,
and that he had known Clements Tome
work quite marvellous cures in other cases
quite as far gone as mine.”

COUNTRY TRIAL ORDER RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED
“Fact”: Popular priest up North lately
blessed two R.O. pugs before they stepped into the
ring to box, and, when the local man went under, his
reverence exclaimed, with a big sigh : “ Ah well, glory be
to God-it’s satisfactory to know that twas a good
Roman who bate I
“ Crank ” : Two boxers were lately matched for
£2O a-side. During the fight, the chief second of one kept
-plying his man with whisky every time he sat down-
literally pouring it into him. About a dozen rounds were
fought, and the whisky-primr 1 pug., though he had got
through one bottle and nearly finished another, won.
*< My word,” said the winner’s second, “ how that cove
can stand the whisky—he’s a wonder 1 ” Turns out now
that the hall-proprietor put up loser’s stake (on the quiet),
and recommended the winner’s second. Methylated
spirit ought to be
Southern devotees of pugdom include an
elderly dame, an old-established high-priestess of Venus.
Never a fight passes without her taking her place among
the five-shilling contingent, wearing full male oostume,
and making a very convincing and not at all attractive
man of short stature. During any willing contest the
lady’s face is a study. Each hit sees her jaws gnp, her
teeth shine through her parted lips, and her shoulde s
jerk forward convulsively. Which recalls the fact that
a well-known Melb. boxing-hall of a few years back had a
gallery lighted during the day by a window opening on a
bar. On nights of action the bar did nothing, and the
whole of the Hebes would cluster round the window and
see the game out.
There is a lady owner who graces unregistered
meetings quaintly garbed In a cross between male and
female dress. She always dons a man s hat and coat, silk
handkerchief knotted loosely round her neck, and it is
rumored that she has appeared booted, breeched, and
spurred. You could sweep up the language she uses to
her jock—in faot, she is “ one on ’em.’ She helps to rub-
down her nags, leads them on to the course, and, if they
plav up, she deals it out to them with an extremely heavy
and dirty right. She drives to and from the course in a
racing jinker, one leg on each side of the horse—ahvays a
good beast—wields a vigorous whip, and is boss of the
road.” ,
One peculiarity about N.S.W. pug. Mick
Doolev was that, after a series of unparalleled de-
feats,' he could find backers and barrackers. In
the early eighties Dooley proved himself, in old
raw-meat style, a glutton for punishment. But in
crowded gas-lit halls he has always exhibited a ner-
vousness like that of stage novices. Several of Dooley s
defeats were more creditable tban many champions
victories. _ *
A successful Melb. pug., reckoned a champion
in his class, had an experience the other day which will
impress upon him the necessity of carrying with him a
ring, a referee, three seconds, a gong, and a 10-sec.
check. He forgot these, and taking a seat on a 8.
Melb. tram, airily instructed the conductor to go to the
devil when the fare was demanded. A threat that he
would be put off the car impelled the bruiser to remark
that ten of the conductor’s sort weren't equal to the job ;
when suddenly there was a wild whirl, and he was well
and truly punched seven times in quick succession. In
vain he appealed to the sky for fouls during clinches. The
conductor didn’t bother about Queensberry rules. He
simply slogged the pug. till all was blue, and when his
arms tired, he handed him over to the nearest policeman.
Next day the injured stousher was fined a fiver for
“ language,” and the court expressed its sorrow that the
untimely arrival of a constable had prevented the
talented young bruiseiGrom being killed.
Constable Waldron, the deceased Hamson of
Vic Poiice Force is to have his various record perform-
ances set forth in a two-shilling book, published under
the patronage of Chief Com. Ohomley. This memoir of
Waldron’s mighty deeds will be for the benefit of his
widow, and all patriotic Australians who recognise the
fact that the champion policeman was quite as great a
national circumstance as Clem. Hill or Billowed, are
exhorted to send 2s. subscriptions for the book to Mr.
Marzorini, 8 King William-street-, Fitzroy (Melb.).

At Menzies (W.A.) a young fellow was biking
home on a recent night, carrying a shin of beef ; colliding
with a fence he was knocked insensible. Two citizens,
coming along present!}' in the pitchy darkness, walked
into the bike, and after groping about a bit one of them
struck the beef and instantly dropped it with, “Good
God ! the man is cut in pieces! Here’s his leg ! ”

The following advt. by a Melb. pony race-meet-
ing proprietor throws lurid light on the condition of the
alleged sport these times : —-“ Notice to Genuine Owners.
Mr. J. D. Spry intends to run his course strictly indepen-
dent of any pony association. All present disqualifica-
tions of any club removed, and. any further disqualifica-
tions of other clubs not recognised.”
* * * *
Public interest in Melbourne has been greatly
stimulated by thrilling details lately published of
the great jam-tart competition between two boys,
when the winner ate 32 against his rival’s 25 in 32)4
minutes, and carried off the silver belt and medal. Since
the prominence given to this contest, arrangements are
in contemplation for a meeting on a large scale, where
prizes will be offered for the largest consumer of soup,
meat, and vegetables. A leading member of society has
been invited to preside, and competitors may be assured
that everything will be done on the square not as was
done in a notable case up-country. Sporting men will
remember the event, when three competitors devoured
a snake, a dead cat, and a paper pie, but, after per-
forming these athletic feats, found that the promoter had.
bolted with the gate-money, and that they were held
individually and collectively responsible for the hire of
the hall.
• * * •
The recent revelation of preserved-fruit poison-
ing at the Inns of Court Hotel, London, suggests a
humorous fate that might have befallen the Australian
Eleven. It happened that the Australians were away from
their hotel when the tinned fruit came along,
and inasmuch as twenty visitors were seized with
an injured feeling, and two of them actually died,
the cause of the trouble was promptly traced.
Now, supposing the Australians had eaten just
enough of that fruit to put them slightly out of sorts for
an indefinite period, and make their loss of “ form seem
quite natural, we should have had the ’Orae papers
rejoicing in the thought that “ English cricket has at last
re-asserted itself,” etc., whilst the despondent wiseacres
at this end were writing columns of nonsense to the effect
that the “overworked ” team had “ gone stale all round.
* *A * *
It is astounding that even the most biassed
cricket scribe can speak one minute of the “ terrible
strain ” imposed upon the Australians (every one of
whom has been rested during the season), and next
minute mention that Ranji and others have scored more
runs and the principal English bowlers nave bowled more
balls than any member of the Australian team. Figures
show that the visitors get thrpugh less-w6rk in a season
than the men who play for All-England, as The Bulletin
has many a time pointed out.

bigger total to has,credit.- . ' - . .’i

Dear Bulletin. —According to a recent
Tit-Bits—
There is in Australia an hotel where rheumatic patients
congregate. Whenever a whale has been taken, tne
patients are conveyed to the works in which the animal
is out up, the whalers dig a narrow grave >n the body,
and in this the patient lies for two hours, the blubber
acting as a huge poultice. This is known as the whale
cure for rheumatism.
Now, I am a “ bit of one” myself, but——.
—Munchausen 11. [Mostly solid fact, neverthe-
less. —Ed. B.]
At South Melbourne (Vic.) police-court
a “ very ladylike woman ” was lately fined 2s. 6d.
for hanging on to a public-house door with both
hands and calling to her husband— who was wast-
ing his substance in the beer-pewter that biteth
like a serpent and stingeth like an adder to
“ come home.” She pleaded that the family was
reduced to great straits the house-father
painting his nose with the price of their daily
bread, and that she was justified in imploring him
to return to the bosom of his family. The Bench
which fined her was described as “ sympathetic,
too. The husband wasn’t fined, yet a man who
leads to such painful scenes by letting his wife
know where he gets his beer should be severely
punished.

dealer offers. _ Anyone who says he has “something just as good,” or “the
same except in name,” is dishonest, and does not deserve your trade
Here is the result of two cases in which the real Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
were used.

Mr. Thomas is the manager of a leading general store in Queanbeyan, and a reporter, calling upon him
recently, found that he had been completely cured of consumption and serious lung affections of long standing

skill and attention that was possible, but notwithstanding his treatment the patient did not appear to improve,
tiis medico described the ailment as consumption, and Mr. Thomas was naturally in despair. He was troubled
with a persistent dry cough, shortness of breath, pains in the chest and sometimes in the shoulders. He had also
a tendency to feverish attacks, and was naturally greatly debilitated by his ailment. In fact, his loss of flesh
and strength was noticeable to all his acquaintances. Other symptoms later on asserted themselves, and
Mr. Thomas would wake up at night literally g vsping for breath. Mr. Thomas’ physician also told him that
P?« 89n ce of asthma, and this naturally made his patient more concerned than ever. The respon

a gentleman who had himself been cured by them. In September, ’97, he commenced taking them,

RHEUMATISM AND SCIATICA VANQUISHED.
(Blayney Advocate and Carcoar Herald.)
A marvellous cure of rheumatism and sciatica having been reported in the town, a reporter from the
Blayney Advocate and Carcoar Herald was despatched to obtain some details of ,the illness and cure of Mrs
Layland, wife of Mr. John Layland, a Well-kuown and highly-respected resident of Blayney, N.S. W., who is
the engineer of the railway pump-house. Mrs. Layland readily consented to give the required information,
md spoke with great enthusiasm in praise of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. “For fourteen years,” said she, “I
suffered excruciating pains from rheumatism in the arm—so that I could not raise my hand to my head. It
affected my arms from the shoulders to the finger-euds;
my hands were swelled up, and I couldn’t move my fingers.
I also had sciatica in the left, leg and couldn’t walk about.
The illness affected me more in the winter time. A doctor was
attending me all the while, and although he was very attentive to
me, his medicines only gave me temporary relief. I also tried
Eucalyptus and patent medicines, but got no benefit from any.
I was never entirely free from pain, and I got that way that I
did not care what happened to me. About eighteen months ago,
a lady friend, who used to come and help me to do my housework,
recommended me to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. At first, I
refused to give them a trial, as I had spent such a lot of money
in taking medicines without deriving any benefit. But my friend
persuaded me to take them, and thankful I am of it, for after I
had used a few boxes, I was thoroughly cured, and have not
had a pain since. I ean now get about and do my housework,
aud after the fourteen years’ pain which I suffered, the en-
tire cure made me feel like a new woman altogether. The
cure must be permanent, as I went through all last winter
without any pains whatever. I have recommended the pills to a
large number of my friends, and can advise anyone suffering as i
I did, to take them.”

Shadows.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beside the Narrow Crossing-Plao* i
(And night was falling gray) |
Two Shadows met, the legends tell , , ,
Each Shadow went its way.
And there was anguish in their eyes,
And tears in both their hearts,
Beside the Narrow Crossing-Place,
Where Shade from Shadow parts.
For they had seen the Rising Sun
In opal and in rose,
For they had seen the Rising Sun,
Who saw the long day close.
And had they met at Morn or Noon
Who met the shades between ?
Ay ! had they met at Morn or Noon . . .
And so—the Might-Have-been !
N.S.W.
B. J. BEADT.
“From the Start!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the year of the Big Strike. Mick Sheedy
and Jack Devine had battled from the Diaman-
tina to the “North West Corner” without having
so much as got their hands into wool during the
whole dreary ten weeks.
To be sure there were many “cuts” offering,
but Sheedy and Devine clung to their Union
tickets, though their clothes were worn threadbare
and their horses woefully leg-weary.
Their luck was clearly dead-out, for they never
seemed to strike a union shed until at least a week
after roll-call; and no matter how keenly their
anxious eyes scanned the walls of the far-off wool-
shed as they tiredly “ heeled ” their jaded steeds
across the plain, never had they discerned one
trap door idle —never a gap in the long, irregular
volley of out-popping, snow-white jumbucks to
betoken a vacant stand inside. So at last they
grew too hopeless to even glance at the busy shed
shimmering through the sun-haze, and invariably
made straight for the huts —and the cook.
They had avoided the strike-camps as far as
possible ; they weren’t quite “stiff,” and scorned
to eat Union tucker while they could buy—or
steal—from the squatters, or strike a Union shed
for supper and breakfast, and a shake-down in
the woolshed, or on the long tables in the
shearers’ hut. And both were well enough known
to Queensland strike-leaders to be trusted with a
roving commission.
But even in Union sheds some of the shearers—
“slow tigers” and Viotorian cookies, mostly—
objected to “ feedin* travellers,” and, half in joke,
half in earnest, ask the cook pointed questions
“ before folk. ”
It was just daybreak at Wirraganna. The two
travellers had struck the station late the previous
night, with horses done-up, and Devine ditto with
sickness. The shearers were swarming silently
from their bunks, and flitting hurriedly but stiffly
to and fro the river bank with soap and towel,
their toil-drawn faces showing pale atad haggard
in the ghostly dawn.
“What’ll it run to this year, cook?” asked a
young giant who, pint-pot in hand, stood with
his back to the roaring fire. The speaker had
probably never been ten miles away from the
paternal potato-patch at Bungaree until the pre-
vious shearing ; but he spoke with the assuranoe
of a veteran, and regarded Sheedy and Devine—
who had just come in for coffee and brownie, pre-
paratory to making an early start—with some-
thing like soorn—the scorn of the superior person,
with an assured inoome, for a possibly better
out of a job.
Devine at once turned and walked out, ooffee-
less, cold and sick. (His mates said that Jack
Devine was too thin in the bide for this blanky
country.)
“ A lot of these fellows is lookin’ fer work and
prayin’ ter Gawd they won’t find it. That’s my
blanky opinion, anyway,” continued Bungaree,
insultingly—to nobody in particular.
Sheedy slowly looked the fellow up-and-down,
took up a pannikin, walked to the fireplace, un-
ceremoniously shouldered the young yahoo aside,
dipped out a cup of coffee—and jumped back just
in time to avoid a kick.
“ Come outside ! ” blustered Bungaree.
“All right; but wait till I drink this.”
The hut was in an uproar by this time, but the
lank Queenslander sat on the end of the table,
chewing his “ brownie ” and leisurely sipping his
coffee. When he had finished, he slowly up-ended,
and followed the crowd outside to where a ring
had been formed, and Bungaree stood stripped
and foaming for battle.
Sheedy asked to see the “Rep.” The latter,
who was ringer as well as union representative,
was found busy at the grindstone.
“ Well,” he asked, “ what’s up ?”
“Union business,” replied Sheedy.
“ Hurry up, then ; the bell ’ll ring in a minute.”
“Got to fight this man,” said Sheedy. “But
I don’t want to risk bein’ belted by a scab. I
want to see his tickets. Here’s mine,” and he
handed out a dilapidated pocket-book.
The “Rep.” opened the book and sorted out a
bundle of grimy Union tickets. “ I see,” he said
—“financial from the start. And what’s this !
A loyalty ticket ? You’re all right, mate. But”—
“I’m no cadger, that’s all,” replied Sheedy ;
“ and I want to see this fellow’s paper for satis-
faction.” ,
The shearers, who had crowded round to see the
fun, grew interested.
“That’s fair,” said one elderly Monaro man.
And the crowd followed the lead. “ Show yer
paper, Bungaree,” they cried.
Bungaree unwillingly handed two Union tickets
to the “Rep.” “This year’s and last,” he ex-
plained. “ I only learnt last year. ”
“ Where’d last year’s ticket come from ? ” asked
Sheedy. The “Rep.” spelled out the signature
of the agent.
“What shed?”
“Dunno,” replied the “Rep.” “Deniliquin
office, though. What shed, Bungaree?”
“ What’s that to do with you?” growled Bun-
garee, savagely. “ I’m a Union man ; ain’t that
enough ?”
An expressive nod passed round the grinning
circle.
“So you learnt in a scab shed ? ” said Sheedy.
“ I thought so ! But as you’re a Union man now,
T s’pose I’ll have to put ’em up. Come on 1 ”
ak ßU j ga J 6 j “, came on” with a vengeance, but
Sheedy dodged and fell. ’
“ Stand up to him, man!” urged the Monaro
man who was seconding Sheedy in lieu of the
Union 106 ' elt anx^ous for the honor of the
-A-g&in Bungaree rushed, and again Sheedy went
down without a blow. There was another growl
of disapproval from the onlookers, and, as the
overseer was seen walking across from the “ bar-
racks to the woolshed, a few left the battlefield
considering such a tame exhibition poor compen-
sation for the loss of a sheep in the day’s tally.
-*7 U , P and finish ifc! ” exclaimed the
a half-start and an anxious look in
the direction of the shed.
Devine, who stood by, pale and ill, turned,
looked j; at the Monaro man, and winked. The
Kep. saw the wink, dropped his shears again,
and waited. ’
, Dungaree again rushed, and again Sheedy
dodged, but this time, instead of falling, he took
Bungaree low down and threw him heavily,
ihere was a yell of approval, and thirty hats went
flying into the air.
.When the fallen man was helped up it was seen
tnat the fight was over; his right wrist was badly
sprained.
_ “ Sta y to breakfast,” called the “Rep.” to
Sheedy, as the shearers hurried away in response
to the shed-bell, leaving the travellers and the
damaged Bungaree to make friends as best they
might. ■ J
Bungaree 6 °°° ked my shearin £> anyway,” said
Book here, said Sheedy. “ You’re an ignor-
ant pup; but you’ll grow out of that in time.
My mate s sick; prads knocked-up. I want a
week s cut. Go and see the boss and get him to
give me your stand. You can have it again when
your hand’s better.’’
Bungaree considered, saw that the proposal was
better than the probability of losing his stand for
good, and interviewed the boss as requested.
“ a dead man’s place,” explained
the. Rep.” to the others at breakfast-time, “for
Bungaree made it right with the boss.”
So Sheedy got a Union cut after all.
In ten days’ time Devine was better, the horses
were fit to travel, and Bungaree returned to his
place on the board.
The boss hinted that he could make room for
Sheedy and his mate by “shooting” two less
competent hands.
“We’re Union-men from the start,” said
Sheedy; and the overseer said no more. Nor
was he much surprised that Sheedy refused to
accept payment for the thousand sheep he had ‘
shorn. “ Pay my mate’s tuclcer-bill out of it,” he
said ; “ and pay the station for our horses’ grass,
if you like. The rest
is Bungaree’s. So
long, boss.”
And th e mates
again battled out
across the whitening
plains in search of a
“Union cut.”

A Case of Snake-bite.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is the Rev. Samuel Stipper’s Sabbath custom
to take a walk in the early morning, and on these
occasions it is my delight to accompany him a
few miles along the bush-road outside the town-
ship, returning with him in time for service.
On a recent Sunday the weather was particu-
larly fine, and we stepped out well, the Reverend
in high spirits, whistling softly as he went, and
switching off the heads of thistles and dandelions
along the track. As ho struck once at a thistle,
the cane flew from his hand, and he was stooping
to pick it up when suddenly he uttered a cry and
started back. A was wriggling away
through the dust. I darted after it and killed it
with my stick, but when I looked towards Mr.
Stipper I was horrified to see him leaning against
the fence, his face ashy pale and his right hand
held out before him.
No need to ask what was the matter. I Caught
sight of a drop of blood on the baok of his hand;
and, clutching his arm, I half-carried him to a
house, fortunately but a couple of hundred yards
away.
It was a bush inn, and I laid the sufferer on a
sofa in the parlor, where half-a-dozen men were
drinking.
“ Whisky,” cried several when I explained :
“ it’s the only thing, sir.” The landlord brought a
bottle, and filled a medium glass with the stuff,
neat.
“ Must I take whisky,” feebly said the patient,
who was a teetotaller. “Is there nothing else? ”
“Nothing,” said the landlord in an inexorable
tone, amid corroborative murmurs. “ It’s the only
thing, sir ; I’ve used it on myself.”
Tears flowed down my reverend friend’s cheeks
as he gulped it; then the landlord filled the glass
again. “ Down with it! ”he said—“ it’s your only
chance of life.”
The face of the afflicted one blazed-up red as the
“ liquid sunshine ” coursed through his chanriels,
and anon he brightened somewhat and sat-up.
“Have another, sir,” said the man with the
bottle. The Reverend raised his hand deprecat-
ingly, but the dose was poured out, and he drank
it almost at a gulp.
The patient held out his hand, and, after brief
examination, the “ bite ” was unanimously pro-
nounced a thistle-scratch 1 The Rev. Samuel had
had three stiff jorums on false pretences, as it were •
and, as it was evidently time for us to go I paid-
up and led my friend away. I was apprehensive
of results, but apparently not so the Reverend
He resumed his whistling, and walked jauntily’
if somewhat unsteadily, along.
“ You had better get some one else to preach
this morning,” I remarked tentatively, eying him.
“Pooh! not I!” he said, beaming. “Going
to preach myself—never felt better in my life ' ”
He seemed all right mentally ; and, supposing
the giddiness would pass off, I let things go, and
we arrived at the church.
The Reverend gravely mounted to his place, and
his voice was rich and full as he gave out the first
hymn ; he even stood up and joined in it himself.
j.hen he prayed, and the people said afterwards
that they had never heard such an impressive and
eloquent petition. But, while the choir was after-
wards singing an anthem, I was horrified to see
that the Reverend was smiling beatifically at
things in general, and noisily beating time with a
hymn-book. His face was fiery red, and he
swayed about perceptibly when he rose to preach.
“Shubjeck of my addresh t’day, my friepsh,”
he said, “ ish—Temperansh—Hvilsh of Shtrong
Drin—Drink ! Itsh cursh, in’ brethren !” he went
on, beaming all round and leaning heavily on the
cushion. “Alwaysh excep’—wh—(hie)—whisky !
Whiskysh goo—good ! Speshally f’r shnake—(hie)
—bitesh ! Yes, my frensh, when whishky bitesh
you, alwaysh take plenty—(hie)—snaksh !”
His head dropped on the desk, his knees bent
beneath him, and he disappeared from our view
with a hollow thump.
I judged it was about time for an explanation,
so I arose and gave one in a few words ; but, when
several of us ascended the pulpit, the Reverend
Samuel had recovered sufficiently to challenge us
to mortal combat.
“Think, because I’m parshon, I can’t fight!
I’ll —hie—show you ! ”
And then, as we led him gently away, he shed .
tears and hiccups on our necks. But at last we
got him to bed.
C.N.
